THE RED PAGE.
THE FINER SPIRIT.
I am the strength of all the dead,
The wisdom and the goodlihead
And the heart of what they did and said.
I am the beauty that hath stood
Bodied, like a beatitude,
In soft, calamitous womanhood
From the beginning. And the Rest
Of saints am I, and all the blest
Rapture of bosoms babes have prest.
T. W. H. Croslcml.
THE INQUEST.
Not labour kills us ; no, nor joy t
The incredulity and frown,
The interference and annoy,
The small attritions wear us down.
The little gnat-like buzzings shrill,
The hurdy-gurdies of the street,
The common curses of the will—
These wrap the cerements round our feet.
And more than all, the look askance
Of loving souls that cannot gauge
The numbing touch of circumstance^
The heavy toll of heritage.
It is not Death, but Life that slays:
The night less mountainously lies
Upon our lids than foolish day’s
Importunate futilities.
F. B. Money-Coutta.
A TAUNT.
In the oldest wood I know a brooklet
That bubbles over stones and roots.
And ripples out of hollow places,
Like music out of flutes.
There creeps the pungent breath of cedars,
Rich coolness wraps the air about,
Whilst through clear pools electric flashes
Betray the watchful trout.
I know where wild things lurk and linger,
In groves as gray and grand as Time ;
I know where God has Written poems
Too strong for words or rhyme.
Gome, let us go, each pulse is precious,
Coine, ere the day has lost its dawn ;
And you shall quaff life’s finest essence
From primal flagons drawn !
Just for a day slip off the tether
Of hothouse wants, and dare to be
A child of Nature, strong and simple,
Out in the woods with me.
Out in the woods, on freedom’s bosom,
We shall be worthy sons of men,
Bred of remotest sires who bearded
The satyr in his den.
Come, just a sip of the wild man’s nectar
Shall show you life from a point of view
As old as the oldest stones of the mountains,
And yet as fresh as dew !
Supple joints and bulging muscles,
Sinews taut as the chords of a harp,
Veins full-flushed, eyes clear as water,
And all the senses sharp.
Who was Shakespeare ? Where is 11 nner?
Can Milton leap, or dance, or run ?
Should you care to cast a fly with Walton ?
Do you envy Napoleon ?
What of this lore of buried thinkers?
What of these classic deaths and heights ?
Better one strong, bright, living creature
Than a myriad trilobites !
Ah, I see you scoff at my meaning,
You flaccid, indolent bookworm, you !
What would you give for my good digestion
And my nerve-cords sound and true ?
Maurice Thompson.
The Heal Australian.
THE interest of “The Native Tribes
of Central Australia,” by Baldwin
Spencer and F. J. Gillen (Lond.,
Macmillan, 1899) would in any case
be more scientific than literary. But the
book could with great advantage have
been made much more literary than it
is. It is a study of the aboriginal tribes
inhabiting Central Australia—their ways of
life, customs, ceremonies, and superstitions ;
and the authors—one a professor of biology,
the other a S.A. magistrate—have worked
hai'd in the light of special experience, and
have amassed a wealth of detail-information,
much of it new, most of it not previously set-
down so accurately. But their book rambles
too much: the information is often not
clearly presented : the matter wants a more
minute and more systematic classification—
wants re-arranging by a stronger brain, like
the brain which works to make books such
as Geddes and Thomson’s “Evolution of
Sex marvels of lucid force. Spencer and
Gillen s book naturally invites comparison
with Dr. Roth’s recent and similar study of
Queensland tribes, and the comparison is often
in favor of Dr. Roth. Where S. and G. are
loose he is tense ; where they fail to classify,
he gives head and sub-head, section and. sub-
section. Dr. Roth’s method is a great help
to the reader, who has his neck, as it were,
severed by one blow instead of mangled by
half-a-dozen; and anybody who has been
decapitated both ways will cheer for the
single-stroke executioner.
* * * *
In the second place, Spencer and Gillen’s
rambling book is a slow book-—almost tedious.
Ihe authors not only write commonplace
English, but they often write withoutanysense
of the dramatic and picturesque value of the
facts they describe, or without any power of
dramatically and picturesquely presenting
their facts in words. That is a great draw-
back, even from the scientific point of view
For the man of science, like every other
reader, wants his mental food chewed and
salivated, pepsined and diastased, before he
swallows it. He wants digestion made as
short and easy a process as possible ; for
he has no time to waste. Thus an author
who sees so clearly, and writes so forcibly,
that his subject, and his statements, and the
resultant analogies and deductions, can be
assimilated with the least possible expendi-
ture of energy, is as commendable in science
as in literature. The old notion that science
was entitled to be dull and dryasdust has
gone to limbo : people with brains recognise
with Buckle that style is half the author’s :
battle, and see that if Herbert Spencer would
write thrice as well he would have twice his
present value. Thus it is pity that ail the
time, all the labour, which Spencer and
Gillen have spent in a most curious and in-
teresting environment have furnished
their book with few curious and inter-
esting passages. Ethnologically, of course,
they are full of interest; though even ethno-
logically they too often confine themselves
to a mere record of facts. “ A mere record
of facts ’ —that is good ; but Spencer and
Gillen’s book might so easily have been
better.
• * • *
It is worth while stressing the point of
style a little farther, so that other Australian
authors with a burden to deliver may keep
in mind that the load is scarcely more import-
ant than the vehicle—since it is the vehicle
which carries the load. Let the Spencer-
Gillen volume open where it will pace
320
After midnight there was performed the Qua-
hara Akakia (plum-tree), of a place called Iliakilia
in the Waterhouse Range. This was acted by four
men, who were respectively Purula and honey-ant
totem; Purula and “native-pheasant”; Purula
and white bat ; Bulthara and illonka. First of all
one man came up to where the audience was sitting
by the Parra. He pretended to knock plums
down and to eat them, and after a short time he
sat down amongst the audience. Then two others
came up, one of whom remained standing while
he knocked down imaginary plums, which were
eaten by the other man, who seated himself on the
ground. This over, both of the men went and
joined the audience, and the fourth man came and
went through the same pretence of eating plums.
* * • 4
Now, this matter is dead: there is no
picture: it costs the reader a mental effort
to envisage the scene and drag its meaning
and colour from the words ; and this mental
effort has to be repeated on nearly every page
of the Spencer-Gillen book. But in that night
in the Central Australian desert, and in t'he
voices of the night; in the scene, dark or lit
by camp-fires; in the faces and figures of
actors and audience ; in the atmosphere of
the whole ; —there must have been material
for fresh emotion and graphic description.
If Spencer and Gillen had presented the
scene as they saw it, or as clear, alert minds
might ha ye felt it—if they had made it live :
then the value of their transferred impres-
sion would have increased a hundredfold,
even scientifically. For only life rouses
and begets life ; and from dead words, as
fi’om dead seeds, nothing germinates. What
would a dozen bright journalists of the
Steevens type have made of the plum-tree
ceremony ? Something infinitely more in-
teresting and valuable—even scientifically ;
for they could see and show the looks and
hearts of the participators. But —if Spencer
and Gillen had devoted time and space to
subordinate incidents, their hook would
have run to half-a-dozen volumes ? So
much the better, if the volumes were
good. As it is, they have erected a
warehouse of facts which should be visited
by everyone who wants to understand the
ways and whys of Australian natives. Yet,
with a little literary architecture, their ware-
house might have been a palace—so built
that it would attract visitors who will never
dream of entering the barn.
* » • *
Messrs. Spencer and Gillen have many
facts about totemism in Central Australia
but to the reason or origin of totemism we
come no nearer. They agree with Dr. Roth
that the practice of sub-incision was not
adopted to limit population ; but demur to
his theory of an imitative rite. Their chap-
ter on social organisation and tribal re-
lationships exhausts the subject for their
sphere. They report that the Central
Australian is never in a hurry ; that
he occasionally employs women doctors;
and that his costume when anybody is
initiated into his “lodge” would make a
Freemason split with envy. And Spencer
and Gillen report many other things of
moment: the objection here taken is only to
their literary style, and in some measure to
their system or lack of it : for information
they are an encyclopaedia and an art gallery
and a museum put together. Many photo-
graphs add value to their book, though in
some cases colored pictures are wanted to
convey the full idea. Apparently the Cen-
tral Australian has not the Red Indian’s
objection to the camera. To make up for
one denounced quotation, several com-
mended quotations follow.
* * * *
The Central Australian Secures a Divorce—
and a Shooting Star.
. If a woman, runs away from her husband and he
is unable to recover her, he and his friends—that
is, men of his local group--assemble at a secluded
spot where a man skilled in magic draws upon the
surface of a small patch of ground, which has been
cleared and smoothed down for the purpose, a
rough diagram . . . intended to represent the
figure of the woman lying down on her back.
It is called [curiously] Aura, a term which has
much the same significance as the word emblem.
While the drawing is being made, and throughout
the whole proceedings, low chants are sung, the
burden of which is an exhortation to the Ariing-
quiltha [or evil spirit] to go out and enter her
body and dry up all of her fat. When the draw-
ing is done, a piece of green bark is placed [at a
spot near where the heart of the woman should
be]. This is supposed to represent the spirit
part of the woman, and then all the men
who are present stick into it a number of
miniature spears, which have been made for the
purpose and have been “sung.” The spears with
the bark into which they are fixed are then flung
as far as they can be thrown in the direction in
which the woman is supposed to be. The party
now returns to camp, and sooner or later, very
often after the lapse of a considerable time, the
woman’s fat dries up, she dies, and her ulthana,
or spirit, appears in the sky in the form of a
shooting-star.
* * * *
The Central Australian Covets His Neigh-
bour’s Wife, and Catches Her.
-A man belonging to a group about 45 miles
away, to the west of Alice Springs, persuaded a
woman belonging to a man of the latter group to
run away with him from her husband, and the
latter, though he gave chase, could not capture the
runaway wife. The elopers went away to the
south and lived for a year in a distant group, re-
turning finally to Alice Springs, accompanied by
some of the man’s friends. On arrival at the
latter place the man went to the UTic/uiijci, or
men s camp, and the woman to the Erlukwirra, or
women s camp. At the Ungunya a long discussion
took place, during which the pros and cons of the
case were discussed, the two men most interested
remaining silent. After some time the man
who had taken the woman got up, . and,
taking with him some spears and a‘ shield’
walked out to a clear space some little dis-
tance from the camp and shouted to the ag-
grieved ma v n, who remained sitting, “ Arakutya
thale iknukunja yinga iltai, ” which meant, “I
took your woman ; come and growl ! ” There-
upon the man got up, and, standing some dis-
tance off, threw spears and boomerangs at the first
man, who skilfully guarded himself with his
shield but made no-attempt to retaliate. When
all had been thrown he rushed into close quarters
with his enemy, and began attempting to out the
thighs of the latter, and his back also with a large
stone knife, the attacked man doing his Best to
guard himself, but not again attempting to retali-
ate. After a time the onlookers thought"' that
enough had been done, and calling out loudly
‘\kutta impara ” —which means “Enough, leave
him ! dragged the two apart. The women
meanwhile had all assembled ; and the aggrieved
man, walking over to where his erstwhile wife was
standing, caught hold of her and cut her about
the legs and body, avoiding, however, any vital
part. Then, leaving her, he waved his knife in
the air and started off for the camp, shouting
“ Untantimma atnina, ipminja tcuta, ipminja
kuta” (“You keep together ; I throw away, I
throw away ”). After having renounced her in
this way, she became the property of the man
with whom she had eloped.
* « * *
The Central Australian Goes Kurdaitcha.
Amongst the Central Australian natives there
is no such thing as belief in natural death ; how-
ever old or decrepit a man or woman may be when
this takes place, it is at once supposed that it has
been brought about by the magic influence of some
enemy, and in the normal condition of the tribe
the death of one individual is followed by the
murder of someone else, who is supposed to be
guilty of having caused the death. . . . When it
is decided who is guilty, a council of the old men
of the group to which the dead man belonged is
held, and, if it be decided that vengeance is to be
exacted by means of a Kurdaitcha party, then the
man who is to play this part is chosen. The name
Kurdaitcha is applied to the latter, and he wears
the shoes to which, by white men, the name of
Kurdaitcha shoes has been given. In the'North,
the native name for them is Interlinia, and in the
South Intathurta.
These shoes have the form of a thick pad of emu
feathers matted together with human blood drawn
from the arm of some young man. They are so
ingeniously made, however, that the use of any-
thing like blood in their construction would never
be suspected ; indeed, it is difficult to detect, even
with the shoes in one’s hands, how the feathers
are matted into such a compact mass without ap-
parently the use of anything like stitching. On
the upper surface is a network of human hair
string made from the hair of any living man or
woman—it does not in the least signify who the
individual is—and in the middle of the network is
a hole through which the foot passes and across
which stretches a cord made of several strands of
hair string twisted together. No woman or child
may see them, and, they are kept wrapped up in skin
or else placed for safety in the sacred storehouse
along with the Churinga. It is said that they may
be used more than once, but the nature of the
shoe is such that it could not last more than one
journey over the hard ground characteristic of the
interior.
Before a man may wear the shoes he has to sub-
mit to a most painful ordeal. A stone is heated
to redness, and then applied to the ball of the
small toe of either foot, it does not matter which,
until, as the natives say, the joint is softened’
when, with a sudden jerk, the toe is pulled out-
wards and the joint thus dislocated. ... In cor-
respondence with this is the fact that the true
Kurdaitcha shoe has, at one side, a small opening
made in the hair network through which the toe
is thrust.
Each Kurdaitcha man when going on his errand
is accompanied by a medicine man, and the two
men are rubbed over with charcoal—black being
in the Arunto tribe the color associated ivith
magic—and decorated with bands of white down.
The hair of both men is tied up behind and a
small, conical helmet of twigs is fastened on with
hair-string. The Kurdaitcha has lines of down
passing across the front of the helmet, down the
side of the face and front of the body and legs as
far as the knees. The medicine man has a median
line running from the top of the helmet to the tip
of his nose; another curved line meeting this at
both ends encloses the eye of each side; and on
the body a broad band of charcoal runs across
from shoulder to shoulder and downwards till, at
the level of the sternum, it divides into two, one
passing on either side of the mid line and so on
as far down as the knee. The bands are outlined
with white down, and, as the pattern is a constant
one, the Kurdaitcha man can always be distin-
guished from the medicine man.
Both of the men wear the interlinia or shoes
which, when thus in use, are decorated with lines
of white and pink down, and, while they are being
put on and attached to the feet and legs with
human hair-string, the Kurdaitcha sings
“ Interlinia turlaa attipa,
Interlinia attipa,”
which literally translated- means “ Interlinia to
me hold fast, interlinia hold fast. ” There is not,
either at the making or at the putting on of the
shoes, anything in the way of an incantation be-
yond this simple one.
Followed by the medicine man the Kurdaitcha
takes the lead until the enemy is sighted. Then the
medicine man falls into the rear, while the Kurd-
aitcha stealthily creeps forward towards his quarry
an'd, suddenly rising up, spears him before he is
aware of the presence of an enemy. Both medi-
cine-man and Kurdaitcha have meanwhile put the
sacred Churinga between their teeth, and when
they are thus armed the spear cannot fail to strike
the victim. As soon as this is done the Kur-
daitcha man goes-away to some, little distance
from the fallen man, and from which he cannot
see the operations of the medicineman, who now
approaches and performs his share in the work.
By aid of his magic powers arid by means, of the
Atnongara stones he heals the victim. These At-
nongara stones are small crystalline structures
which every medicine-in an is supposed to be
able to produce at will from his own body,
throughout which it' is believed that they are
distributed—in fact, it is the possession of
these stones which gives to the medicine-
man his virtue. Into the spear-wound he rubs
a white, greasy substance called Ernia, which'he
obtains by pressure of the skin glands on the out-
side of the nostril. After all external traces of the
wound have disappeared he goes quietly away and,
together with the Kurdaitcha man, returns to his
own country. Having been touched by the
Atnongara stones, the victim returns to life,bint is
completely ignorant of all that has taken place.
He returns to camp and in a short time sickens
and dies. His death is-attributed to Kurdaitcha
or to .some other form of magic influence, but no
one will be able to trace the tracks of the Kur-

general recognition of the immense importance of this

with mothers that
FARINACEOUS FOODS
ARE BAD FOR INFANTS,

dearly bought experience, which is paid by the lives of
thousands of little ones.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Westralkn Government has made an
ass of itself as usual. It doubled the rate of com-
mission on P.O. orders to stop the outflow of
money—the small sums which the miner sends to
his wife and children in S.A., or Victoria,
or N.S.W. The Forrest Ministry was convinced
that this would make the miner keep his money
in the country and let the old woman starve, but
as it quite forgot to put a corresponding stamp-
duty on bank-drafts the cash went through the
banks instead, and the P.O. revenue went all to
pieces. And now the Forrest Ministry, having
dropped to this obvious fact when it is too late,
has reduced its rates again in hopes of getting the
lost business back again, and has given up all
hope of stopping the outflow.
Farewell!
Farewell! The memories we share are sweet,
And yet so few.
Thrice only have we chanced to meet,
Just I and you.
Ah, well! farewell! The short, sweet time has
fled;
We say adieu.
The Autumn days, so bright, so soon are dead
For me and you.
Goodbye, but once more hold my hand —
The clasp is true ;
Look once more in my eyes as here we stand,
Just I and you.
Farewell! and now with memories of the past,
And our adieu,
A kiss, and that the very first and last
For me and you.
The first and last! Ah, well, dear lass,
Between us two
Lies Love afraid, and so the dream must pass
For me and you !
A. LEO WATTS .
A Ballade of the Hoad.
When.the drums are beating within the breast.
When the heart is high and the pulse is hale ;
When the tongue is ready with genial jest,
And we leave the town as a dreary gaol,
When we jeer at the jog of the sober snail,
And the load is light that our shoulders bear—
Oh ! the music swells on the morning gale,
And we’d fiddle a smile on the face of Care.
When the skies are dun and the birds at rest,
And the world is loud in a weary wail ;
When the brain is dull and the heart opprest,
And the road before is a dusty trail.
His breast is girt with a hero’s mail,
His soul is ready to do and dare,
Who can lift a tune when his comrades quail
And fiddle a smile on the face ot Care !
When the wind is hot from the drowsy West,
And the junk is hard as a seasoned rail,
When the air is black with the winged pest,
When the water’s scant and the damper’s stale,
It’s meat and drink to the man that’s frail
To turn to a brother who won't despair,
To feel: “ This fellow—he can't say ‘ Fail ’ —
He’d fiddle a smile on the face of Care ! ”
ENVOI.
In a weeping world it hath more avail
Than the grandest hymn or the deepest prayer,
This power to laugh at the craven tale
And fiddle a smile on the face of Care !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Irony.
All night a great wind blew across the land,
Come fresh from wild and salty seas,
With many voices loud and low
Appealing to the sympathies
Of those with whom long, long ago
It had been friends, but who
Had lost the way to know and understand
Its weird and tearless woe.
A sleeper drawn from ancient fancies, stirred,
Breathed strangely in a deep unrest
As though his heart were choked with grief ;
The moon down-stealing in the west
Threw every move of limb and leaf
Upon his blind. Now this
Was he the wind sought wildly—had he heard —
Alas, the friend in need was deaf !
All time a great thought wandered round the
world
Naked and breathing loveliness,
Seeking in alien souls a home
But thwarted always, knocking no less
At every door, yet forced to roam
A wonder unexpressed ;
A sense of strangeness as of wings unfurled
Hovered at times o’er some.
He heard the knocking at the inner door;
He saw her face a light intense
And stood amazed, irresolute.
“ Now, thou who hast the poet-sense
In song serene and absolute
Proclaim my hidden worth.”
He sobbed, she drooped her wings. . . Woe ever-
more !
The chosen mind was mute.

jjankee jjoodle
IN AMERICA.
The £>egend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and fife
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Doodle was heard as never
was heard before or since.

any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.

‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding _ two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, APRIL 15, 1899.
A Plaint from Victoria.
The Insurance and Banking Record (pub-
lished in Melbourne) in its last issue prints
a solemn and low-spirited article about the
want of any notable increase in Victoria’s
population. In the last eight years Victoria,
if the estimates are reasonably reliable,
shows the smallest percentage of increase
among the Seven Provinces. Of course the
estimates, barring those of Maoriland and
Tasmania, are not wholly reliable ; in these
two provinces all arrivals and departures are
by sea, and all sea-passengers are carefully
counted, except stowaways and lunatics who
depart in skiffs, but in the mainland pro-
vinces there is a constant drift of land-
pilgrims back and forward over the borders,
and of them the record is never remarkable
for its accuracy. Still it is probable that
official guesses show some semblance to
the real state of things, and by these esti-
mates Victoria’s population, since the ’9l
census, has shown the smallest advance.
On this subject the Record wails as follows :
It is next to imposible to prescribe means by which
population can be induced to return to Victoria under
conditions affording a tolerable prospect of permanency.
The capital (Melbourne) does not want to add to the large
proportion of its only partially-employed and poorly-paid
inhabitants, for it alread\ T contains very nearly 40 per
cent, of the population of Victoria. Its magnificence and
marvellousness may be conceded, but there is no need to
augment its misery. If, therefore, Melbourne cannot
find employment for a larger population, and if rural
development continues to be slow, what remains to be
done ? Any suggestion that can be offered will be un-
palatable. The public expenditure, and with it taxation,
ought to be reduced in the most drastic fashion to bring
them into harmony with the circumstances of the people,
and to free money for private enterprise. But the civil-
servants, who constitute a formidable proportion of the
electors, are antagonistic to a reduction of expenditure,
while the Protectionist party would strongly object to a
reduction of Customs duties. Going further, it may be
urged that the administration of the Treasury is repelling
to all owners of capital, whether large or small, and that
if capital is to be retained in Victoria reform is necessary.
* • » *
This is the sort of case where all the wild-
eyed people who know exactly how to put
the whole world right with their one nostrum
come in. Therefore, the Single-taxer reckons
that the way to make things boom again in
Victoria is to put all the taxation on land, and
let the money-lender and publican and im-
porter and jerry-builder and banker and
other financial magnates go free. And the
importer is quite sure that lots of calico im-
ported free of duty would make the popula-
tion multiply amazingly. And the promis-
cuous Fat Man assumes that all that is wanted
is the abolition of the income-tax which
annoys him personally ; and the Age holds
that what is required is lots of borrowed
money and new public works. Also, it is
urged in certain quarters of great stomach-
circumference that the minimum wage and
Labor legislation generally are the causes of
the whole trouble, and that with long enough
hours and small enough wages all would be
well. The Bulletin doesn’t know exactly
whether increased population is so great a
blessing as to be worth all this row. Yet
the cause of Victoria’s present situation
seems sufficiently visible to the naked eye,
provided the eye has an unbiassed intel-
ligence behind it.
• * * *
Victoria has been, for the last 30 or 40
years, a phenomenon among the Austra-
lasian States. In proportion to its area its
population is even now twice that of Maori-
land and Tasmania, 3t? times that of New
South Wales, 20 times that of Queensland,
over 30 times that of South Australia, and
about 100 times that of Westralia. As
things go in Australasia it is a frightfully
overcrowded little corner of a huge, empty
land. To make things equal, and leaving
out all considerations of the age of the
various provinces, New South Wales should
have 4,000,000 people, and Tasmania
350,000, and Maoriland 1,500,000, and
Queensland 9,000,000 to place them on
Victoria’s level. Overcrowding is a relative
term, but in a small way Victoria is to the
rest of Australia what Britain is to Aus-
tralia in a larger way —the parent State
which supplies them with population.
Because of the extra density of population,
agricultural land is dearer than anywhere
else in Australasia ; soil that costs £8 an
acre in many parts of Victoria is no better,
and no better situated, than earth that can
be obtained in New South Wales for half
the money, or in South Australia for per-
haps less than half. Therefore there is a
powerful inducement for new agriculturists
with little capital to make for the adjacent
provinces. There is very little State land of
any value left to be sold cheaply in the small
province, while there is still a good deal in
the large ones adjacent, which is another
reason why the new agriculturist with little
money should go to the other side of the
border.
m m m m
By primary industries—agricultural, pas-
toral, mining, dairying, fruit-growing, &c.—
Victoria manages to drag out £lB4 worth of
produce per annum for every square mile of
its surface. Maoriland and Tasmania make
the next best showing. New South Wales
only produces £B2 worth, and South Australia
less than £6 worth. This is another evidence
that Victoria is overcrowded by comparison
with its neighbors. It has only a little more
than half the annual mineral production of
New South Wales, which is a natural dis-
advantage'that is beyond all visible remedy.
And in late years —the years when popu-
lation has made such slow progress—Vic-
toria has lacked the artificial stimulus of
great importations of borrowed money. The
borrowed money expended during recent
years in New South Wales has been sufficient,
on an average, to continuously support
18,000 men and their wives and families ; in
South Australia it has been sufficient to
maintain about 5500 and their families ; in
Victoria only about 1800. And, whatever
the after-consequences may be, there is no
doubt that the country which borrows plenti-
fully and darns the consequences can get
increase of population while the borrowed
money is being expended, just as, other
things being equal, the man who borrows
money and spends it can enjoy more luxury,
while he is spending it, than the man who
doesn’t borrow. And if one province is able,
through its borrowing, to offer a large amount
of profitable or unprofitable work at building
things which are or are not wanted, or at
digging large holes in the ground and filling
them up again, and another province, which
has been doing the same thing, suddenly
leaves off, that floating section of the popu-
lation which has lived on such work is
always liable to leave the latter province and
make for the former. Victoria left off pour-
ing out borrowed money just as Westralia
commenced. Therefore, that part of the
Vic. populace which was neither agricultural,
pastoral, manufacturing, mining, nor com-
mercial—the section which was' created by
the lavish borrowing of the Gillies era, and
which lived on public works mostly
gathered up its swag and lighted out for the
sunset.
* * * *
Therefore, when a certain angry fragment
of the Melbourne press rises up to ask why
Victoria’s population hasn’t increased much
in the last seven or eight years, it asks, in
The Bulletin’s opinion, a very easy conun-
drum. Victoria suddenly left off finding
constant support for about 90,000 or
100,000 people (counting in men, women, and
children) by building railways, &c., with bor-
rowed money, because it found that railway-
building with borrowed money, in a very
small state, couldn’t go on at a furious
pace for ever. If the other provinces had
left off at the same time things would
probably have been about equal and no
exodus would have ensued ; as they didn’t
much of the railway-building part of the
community went west. Despite its small
size Victoria supports a far larger agricultural
and manufacturing population than any—
even the largest and most populous —of its
neighbors, but neither agriculture nor manu-
factures could find immediate employment
for all that extra population which the
railway-building drunk of the 80’s gathered
into Victoria, and which the sobering-up of
the 90's necessarily dispersed again. But
when that same indignant fragment of the
Melbourne press points to the increase of
population in New South Wales and asks
why its own Government doesn’t “ do some-
thing ” to make Victoria hold more people
every year to the same extent, it opens up a
very large and very foolish question.
m * * *
Obviously if Victoria will borrow as much
as New South Wales does, and will find
s imewhere another 220,000 square miles
of land to settle farmers and squatters upon,
and discover mines equal to those of New
South Wales, and get as large a land revenue,
there is no reason why it shouldn’t increase
in the same proportion. If it can’t do that,
its only other chance is to make its small
and, by comparison, grievously overcrowded
territory four times as attractive as the place
across the border. Freetrade won’t produce
the desired effect; the only reasonable
assumption is that a tariff similar to that of
New South Wales would put its manu-
factures, in proportion to population, on the
New South Wales level, and thereby throw
between 5 00 and 8000 people and their
dependents out of work. It can’t be done
by borrowing money and building railways
on a huge scale—no sane individual would
try to put as much railway on 87,000 square
miles as on 310,000. It can’t be done either
by encouraging agricultural immigrants on a
big scale, for the small province hasn’t the
land to settle them on that the larger
ones still possess. For many years
Victoria, partly by luck, largely by
amazing energy, managed to keep far ahead
in wealth and population of all the other
mainland provinces, the smallest of which
is nearly four times its size, but the pro-
cess can’t go on for ever. And the papers
that want to know why the Government
doesn’t maki the process go on whether it
will or not are asking something very large.
* * * *
Still there is abundant room for expan-
sion, though on a smaller scale than formerly.
As the easy process of attracting new agri-
cultural settlers by selling them State lands
cheaply is practically ended, because there is
little good land left to sell, the obvious course
is to make the population sit closer. As
huge landholders insist on keeping great
areas practically vacant in an overcrowded
province, the State must needs lay compul-
sory hands on their big estates and cut them
up for closer settlement. This will make
more room for agricultural population.
It wants to shut down hard on every
mining - shepherd who holds areas of
auriferous land unworked, and thus make
more room for mining population. It
wants an all-round land-tax, instead of the
poor apology now existing, and then it can
build railways with the proceeds and thus
make an opening for population in the
mallee country and in Eastern Gippsland,
Where there is still vacant space of a kind,
but space that is at present inaccessible. If
the most crowded place in Australia wants
to keep on multiplying population in any-
thing like the same ratio as its comparatively
empty neighbors, then it must take extra
vigorous measures to find room for the new
people, and the obvious way to do that is to
shut down hard on the gentleman who is
occupying more than his share of room now.
And with Victoria’s protective tariff there is
room for great increase in the manufacturing
population. But the assumption that Vic-
toria can keep multiplying population in the
same way as the larger provinces are doing,
by the same policy, is absurd ; it is merely
assuming that because a pint will fill a pint
mug it should fill a quart pot equally well.
Briggs and Kelly.
To which The Bulletin says Amen :
Some men have all the luck. For, instance, there is
Michael Henry Kelly, tried last week at Darlinghurst
for the murder of Maria Flynn, the woman who fooled
him. Kelly was tried on the day after the hanging, at
Darlinghurst, of Briggs, for shooting the woman who
had fooled him,. Broadly speaking, there was very little
difference between the two cases, and where a difference
existed, it was not all in Mr. Kelly’s favor. Both shoot-
ings were more or less prompted by erotic furor and
jealousy ; but there was much more' squalid sordidness
and material motive in Kelly’s murder, judging by his own
admissions, than there was in that of Briggs. The latter,
a great big softy full of red blood, went daft—clean off his
miserable head, despite anything that may be said to the
contrary—over a slender little Eurasian woman with
glittering eyes and teeth and hair, the sort of girl who,
though he could have almost put her in his pocket or
carried two like her under each arm, could yet lash him
to madness with a toss of her little head or a curl of her
pretty lips. She did lash him to madness—anyhow,
Briggs got mad over her, and, taking a revolver]! slew
both her and her old grandmother, whom he blamed for
having counselled her to throw him over. The jury
found Briggs guilty, and did not even recommend him to
mercy ; so this mad fool was hanged. And on
the same gallows were also hanged, to all social
intents and purposes, his decent-living old father and
his loving sisters and the mother who gave him birth.
This in order to teach men tiger-mad from love that they
must not kill!
In the case of Briggs, the giant of baby intellect who
used to discourse at large to his fellow-employbs, at all
times and seasons, on what a beauty his Maggie was and
how he loved her, there was, it must be admitted, some
sentiment; but there was not much sentiment in the
case of Michael Henry Kelly, who also avenged his
alleged wrongs, whatever they were, with a revolver
which he deliberately took with him for the purpose—if
not for the purpose, then for what other purpose ? The
burden of Kelly’s song was not how he had loved
Maria Flynn— his plaint as disclosed to the police
and reporters was how angry he was at being swindled of
money ; also he was determined to get back his engage-
ment-ring—or rather, he explained, he didn’t so much
want the ring as the money it had cost him. Anyhow,
Kelly went to interview Miss Flynn, and after an alter-
cation chased her round and shot her—once, twice,
thrice. And she died ; but, whereas Briggs is hanged
and his corpse sent home as an Easter offering to his old
mother, Kelly (the man who also deliberately put a pistol
in his pocket to settle accounts with a recalcitrant
woman) is adjudged guilty of manslaughter only and gets
seven years—which means about five if he behaves him-
self in gaol.
There may be differences between the two cases other
than those here pointed out, but the line of demarcation
between the guilt of Kelly and the guilt of Briggs is far
too fine to justify one man getting five years and the
other all eternity. The inequality of the alleged justice
meted out in the two cases is absolutely appalling—but it
is easily accounted for. In the first place, both were
actuated by uncontrollable influences, which the law (as
Acting-Justice O’Connor explained)doesn’t recognise, but
the Kelly jury was evidently taught by the fate of Briggs
not to trust to the finicking distinctions of lawyers and
official medicoes. There would, probably, have been no-
public agitation on Kelly’s behalf, as everybody would
be too tired and disappointed over the previous case; so,
the jury very sensibly decided to dole out its own mercy.
Also, Kelly was lucky in not having shot so young and:
pretty a girl as Briggs did, and in being tried in a much,
more Irish (which is to say sympathetic) atmosphere.
The mellifluous barrister Moriarty, instructed by Crick,
made the very best of the defence, and Acting-Justice •
O’Connor in his charge to the jury, though clearly point-
ing out what the law on the subject was, em-
phasised the fact that it was in their power to save the
man’s life. They did save his life ; and how, after all is
over, how many people are there in the community who,
if they had to vote on the question, would not, were it.
possible, save Briggs from the scaffold? If Briggs
deserved hanging, so did Kelly —while the law remains.
as it is.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Kingston as a Tory.
S.A. Premier Kingston ought to be a splendid)
man for a political enemy to cock-shy at in a
general election. For he is always doing some-
thing vigorously ; and, as a general thing, the .
more a man does the of be tier he is likely to go ,
wrong, and the more vigor he puts into his .
work the more attention he is likely to get.
And even if he doesn’t go wrong he gives his .
enemy more chances to misrepresent him. But
not only does Kingston keep moving ; he has.
been doing it far longer than any previous S.A.
Premier. Yet, notwithstanding these things,,
and notwithstanding that he packed his policy-
speech with debatable subjects (theoretically
debatable, anyhow), and threw out a chal-
lenge to Conservative critics every minute'
or two, his enemies clearly don’t know
where or how to begin ; and they have'
been mostly content to cry vaguely that “it
means ruin.” The way of the Oppositionist in
S.A. is hard, though ; for adult suffrage pre-
vails there, and the only way to overcome the
man who is “in ” is to show that the man who
is “ out” is more Liberal and progressive than
the other is ; and, as the Conservatives can’t
attempt that in this case, their only hope is to
show, not that Kingston isn’t so Liberal as
they are, but that he isn’t so Liberal as he says
he is. In the first place, he hasn’t always be-
lieved in the things he believes in now, they
say. S.A. Register triumphantly declares
that it had to fight him in order to help win
some such radical measures as women’s franchise.
But that is simply what Kingston’s friends claim
—that he has gone ahead while his opponents
have slid back. To know the position that
people and papers occupied 20 years ago is
of interest and value to-day chiefly because it
enables us to measure their movements since.
The further they have gone either way the
further they are likely to go ; and the more
political progress Kingston’s enemies can show
he has made, the bigger the hole they knock in
their own argument that he isn’t a progressive
Liberal. And, then, they say that he is an auto-
crat —the worst that was ever in Ministerial
office in S.A. Yet inside five minutes they are
busy pointing out that, though he personally
believes in the capitation grant for denomina-
tional schools, he has so little spirit (is, in fact,
so little of an autocrat) that he is going to re-
spect the referendum vote which decided against
that grant. They don’t say that their sort of a
Democrat—the “real Democrat,” that is—
would simply tell the majority to go fishing
while he fixed things up his own way ; but as
they yell at Kingston for not doing that,
clearly that is their conception of a “real
Democrat.” Fortunately, the majority is likely
to have its own idea about the matter.

British Government backed up Ballance
against the Governor were set forth by Lord
Ripon in his famous despatch. They were :
. 0 ) That the Ballance Government, having a majority
in the popular Chamber, but being in a great minority in
the Council, was entitled to ask for more representation.
(2) That the proposed addition would make the House
no larger than it had been at some previous times.
(3) That the proposed addition wouldn’t wholly destroy
the existing balance of parties.
Sydney Daily Telegraph has been quoting
this despatch against Reid ever since the pro-
posed new appointments were first mentioned.
And Reid has acted on it to the letter. His
12 new members don’t make the Council larger
than it has ever been before ; they only in-
crease the number of M’s.L.C. to 03, and the
number has, within the life of the present
Ministry, been as high as 74. Also, in ’92 it
was 76, so Reid can make seven more appoint-
ments and yet be strictly according to prece-
dent. And they don’t destroy the balance of
parties ; the anti-Billites showed a majority of
15, and this addition still leaves them in a
majority of three, so two more appointments
would still leave that precedent in perfect re-
pair. The provincial party and its organ have
been shrieking to Reid to be constitoosh’nal, as
the constitoosh’nal aspect of the case is laid
down in the famous Ripon despatch—and now
that he has done what they asked with a big
margin of Constitoosh’n to spare, they are a
little more angry than they were before.

Sandgroper Prison. Discipline.
The Westralian Penal Commission, which is
going into the individual cases of local prisoners,
is strengthening the demand for a Public
Defender and a Criminal Court of Appeal ;
also, it exposes once more that remarkable ad-
ministration of Sandgroper gaols under which,
for any of a multitude of breaches of discipline,
sentences are piled up until the Judge’s original
order is far exceeded. Thus a prisoner was
unearthed who has “done ” two years of a five
years’ sentence for stealing £5 worth of goods,
and who has still 4 years and 3 months to serve.
Another, a youth, sentenced for a trivial
offence, tried to get away. He was flogged,
and, when the Commission called round, “ap-
peared to be rapidly deteriorating, physi-
cally, morally, and mentally.” Another
prisoner complained that he ought to be
out of gaol ; he pointed, in proof, to the
scale of remissions exhibited for the en-
couragement of good conduct. Then, as
he seemed to have established his cp,se,
the authorities were appealed to; and
they showed that they calculated remissions on
a different scale altogether. Another prisoner
bad been found guilty of robbery from someone
whom the police could not produce and did nob
know. The case against him was extremely
rocky; but the accused, clumsily defending
himself—there wasn’t anybody else to do so—got
it out that there were previous convictions
against him. The jury accepted that as quite
good enough evidence, and convicted. The
Attorney-General afterwards seems to have
seen what sort of a case it was; but to a re-
quest made by the Commission for the man’s
release he replied that that “ would undermine
all discipline in the gaol ”! In still another
case, in backing up a refusal to recommend a
remission, the Attorney-General mis-stated
an offence for which a prisoner had been
previously sentenced; so that that “Court
of Appeal,” anyhow, is nob particularly
reliable. Nothing is, however, more strik-
ing in the Commissioner’s second report
than the scattered observations concern-
ing policemen and detectives dropping in to
tell prisoners that the officers know now they
are innocent. Eventually the Commission is
driven to remark upon this that “it is most
undesirable that officers of the police depart-
ment who have doubts as to the guilt of con-
victed prisoners should make any statements to'
the prisoners themselves.” They should “ com-
municate their information to their superior
officers.” But the Commission forgets that
discipline which must not be undermined !
Speaking generally, the apportionment of sen-
tences and the administration of prisons are
among the most unscientific things Australia
owns ; but in the West the traditions and pre-
judices of lagdom make them even more un-
scientific than usual.
More Camel.
Two more Afghans who broke their iniquitous
and oppressive agreement with their nigger-
driver, the Camel Carrying Co., of Bourke,
have been arrested on warrants granted by
some white magistrate or J.P. of N.S. Wales,
and are awaiting trial. The Bulletin’s
opinion of the awful beak who grants warrants
in such cases is suppressed as being unfit for
publication. But of one thing it is certain :
If any ordinary white broke his
agreement, and his vindictive boss wanted
a warrant for his arrest, in nine cases
out of ten any magistrate would refuse,
and tell that boss to proceed in the
ordinary way by summons. And the law
shouldn’t do things to help the brutal boss
nigger which it wouldn’t do to help the brutal
boss white—a nigger is no better than a white
man after all. Another thing is certain : When
the employes of the Camel Carrying Co. insist
so unanimously on breaking their agreement
and bolting from work and food and wages in
this bad time and this workless country, and
when no one wants to take their places, despite
the fact that the unemployed are around in
swarms, the Carrying Co.’s service must
be hell —or next door to it. No ordi-
nary boss nowadays cares whether his
hired man breaks his agreement or not—
he can get 300 more at any time. The boss
who has to put his employes in gaol to avoid
losing them just now—now when the person
who advertises for a man to do anything is in
danger of being smothered beneath the heap of
applicants —must be something unspeakable.
And the magistrates who grant warrants for the
recapture of the Carrying Co.’s lost nigger, and
the white man’s country of N.S. W. which
tolerates these magistrates, act as man-hunters
and nigger-drivers and assistant bullies to the
boss camel-man. They are the boss camel-
driver’s gratis bloodhound and mun-SAMBO.
Altogether it is a proud situation for any white
community to find itself in.
His Honor and a Lady.
Last week, in Melbourne, where the adminis-
tration of Justice is beginning to stink more
pungently than even the Yarra, for the first
time in a very long while a woman was
ordered solitary confinement. Judge Hodges
was holding the scales for the time being, and,
seeing that after his treatment of the Dungey-
Bromley case the balance showed a decided
lurch to starboard, he proceeded to make
things sultry for succeeding prisoners. The
woman’s offence was common or shop-door
larceny, and she had been six times previously
convicted —but not of larceny. The previous
offences were mostly “drunk ” or “ insulting
behavior” or “ langwidge,” and the whole lot
together only totalled three mouths. The
steal was a very small one, and was com-
mitted under the influence of drink. The
woman, who was undefended, drew his
Honor’s attention to these matters, and tearfully
pleaded for a lenient sentence. But no ! His
Honor was adamant! Justice must be vindi-
cated and the prisoner be made to squirm
especially after the Dungey-Bromgey farce.
So Mr. Hodges ordered 18 months’ hard labor,
together with six days’ solitary—two at a time.
Then the woman hysterically asked : “Can’t I
make an appeal to your Honor ? ” and Hodges
replied: “No, I’m afraid I’m often too soft;
but there are.times when I can’t be soft.”
Which remark was decidedly bad policy on the
part of Hodges. It drew attention to the fact
that His ’Onah had been remarkably soft in the
case of the other woman—the one who hired
herself out to the detective to persuade
a miserable individual to attempt abor-
tion on her; and who didn’t mind re-
presenting herself, for cash, as a female in
trouble—which generally means a female of
loose character ; and who was quite willing, for
cash, to hand over her body to be pawed (so far
as might be necessary to secure a conviction)
by complete strangers ; and who, having per-
suaded two wretched people to break the law,
rounded on her accomplices and got them
gaoled. His ’Onah was so soft with this woman
that he declared he wouldn’t punish her at all,
even if she was convicted. And his ’Onah’s
remark about the variations in his ’Onah’s
mode of administering justice—about the times
when he is soft and the times when he isn’t—
suggested to ignorant people that his ’Onah fell
like a big Alp on the friendless creature who
did her first little steal in an unsober moment
to make up for the way he grovelled to the
woman who had the Crown influence behind
£er.
Slavery in W.A.
The wrath of Big John Forrest and his crowd
at the badness of those bad men who talk
about nigger slavery in YVestralia is very
pathetic, but the slavery is there all right—
barring the name. The indenture system and
the judicious use of the vagrancy law amount
between them to a very comprehensive policy
of nigger bondage. The W.A. law regards
every nigger as a vagrant —not only the tame
nigger who loafs round towns but the wild
aboriginal in the bush. He is a vagrant because
he lives as his fathers did before him, and
doesn’t pay rent for a stone mansion, and go
daily to a place of business with a bell-topper
on. When the squatter wants aboriginals the
strong arm of the law goes out into the wilder-
ness and catches them, and proceeds to “ lum-
ber ” them as having no visible lawful means
of support. As a matter of fact they have all the
visible lawful means that they want—the wal-
laby and the grub and other wandering victuals
are their visible means—but the pastoral J.P.
refuses to take cognisance of any food that isn’t
bought in a shop. So he gives the nigger six
months, with the alternative that he may get
out of his vagrant state by being indentured for
a long term of years to the squatter. Any
able-bodied nigger that the pastoralist wants
has the alternative of going into bondage under
the indenture system, or of being gaoled over
and over again by the squatter J.P. till he
thinks better of it. But it isn’t slavery—not
actual slavery. The nigger need not be inden-
tured unless he pleases, and he needn’t go to
gaol either, if he obeys the law. Any nigger
who likes to come out of the bush, and open a
bank-account, and live in a good house, and
have an office or a shop and float syndicates,
can go about his business as freely as any
white J.P. It is his sinful obstinacy in not
doing these things which causes all the trouble.

“ Who Benefits by Old-Age Pensions ? ”
Christchurch (M.L.) Press, always strong in
the Conservative interest, wants to know
“who benefits by the Old-Age Pensions ” :
One of the pensioners who appeared on the platform
the other night to present the address to Mr. Sbddon
applied for an order directing his five sons to contribute
towards his maintenance. It appeared that they had
been for some years contributing among them Bs. 6d. per
week without an order, but now that he received a pen-
sion of 7s. per week they thought there ought to be a
“ rearrangement.” The Court ordered them to pay Is. a
week each, so as to make up the applicant’s income to
12s. a week, including the pension. The net result is,
therefore, that these five sons are relieved to the extent
of 3s. 6d. a week in the amount which they
contribute to their father’s support, and the general
taxpayer is called upon to give him 7s. a week.
Ought the aged father to be maintained by his five sons
whom he brought into the world, and doubtless kept dur-
ing the early part of their life, or ought the whole or part
of such duty to be cast upon strangers ? If the second
alternative is to be adopted, the result must in the end be
a loosening of filial bonds and a weakening of the family
as an institution. At the same time, it is easy to under-
stand why Mr. Seddon looks upon the Old Age Pensions
Act as such a good electioneering card. The sons who
found themselves relieved yesterday of a substantial por-
tion of their filial obligations by the State will naturally
vote for the Ministry which shifted the burden from their
own shoulders on to those of strangers.
A great (leal depends on how you put the
thing. There are about 160,000 males of the
taxpaying age outside lunatic asylums, hos-
pitals and gaols in Maoriland, and the Old Age
Pensions will probably cost them something
over £1 a year each. Now, the old man above-
mentioned was a very ancient old man, and to
estimate his life at eight years more is pretty
liberal. And (as a son is generally some
years younger than his father) the five sons
will probably, on an average, judging by their
present ages, remain taxpayers for another 20
years —if they are as tough as the old man they
may keep on paying taxes for even longer than
that. So if there had been no Old Age Pen-
sions their little bill for the maintenance of the
old man would probably have been :
Eight years at £22 2s. per annum ... £l7O 10 o
And under the Old Age Pension system their
bill seems likely to be
Eight years direct payments to the old man
at £l3 per annum £lO4
Twenty years’ contributions to cost of Old
Age Pensions at £5 per annum 100
£204
Apparently these five sons, whose case is such
a horrible example of how to shift the burden
of the aged progenitor on to strangers, have
fallen in. And, meanwhile, the aged progeni-
tor, who used to get £22 2s. a year, now finds
himself revelling in the unaccustomed luxury
of £3l 4s.—so he is doubtless quite satisfied.
What’s the Tory row about, and where does
the “shifting of the burden on to strangers ”
come in ?

A Federal City.
“ Aletes ” to The Bulletin :
Have visited one of the Federal cities, and was nobbled
at my first glimpse of her. Had traversed hundreds of
miles of burnt-up country any shade but green, when
before me appeared an oasis,'a gleaming emerald in a
setting of sapphires. Early morning, on a hill that glit-
tered with the sheen of silver leaves, and looking east-
wards. A new-born sun swathed in clouds. A valley of
blue mist, soft and delicate, a sea of thistle-down. Out
of it rising in harmony together the aspiring efforts of
nature and of man. All around, far off, dark blue hills,
streaked with shadows, undulating gracefully against a
pearl-grey sky. Southward, a grey riband unrolling
and twisting in and out of flat, verdant meadows.
In the air the exhilaration of wine. Went down into
the mist and it faded. Found long wide avenues of
charming, robust trees, clad in autumnal vestures, grey-
green and olive. Beyond and between, peering and
glowing, warm red homes with sweet-smelling gardens.
Wandered south through fields yellow-starred with
dandelion. Found the grey riband a stream of rushing
waters that eddied and gurgled over snags and shallows.
And the shore whereon I stood was alien to the shore
whereon I looked. Two sullen provinces glared across
that laughing face. It was the time-honored barrier that
had kept, would keep, them twain. But the river, know-
ing not, caring not, caressed both shores and mingled
their kisses on its pebbly sandy floor. When lips are
kissing, will hearts be long sundered ? And the stream
knew that beneath it, such a little way down, lay a bond
that all the mighty force of all the mighty nations of the
world could never break. Here surely Nature whispers
of—points to—the cradle of a New Nation. Hgre in this
rich, juicy land of the Grape—
“ The Grape that can with Logic absolute
The two-and-seventy jarring sects confute
The Sovereign Alchemist ”•
Is not this an ideal rendezvous for our future statesmen ?
Destiny hovers above us, shackled, bound to earth. Let
us break the shackles, sever the cord. Then, after
Her !—be it to Paradise or to Perdition !
Hooray !
All last week or more Sydney Telegraph
was fishing with the decomposed worm of
prejudice for old precedents bearing on the
proposed “ swamping” of N.S. W. Leg. Council.
The old precedents didn’t bite freely, still it
got a few. What it struggled to prove, in long
wildernesses of print dealing with the con-
stitoosh’n, was that, if the people were in favor
of some particular course, and the popularly-
elected Assembly was also in favor, and the
Council blocked the way, the only thing to do
was to dissolve the Assembly and get the people
to say all over again that they approved. And
if they did so, and the Council condescended to
be quite satisfied that they had done so, then
the Council would give way—at least the
Telegraph said it would. And if the Council
didn’t give way then there was nothing to be
done. In any case swamping the House of
Nominees was so sinful and illegal as
to be quite out of the calculation. But
Premier Reid waddled quite unconcernedly
through all these cobwebs of precedent, and
paid no more attention than if the D.T. had
been a grasshopper lecturing out in the pad-
dock. The precedent he went on was that a
Government with an Assembly majority behind
it has always had the right to recommend as
Ms.L.C. any moderate number of sober, clean
male persons who haven’t been in gaol, and
don’t whack their wives too publicly, and to
do it without giving any definite reason for its
action. And that seems to be all the precedent
that the case called for.
Beautiful lie which appears persistently in
the advertising columns of a N.S. W. sugar-
district. paper:—
rpo THE RESIDENTS ON THE CLARENCE
-*- AND RICHMOND RIVERS.
Do you know that directly Federation takes place
a bounty of Five Pounds (£5) per ton will be given to
the Victorian growers of beet sugar ?
Sydney, March 24.
The Bulletin respectfully asks the Dibbs and
Heydon and GEORGE-PiLE’s-dog crowd, in
Sydney, if it will repudiate the enthusiastic
member of its party who invented that lie, and
state that, though it is anti-Federal itself, it
wants to win by fair means, and not by such
three-card tricks as the above ? Or will it just
be thankful for whatever votes the above
Ananias puts in its way and say nothing ?
Brisbane Telegraph, among other things,
denounces the Federal Bill as—
A measure which strips all the States of the greater
portion of their natural rights, powers, and possessions,
&c.
And yet it only amounts to this : The six
States (separately) give up to the same six
States (jointly) certain “natural rights, powers,
and possessions.” The anti-Bill crowd’s doc-
trine is : We want union without uniting any-
thing. We want to retain our separate powers
and privileges and to unite at the same time.
We don’t want to unite our customs, excise,
railways, debts, posts, telegraphs, or anything
at all—we merely want to unite in theory.
What we desire is a' disunited Federation in six
separate combined pieces, and we are prepared
to whoop for the distracted Federal fragmen-
tary busted league of Australia for all we are
worth, and to perish fighting for the dissevered
confederation of our great country.
Westralia possesses an annoying person
named A. P. Matheson, who was lately
appointed a member of the Federal Council,
and who, unlike most of those representing the
province on the Council or at the Convention,
considered it necessary that he should know
something of Federation. So when the two
Perth dailies started with a wild screech to
describe the rum Federation would bring on
W.A., Matheson got out his copv of the
Federation BiH ancf compared
With it. He found the West Australian
declaring that the smaller provinces would in-
evitabiy be overruled in changes of constitution,
because if a proposed change were twice
rejected (m a Federal House) it might be at
once sent to a referendum, and, if passed,
become law. Therefore, argued the West
Australian, the big States would have all
their own way in the matter. But Matiie-
son upset this beautiful argument by showing
that clause 127 of the Federation Bill required
a so that the proposed change of constitution
siiould be approved by the electors of a majority
of States. Next the Morning Herald entered
npon its groan and pictured Westralia losing
Customs revenue, because much foreign goods
would be shipped to the larger provinces and
thence distributed to Westralia in coasting
vessels, the importing provinces sticking to the
Customs revenue. Matheson arose once more
and pointed out that by section 93 of the
Commonwealth Bill it was expressly provided
that all duties of Customs and Excise charged
on goods imported into one State and after-
vvards passing into another State for consump-
tion would be credited to the consuming State.
And now these Federation writers who know
nothing of Federation call Matheson an un-
patriotic hireling of the Eastern States, because
be showed them up.
Johnny Haynes and E. M. Clark, M’s.L.A.,
shook the seedy suburb of Leichhardt with
their anti-Federal yell last Saturday. The old,
smiling, clap-trappy John told the people
(Daily Telegraph report) that—
The forcing of a measure through by mere numbers was
utterly opposed to every principle of iiberty dear to Eng-
lish-speaking communities.
So, evidently, the majority shouldn’t rule, for
the only thing a majority can do is to force its
measure through by force of numbers, and as
that is “ opposed to every principle of liberty,”
the proper caper is to let the minority run the
show. And the bawling E. M. Clark got off
the other stereotyped wheeze :
N.S.W. was in the unfortunate condition of the prodi-
gal’s father. The other colonies had broken away from
her and had wasted their substance in riotous living, and
now they wanted to come to her for succor.
It will probably be quite a surprising piece of
news to S.A. and Westralia to hear that
N.S.W. ever was their father, or that they
ever “broke away” from “her” (the father
aforesaid). And the other provinces, after re-
ceiving, in nine years, four urgent appeals
from N.S.W. to come and federate with her,
will probably be a little astonished at the con-
stant lie of the N.S.W. anti-Federal push that
it is they who have been coming to N.S.W. for
succor. But all sorts of strange things happen
in the strange menagerie of anti-Bill. Even
the spectacle of a father being “ her ” is quite
natural there.

A sample of N.S. W. Jaypeeism, from Armidale
Argus, l/4/’99
Thomas Harris, on remand [! ! ! —Ed. B.] was charged
with being indebted to Walton M‘N kill, of Boggabri, in
the sum of £1 2s. for work done, and absconding from
that place whilst so indebted. Mr. S. J. Kearney ap-
peared for accused.
Constable Cornish gave evidence as to arrest, and also
produced a warrant, signed by a magistrate at Boggabri
for the arrest of accused.
Mr. Kearney asked the Bench to dismiss the case against
his client, as the warrant did not contain any specific
charge, and the offence was not to be found on the statute
book.
The P.M., in discharging the accused, said that his
arrest was perfectly illegal, and that the warrant should
never have been issued. The police at Armidale were
quite justified in the action they had taken, but he
thought that the magistrate who signed the warrant had
rendered himself liable to an action for damages. He
considered that the matter ought to be brought under the
notice of the Government.
Just imagine it ! Arrested and remanded on a
charge of owing 225. !

Thus the Australasian on the Q. elections :
Nearly all the mining towns—Gympie, Charters Towers,
Clermont, Croydon—have been captured by Labor. . . .
But the extraordinary thing is that nearly all the great
pastoral electorates in the Central-West and the North-
West remain the spoil of the Labor politicians. The em-
ploying class, which finds the capital and takes the ter-
rible risks of a capricious climate, is prostrate before the
class it supports ! !! ”
This is truly fine. The poor devils who shear
in a shed where the mercury runs up to 120° in
the shade don’t take any risks at all. The
hapless swagman who dies of thirst beside a
dried-up tank in a droughty summer isn’t
exposed to the fury of a “ capricious climate ”
in the least—we suppose. And, as to supporting
any class, how can a poor wretch who has no
capital beyond a pair of brawny arms and a
weary swag do anything at all in the line of
“ supporting ” ? Why—he’s only here on the
earth upon sufferance.
Re the frequent solitary confinement of young
“Willie” Phillips, prisoner at; Pentridge
(Melb.), a former mate of the unhappy
crank informs The Bulletin that Phillips
—“loathsome little beast” was driven
to insubordination by the system. In the
first instance, the murderer of bookkeeper
Brady, whose lying “ confession ” had saved
him from the gallows, was a pampered criminal,
as Pentridge pampering goes. He robbed the
prison store, was sent to solitary confinement,
and thence to separate solitary confinement—
whatever the difference may mean. Now he is
a persistent rebel, banging away at the door of
his lonesome hell, and rapidly qualifying him-
self for a certificate of lunacy. The Bulletin,
whilst opposed to hanging as a judicial punish-
ment, admits that an early death would have
been a good thing for the individual Phillips.
But, seeing that his sentence was commuted,
Phillips should be treated by the law as one
whose life is worth preserving. At present, he
suffers persecution, not on account of the
murder and thefts he committed in and out of
gaol, but because he has lost his last vestige of
self-restraint. It were hard to say what ought
to be done with Phillips, but it is certain that
the thing which ought nob to be done happens
every week in this case.
It is officially estimated that the population
of Westralia decreased by 771 during March,
making a decrease of 1657 for the first three
months of ’99.
