To-day (Thursday afternoon) Mr. R.
D’Orsay Ogden is to have a benefit at Sydney
Criterion. Among the patrons are Premier Reid,
the Mayor of Sydney, the Major-General, and
other full-rigged notables of great tonnage and
carrying capacity, and a very fine pro-
gramme has been arranged with many good
people in it. Apropos of the above, a cor-
respondent sends in a summary of Mr. Ogden’s
troubled career. Mr. Ogden is now 64, and it
is 43 years since he was persuaded to leave
the stage (he had just made a beginning in
the Southern States) and go with Walker, about
the last of the filibusters, to invade Nicaragua and
Honduras. Walker ran an exciting dictatorship
for a year or two and was then captured and exe-
cuted, and young Ogden and 84 others were
going the same road when some influential Ameri-
can friends obtained a reprieve for the former.
The 84, having no influential friends handy, “went
up.” Ogden left the stage again to be a captain
in the Confederate service at the beginning of the
Civil War, and he saw it through ; the battered
remnant of him was among the crowd which sur-
rendered with Lee in ’65 at Appomatox Court
House. Connoisseurs who have seen the old
veteran without his clothes say that he isn’t
pleasant to look upon “in the nood,” being
grievously scarred and hacked and chipped by
shot and other hostile expressions. Anyhow, The
Bulletin trusts that his benefit will be a soure e
of great wealth, and that his latter days will be
more peaceful than those in which he carried a
gun in Nicaragua.
** * *
The unpaid wash-bill of Bonaparte still
waves rather unsatisfactorily on the outer wall at
Sydney Royal. There is nothing large the matter
with the new Brough venture, but various
small things, are against it, and, so far as The
Bulletin can size up the situation, Sydney only
feels moderately good over the new venture. It
expected great things of the co. to begin with, for
it remembered the almost perfection of the two
B.’s combination in its best days; and the only
comparative regularity of the goose-step kept by
the new crowd is a disappointment. It is a
respectable, ordinary co., so far as can be seen, but
miles behind the old 8.-B. tradition. That
tradition, pursues Brough like the wet grey dog
that will insist on coming home with one at the
hazy hour of 13 p.m. Then “ Sans-Gene ” depends
practically upon two characters, and Brough is so
far away from all the accepted theories of
the Man of Destiny that 'one of the two
fails to strike the public in the right place.
Possibly he is nearer to the real individual than
the idealised portrait of the man with the pallid
face and the dark forelock. Napoleon I. was un-
fortunately out when The Bulletin called at his
palace, so'it has to take his countenance on trust.
But, anyhow, it is too late to start making a new
Bonaparte now, and that is practically what is
being attempted at the Royal. “ Sans-Gene,” at
the best, is never likely to catch on with the
gallery or the back row of the pit. It has no par-
ticular story to tell, and no particular ending.
The fact that the rather inaudible Austrian gentle-
man escapes from serious trouble in the last act
would strike the public harder if the said Austrian
had previously got a hold on its sympathies, but
he hasn’t. If Napoleon would even pay his long-
forgotten wash-bill it would be something definite,
but just when he is about to pay it he finds he has
no money in his apparel, and the chance is lost.
#* * *
The unaccountable brown lady at Sydney
Lyceum, who makes so many excited remarks
about the British Flag, hasn’t retired from her
illogical attitude on that subject, and all demands
for an explanation remain unanswered. The
Eublic has taken rather kindly to her on the whole.
he wears gaudy red and yellow clothes which are
so built that one plump, coffee-tinted shoulder and
one bangled arm are bare —-the arm with which
she waves the knife that never injures anybody;
she always waves it when she is having her worst
fit about the British Flag. And once, when
something very awful has just been done on the
stage, a back curtain goes up silently and impres-
sively, like the price of eggs rising in the night,
and reveals her as a brown statue pointing her
knife at the Milky Way. There is a palm-tree or
a cactus or a baobab, or something, in the back-
ground, and a streak of starry sky and a patch of
sea, and the vision leaves an idea that the native
lady sleeps that way instead of going to bed.
Nothing new has been done in the way of scenic
effects since last week, for if any more scenery
was put in the Lyceum would burst.
** * *
The last nights of the “ Geisha ” are an-
nounced at Her Majesty’s, so whoever has not seen
the artless Jappy frivol is hereby warned to roll
up. The principal reason why everybody should
roll up is that the larger the multitude the more
chance there is that somebody in it may be able to
really explain what all the rolling-up is about.
For The Bulletin doesn’t know and never ex-
Sects to. “ lolanthe ” is in preparation at Her
lajesty’s. Considering what a furore the
“Geisha” made, beautiful “lolanthe” should
cause such a rush that there will be fatal acci-
dents on the stairs, but the corpses will be
carried up in the storm of flesh and tall hats and
swallow-tails and won’t have room to drop.
** * *
“ Othello ” keeps up its record of unquali-
fied success at Sydney Criterion, but Rignold
wisely recognises that the life of even the best
Shakespearian show is limited, and the “Merry
Wives of Windsor ” is in preparation. The date
of the new production is yet unfixed, but the
management is getting ready to take off its
strangled Desdemona as soon as circumstances
call for her removal.
** # *
Three lady gymnasts bearing the strange
out-of-date name of Keziah —three Keziahs—are
to join Rickards’ Tivoli co. on Saturday, and
Manuel Woodson, contortionist, is due at the
same date. So are Edith Conley and George
Carew. The latter pair will try to fill the vacant
places of Albert Bellman and bright Lottie Moore,
who are going this week.
** * #
Fitzgeralds’ Circus is in splendid health
at Redfern (Sydney), and is crowded to the door
every night. It has done even better this time
than on its last appearance ; probably the extra
people come in hopes that the lion and the ele-
phant will have an unpleasantness and fight it
out on the tricycle, or that the tiger will take
an objection to the horse. Among the large
collection of beasts which smell more or less
in the back part of the show the ourang-
outang seems to draw most money. He is
supposed to be a direct ancestor of man, as
man was before he scraped the hair off himself and
started to build gaols to put himself in ; and he
looks like an animal with a Past. Both the
menagerie and circus grow larger every time the
Fitzes come this way, and if it grows much larger
the average tired man will have to hire someone
to help him to look at it.
** * #
“Round the World in Eighty Days” is
doing its last week at Sydney Opera House, but
its successor is not announced up to time of
writing.
* * *
The Bulletin has been requested (by a
lady) to unreservedly withdraw some allusions in
its issue of 25th March, as follows :
In the absence of any further law reports about the
fury of the American gentleman who used to offer cock-
tails to the press of Sydney, it is generally understood
that the troubles of Dante and his late partner have been
settled. At all events, Dante and manager Lohr are left
in peaceful possession at the Palace, and the former
draws his spectral refreshment out of the empty drain-
pipe with the air of a man who has no troubles on his
mind, and his programme of strange appearances and
disappearances, and his bottle of innumerable drinks, and
his other mysteries, hustle along in a pleasant, light-
hearted fashion. Dante’s real name—as sworn to when
the embittered American gentleman wanted damages, or
the earth, or something—is Oscar Eliason, which would
look a good deal better on the bills, in The Bulletin’s
opinion, than his professional cognomen—&c.
* * * *
The paper feels in a pacific mood this week,
and so friendly that it would feed out of the hand,
so it doesn’t mind withdrawing. Every hospitable
American gentleman who comes this way offers
the press a cocktail when they happen to meet,
but if the friends of the one above-mentioned de-
sire to have him represented as a dry and inhos-
pitable American gentleman—well, the cocktail is
off. And if he happens this way again and doesn’t
approve of the action of his friends in this matter,
the cocktail can be turned on again. It was a
very good cocktail, too, that one which didn’t
happen; and the writer would have enjoyed
it very much when he and the Ameri-
can gentleman foregathered at the Marble Bar
if either of them .had been there, only they
weren’t. This paper doesn’t mind going further,
if it will make things any pleasanter, and saying
that there is no such person as either of them, and
the idea of a myth offering a drink to a baseless
rumor is quite absurd. Anyhow, the gentleman
wasn’t furious, nor embittered, nor any other
adjective whatever, nor any pronoun, verb or in-
terjection either, and he didn’t want the earth nor
anything else. The Bulletin is quite prepared
to say that he was so absolutely contented that he
didn’t want anything at all—not even his own
clothes. The present writer has always wanted
the earth himself, and doesn’t fully understand
this contented state of mind, but if the above
statement, or any other statement, will help to
make things any smoother, or help a fellow-mortal
on his path, it is all right. If there is anything
more that the friends of the gentleman so unfor-
tunately alluded to would like to have withdrawn
they have only to mention it. The withdrawal it-
self, if it doesn’t suit, can be withdrawn at the
shortest notice. Present writer, in earlier and
more stormy days, made a living for a while by
withdrawing timber off a range of mountains
with a horse-team, and he once withdrew from a
mine just before it caved in, After that sort of
experience he reckons he knows enough about the
business to withdraw anything.
** * *
Melbourne is not very angry with “The
Belle of New York ” now that the first-night
disappointment has spent its fury. The
“B. of N.Y.” is to ordinary musical comedy
what “Evangeline” of yore was to contemporary
opera-bouffe. In defence of the present importa-
tion, the Firm may plead that, whereas “Evan-
geline” —an inane “opera” with a fat Fortescue
and a speechless Lone Fisherman in it— always
ranked as insufferable rubbish outside its
native country, the comedy which contains a
Mixed Ale Pugilist, a Polite Lunatic, and a
Eiir of Automatic Twins is a success in
ondon and other un-American places. But it
appears, from information received, that “The
Belle ” has prospered abroad mainly through the
saving grace of the lady in the name part. Nobody
thinks the comedy humorous, but everybody
admires the picture of the dainty Salvation Army
girl, who makes the whole Bowery fragrant and
flowery and leads all the young men from destruc-
tion for just as long as they can keep her figure in
view. There’s a happy dramatic thought in the
lament of the eternal Feminine, as she droops her
tambourine and gazes straight down her dear little
nose—“ They don’t proceed to follow the Light:
they only follow me.”
* * ■* *
If Miss Louise Hepner, in the name-part,
and tall, thin Oscar Girard, in the character of
her adopted guardian, were not bright, refined
artists, “ The Belle of New York ” wouldn’t de-
serve any more consideration than “The Ameri-
can Girl ” got at the Princess’s last year. On the
other hand, if Miss Hepner had a finer voice and
a little more magnetism, and if she looked as con-
vincing to the people in the way-back stalls as she
appears to the front rows of audience, the Firm’s
latest production might have been less roughly criti-
cised by folks in general. But it occurs to Bulle-
tin that although Miss Hepner would carry 40
per cent, more charm in a smaller theatre, the
performances of the second-raters (the very
second-raters) would be less trying if the house
were much bigger ; and, anyway, the Princess’s is
just the right size for the Firm’s chorusers and
chorusesses, who are many and frequent in the
“B. ofN.Y.” Some bottled-up feeling concern-
ing the awfullest features of this show will pro-
bably explode next week, or later. Meanwhile
the appallingly stout gent in the tight-fitting
fancy vest is under observation; but the young
hero, the automatic Twins, and the mock trage-
dian named Mugg have been denied the benefit
of the doubt, because they are beyond any reason-
able doubt whatsoever.
** * *
The opinion of Easter multitudes at the
Royal—they swarmed there every night last week
—was palpably favorable to Bland Holt’s gorgeous
undertaking, so it may be that “ Woman and
Wine ” is booked for a long run. Success comes
easily to a melodrama which reveals a steeple-
chase, a ballet, and a knife-contest between two
improper ladies, especially when the duel is
backed up by artist Brunton’s best work. The
Paris Flower Market, growing brighter in the
rising sunlight, is a great scene for a mortal
combat, and the destruction of Miss Wat-
son by Miss Gill now" shows a great improve-
ment on the first-night effect. If the struggling
furies will kindly add a couple of . inciden-
tal shrieks, and allow Caliban to re-sharpen
their knives on his grindstone at half-time,
“Woman and Wine” will be as crimson a
spectacle as one could wish. The grindstone is a
delightfully gruesome idea as far as it goes, but it
ought to be carried further. “Caliban” Styan,
Miss Gill, Walter Baker, and Miss Watson are
expending a lot of evil passion upon this well-
painted drama, also the comic relief seems to
appeal to that part of the audience which
doesn’t always remove its hat. Mrs. Bland
Holt, whose yellow hair and new, frilly man-
ners suit her nicely, is the lawful mother of
Bland’s live baby, and the baby is discovered by
father Brown before he has learned of his boy’s
matrimony. When Brown charges boy Bland
with the authorship of the mysterious infant, and
demands the name of the mother that he may
force her to “make an honest man ” of Bland by
marrying him, the dear creatures in the stalls and
circles enjoy a roar of innocent merriment all
round. Then they remark to one another that the
joke is “ rather suggestive,” and they endeavour
to feel like angels suffering from a sudden attack
of double entendre.
** * *
Some years ago Melbourne went suddenly
crazy over the matter of John Kruse, a Victorian-
born violinist of the most respectable character.
R. S. Smythe was the power behind the Kruse
boom, and most of the profits went into his mUch-
travelled pocket—which was a fair thing under
the circumstances. Since the Kruse craze there
has never been a Melb. Town Hall rush to com-
pare with the rush to hear Miss Amy Castles,
“the wonderful soprano,” on Saturday night.
Miss Castles, from Bendigo, represents the
first attempt of the Austral Salon to create
a Future Melba and shine in her reflected
glory, and the result thus far is a great
success for the hens and chickens of that
clucksome institution. The Salon influence is
responsible for Cast.es in the air, where the dam-
sel will remain awhile. The public believed her
wonderful before they heard her sing ; her voice
was sufficiently strong and sweet to keep the pub-
lic faith alive. That’s how the matter stands. To
the wild, hysterical eye of the easy believer, Miss
Castles presents herself as a young and growing
girl whose voice will rise and swell to the pitch of
perfection under scientific tuition. Many musi-
cal authorities profess to take the same view of
the case. Musical experts are always seek-
ing outlets for their enthusiasm, and they are
all open to receive a new pupil. The present
writer is a mere Goth, haunted by recollections of
previous future Melbas and Crossleys, whose
“wonderful” promises haven’t been fulfilled,
therefore this scribe will swear to nothing re Miss
Amy Castles, except that her prodigious self-con-
fidence doesn’t indicate what Svengali called “ the
artist nature.” Miss Regina Nagel’s nervousness
was much more promising. The Goth is told
Miss Castles will be 18 next birthday, and he
presumes that her mother hasn’t lost count of one
or two years, but he reckons that the young lady’s
childishness begins in her loose hair and terminates
round the hem of her short frock. At the
ago of 18 she is seemingly a little woman
of unrobust appearance. She sings with a
big, round voice, which is quite likely to
lose some of its music in course of educa-
tion, but is not very likely to develope more
feeling. If a full, round voice doesn’t thrill one,
the power to thrill is, presumably, lacking. This
unbelieving scribe has heard Miss Castles sing
“ Qui la voce ” in a room and at the Town Hall,
and no chord came from the soul of her vocal
organ to enter into his. Therefore, he assumes
that either he or the damsel in the juvenile frock
hasn’t a soul, and he considers himself an ignorant
brute, anyhow.
At Melb. Alexandra the unspeakable
humor of “Jack Sheppard ” was seriously digested
by a vast audience on Saturday, and the plump
she-hero known as Jack aforesaid will commit
various outrages for one week only. Also, Jona-
than Wild, assisted by Blueskin, will die of an
alleged gunpowder explosion, leaving the small
boys in the gallery to whistle his requiem. As a
lesson in managerial policy, and a revelation of
weird wigs and other clothing, “Jack Sheppard”
is a thing to see and remember evermore. Duncan
Macallum’s advt. exhorts the masses to look out
for “Waiting for the Verdict,” which will
probably happen next Saturday. The Bulletin
is looking out.
** * *
Dante, the conjuror who captured the
vote and interest of this journal when he first
appeared in Sydney—Dante the Great, likewise
the Only—is to introduce hifnself to Melbourne on
Saturday next (15th) at St. George’s Hall. The
long, lank building next to the Theatre Royal has
been an unfrequented place for a good many years,
but Dante will just come along and produce large
and fashionable attendances from his hat every
evening until further notice. At the Bijou all is
well with the Rickards Co., and in Melbourne’s
highah musical circles the fame of Herr Frieden-
thal looms large just now. The German pianist,
who plays a lone hand, i. e. , two lone hands, and
is said to keep his audiences excited all the time,
will appear at the Town Hall on Saturday and
some days next week.
*# * *
Allan and Co., the Melb. music publishers,
have removed from their fortunate “ pitch,” at the
corner of the Block Arcade, into bigger premises
(a few doors higher up in Collins-street) erected on
the site of the warehouse where the late George
Allan, sen., started business 22 years ago. For
the new warehouse, which formally opened last
week, the present George Allan claims that it is
the best-equipped institution of the kind in Aus-
tralia, and all the long-haired professors who have
sampled it sing praises to its name. In fact, Mel-
bourne’s musical taste will need to advance and
expand considerably in order to get on level terms
with the place where the local plutocracy gathered
for harmonious drinking purposes last Thursday.
Allan and Co. have done much towards creating
customers for the music-trade, and they hope to
do better as time goes on. Meanwhile the manager
makes a reasonable appeal for a municipal brass
band, chosen from the local military tunists, to
play in the city’s public reserves and prepare the
souls of the masses for the gospel of Marshall Hall.
Perth has a Chinaman called Shoo Hen.
“Gladstone is of the believing tempera-
ment, without a single conviction.” — Froude.
“ The French headquarters staff were cog-
nisant of Esterhazy’s guilt, but shielded him, lest
proof of his crimes should exonerate Dreyfus.”
“ The Honor of the Army ! ”
In Chambers this week Chief-Justice Dar-
ley gave a rule nisi in the case of an Afghan em-
ployed by the slave-driving Camel Co., of Bourke;
restraining the local magistrate from further
iniquitous imprisonment.
Judging from the wired accounts of his re-
ception at Adelaide, Gov. Tennyson takes himself
very seriously indeed. Also, he is rather inclined
to lay on jam with a trowel and spread his little
net in sight of the birds.
One circumstance in connection with the
recent Victorian military corroboree that wasn’t
reported by the great dailies, was the hopeless
breaking-down all over the shop of the ammuni-
tion waggons constructed in a hurry for use at the
encampment. One contingent went to .pieces
within a mile or two of Melbourne, and a lot more
were scattered about in ruins before the great
martial scramble was ended, and in several cases
crazy furniture vans had to be utilised to carry
round the requisites, thus imparting a painful low-
comedy flavor to the warlike and bloodthirsty
proceedings.
The Victorian minimum wage law has
raised the wages in the furniture trade from 30s.
to about 50s. Owing to the fact that nearly every
Chinese tradesman in Melbourne is in the furni-
ture biz, this was the most utterly depressed in-
dustry in the city, and the minimum wage gave
it a great lift. But the Chow cabinetrmaker
breaks the law so persistently that there will either
have to be a separate inspector for each Chow, or
the alternative will some day lie between letting
go the minimum wage or rushing the leper wood-
carver into the mail-boat for home. And the
sooner the latter is done the better.

* * * *
The depression in the tin-market of late years
starved out nearly all the white leaseholders on Tas. East
Coast, and as John Chinaman mostly took up the alluvial
he is, if the present boom continues, going to be a big
man in the Isle of Bad Dreams.
* * * •
Just when copper was booming most wildly the
death was cabled of M. Secretan, projector of the disas-
trous French Copper Syndicate of eight years ago. But
France is “ off’’.such corners ; and when, the other day,
there was a Parisian rumor that French speculators were
dabbling in a copper “operation” again, there were
prompt questionings in the Deputies and the equally
prompt promise of the Justice Minister to inquire, and
prosecute if there was found to be anything in the rumor.

Jaques Bros., engineers, Coppin-st., Richmond,
Melbourne, Vic., assay ores, and treat samples up to
5 tons, by chlorination, cyanide, or amalgamation ; wet
or dry crushed. Manufacturers of Rock Breakers, Stamp
Batteries, Roasting and Smelting Furnaces, and General
Mining Machinery.*
* * * *
The Chief Engineer of Vic. Railways, discussing
apian drawn nine years ago for a big central station in
Melbourne, gave some figures which go far towards excus-
ing the errors of Speight’s sanguine management. It
seems that between 1883, the year of Speight’s arrival,
and 1890, when the land-boom was at its boomiest, the
annual number of passengers booked on Melb. suburban
lines rose froha 25,000,000 to 59,000,000. Speight’s calcula-
tions were based on an almost incredibly false state of
things. A yearly increase of traffic was taken for granted
at that time, whereas the suburban railway returns last
year, in spite of “ returning prosperity,” showed a reduc-
tion of about 16,000,000 passengers on the figures for 1890.

N.S.W. Chamber of Mines urges that tnere
should be a provision in the new Mines Act for the
licensing of all gold-buyers. Simultaneously Vic. Mana-
gers’ Association (a body which, by the way, doesn’t put
on any frills) asks the Miners’ Association to co-operate in
the same direction. No reasonable objection can be
found to the proposal ; but it isn’t going to stop either
gold-pilfering or the selling of pilfered.gold. In'Vic., at
any rate, nearly all the “ crook” gold gets into the banks ;
and it has been suggested, too, that branch officers, sus-
pecting that all hasn’t been quite right, have ventured to
offer below a fair price, and have then flattered them-
selves that they have done good biz. for their bosses.
While Parliament is about it, it ought to license gem-
buyers, too ; that is wanted in some districts very badly,
if only to silence nasty rumors.

» * • »
Prospects seem better for tin than for copper.
Of the former metal, by far the biggest producers are the
Straits Settlements, worked mostly by Chinese. Now,
without digging up once more the sadly-battered “ cur-
rency problem,” it is certain that these Eastern mine-
bosses have benefited hugely by the fall in silver ; they
sell for geld and pay in the white stuff. Their advantage
has been so great that for three years past they have
strained production to what is believed to be the very
limit, and indeed past the limit that can be sustiined.
The recent big rise, therefore, is not at all likely to be
followed by that spurt in output which most big rises ring
in.
• * * *
Mephan Ferguson, engineer, etc., has just com-
pleted at his engineering works, Footscray, Melb., one of
the largest planing machines every made in any part of
the world. It weighs about 100 tons, and is the machine
to be used in planing and thickening up the edges of
28ft. x Jin. plates required in manufacturing the patent
Lockingbar pipes for Coolgardie water supply contract. All
the steel bars for this contract will be'supplied from the
Earl of Dudley works, Eng., but in order to get prompt
delivery the order for the steel plates has been divided
amongst four German firms. The first shipment of 500
tons will be dispatched by the Maori King, now loading.*
* * * *
“The value of Klondyke gold varies much, ac-
cording to locality where found,” writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent. “ The maximum price has been £3 7s. 6d. to
£3 10s. per oz. The ‘ Bench ’ claims turned out very
good last season—notably French Hill (on the Eldorado)
and Big Skookum (now Gold Hill, at the fork of the
Bonanza and Eldorado), though, as in creek .claims, the
patchy nature of the deposits in bench claims proves
many a hard-sunk shaft a duffer. One man’s claim on
the second tier on Big Skookum turned out rich, whereas
the next but one, though located precisely over the old
river bed, has not even a color on bedrock. Another
claim on No. 3 tier had two cradles going for nine or ten
weeks, daily average 200 oz. coarse gold, and yet the next
claim was utterly valueless. No payable quartz-leaders
have been found as yet; reports to the contrary are wild
cats, and it needs reef-mining to give the place stability ;
but the country is so vast that prohably such finds will be
made yet.”

At time of writing the expenditure accounts
from two provinces were to hand. They compared with
revenue as follows:
Queensland (Surplus) .. £570,168
New South Wales (Deficit) .. 565,204
Owing to the different dates at which interest on the
public debt falls due, Queensland usually has a surplus
and N.S.W. a deficit about this time. The only interest-
ing point is that both are a good deal larger than usual.
The New South Wales deficit at 31st March,
1893, was £316/171, so things are £248,933 worse than they
were at the corresponding period of 1897 and this,
despite the fact that N.S.W. is the only province of the
group which materially increased its taxation this year.
The increase of £474,049 in the expenditure and the fact
that the income-tax returns are late this year are the
visible causes of the trouble. For the nine months to
31st March, 1899, the expenditure out of loans has been
£1,496,054 —£225,140 more than the loan expenditure for
the nine months to 31st March, 1898. Every year the
pile of small unproductive everyday works and jobs and
repairs which ought to be charged to revenue, and which
are charged to loans to make the deficit look smaller,
grows more formidable. Ttie Bulletin begins to foresee
a joyous time (if Reid remains long enough in office) when
everything will be charged to loans, and therefore all the
ordinary revenue will be surplus.

When the Federal Enabling Bill was before the N.S.W.
Council the proviricialist fossils rose one by one to lament
that N.S.W. was the only financially progressive province
in the group and shouldn’t dream of federating with the
other wrecks) whose poverty was so awful that they
would have to live on N.S.W. As old Macintosh wailed,
the other States “are now in such a position that they
can hardly raise any money or pay interest on their debts ;
most of them are in a decline.” To the ignorant old
political louts of the Council it is so much an article of
faith that N.S.W. must always be the only prosperous
place in the crowd that they never worry about the facts
or the. figures. The Macintosh kind of man is beautiful
material to make a life-legislator out of.
* * * *
The complete report by mail explains that Vic-
toria’s conversion loan of £1,600,000 realised £94 17s. 6d.,
and as stamp-duty to the British Govt., &c., presumably
have to be deducted, the net result to Vic. will be about
£94. And this is described as a success. Now, let it be
supposed that, instead of borrowing from Bull, Vic. Govt,
had had pluck enough to use itsown credit, by issuing notes
which would be legal tender within the province for all pur-
poses, including the payment of State taxes, and adopted
the Canadian principle of requiring the banks to keep
40 per cent, of their local reserves in State notes. That
would have enabled it to lay its hands on the required
£1,600,000 and a good deal over without interest. If it
was prepared to be just half as generous to the local banks
as to the British money-lender, and gave them £lOO
in notes for £97 in gold (the British money-lender got
£lOO paper for £94 gold) that would have meant a
bonus of £48,000 to the banks, which would make these
institutions feel good all over ; and the State would still
have saved £48,000 cash and £48,000 a year interest. And
if it liked to put £30,000 a year of the saving to a sinking
fund, to accumulate at 2J4 per cent., so as to create a
cash-reserve against these notes, the liability could be
wiped-out in 34 years without costing the State a copper.
And still the State would be £48,000 better off in immedi-
ate cash, and, during the 34 years while the sinking fund
was accumulating, it would be £lB,OOO a year better off,
and, when the 34 years had expired, it would be £48,000
a year better off for ever after in interest and £1,000,000
better off in the principal of its debt; and the banks would
have made a clear profit of £48,000. There is absolutely no
need for any sinking fund except to reassure an unaccus-
tomed public ; and the proposed £48,000 bonus to the
banks is pure bribery to buy off their opposition—but
even with these allowances there is a lot of money in the
idea. Also, these calculations are based on the assump-
tion that the State-note-issue system gave the Govern-
ment command of only £1,600,000, but, as a matter of
fact, it would, on the Canadian system, produce much
more. Of course if the State made a further rise by
suspending private note issues and substituting its own
notes, the money thus raised would cost a trifle per cent.,
being the amount of the lost note-tax—where there is one
(the highest is only 2 per cent). But the money raised
by requiring the banks to keep part of their reserves in
State notes would cost no interest whatever.

The best news that has come from Westralia for
a long, long time is the official announcement that the
wheat harvest is 857,362 bushels, as compared with
408 595 bushels last year. The yield is 11$ bushels to the
acre against 10$ bushels last season, so the increase is
mostly through increased acreage under crop. There is
far more substantial wealth in agriculture than in
trvim- to sell the elusive wild cat on the London
market Yet even now W.A., despite its huge area,
small population and great extent of rich, well-watered
land, doesn’t grow nearly enough wheat for its own con-
sumption ; in proportion to population it doesn t pro-
duce one-third as much as crowded Victoria. And this
year it will have to pay, out of its scarce supply of money,
for 529 000 bushels of imported wheat, when it ought to be-
the biggest exporter in the group. Tim ’groper’s capacity
for slumbering beside his rusted plough is something in-
credible. . „
F. E. Winchcombe, of Sydney, who is now run-
ning fora seat on A.M.P. directorate and wants to intro-
duce some new blood into the board, is an eminently
successful and trusted businessman ; and the biggest life-
assurance society in this hemisphere should afford just
the right field for his abundant energy. Eleven years ago
he was salesman to the old firm of Mort and Co. Now he
is senior partner in Winchcombe, Carson and Co., which
firm he founded, and which has grown up under his direc-
tion to be one of the principal wool institutions of Aus-
tralasia. Also he appeals tP A.M.P. members as a veteran
policy-holder of 24 years’
The Planet Permanent Building Society (Mel-
bourne), a large concern which has long been struggling
with its difficulties, announced that its operations
for 189 S had reduced the accumulated loss from .£11,823
to £9782. On the face of things this looked like a profit
of over £2OOO, but an inspection of the balance-sheet ap-
pears to knock this favorable impression endways. Some
time ago the Planet Co. surrendered its office-buildings to
the mortgagee under certain conditions, and he sold
them, apparently, for £5781 more than the.mortgage, and
this sum, being put in among the ordinary revenue, has
enabled the society to reduce its debt. The ordinary
revenue and expenditure for the year is as follows :

The profit on the sale of the society’s office building Gan
only happen once, and the loss on sales of foreclosed pro-
perties appears likely to go on for a long long time, for
out of £157,000 worth of alleged assets properties in pos-
session represent nearly £132,000. Anyhow, the Planet
isn’t earning enough to pay interest and working expenses.
Its deposits only cost it 2$ per cent, at present, and even
at that low rate it fails to make ends meet—the chief
cause of the difficulty being that its assets only yield
about 2 per cent., and they are all in pawn to another
building society. During the year it seems to have sold
property worth £9250 on paper, and realised £6141, and
if all the properties pan out like this, the loss on realisa-
tion of £132,000 worth will be £44,000.
»,¥ ¥ *
The Equitable Life Assurance Society of the
United States publishes its report for 1898. Premiums
were £8,050,105 —an increase of more than £200,000 as
compared with 1897. Consideration for annuities
(£163,723) shows a decrease of £23,000. Interest and
rents (£2,177,289) show an increase of over £lOO,OOO. The
total income is £11,301,446, and the amount added to the
funds is £4,494,518. The Equitable’s total pile is now
£53,445,840, and the surplus of assets over liabilities is
£11,918,852. These are figures of great magnificence, yet
there are sundry contras to set against them. The surplus
appears to be arrived at by assuming a 4 per cent, future
rate of interest on the funds —so far as concerns
great part of the business at all events—and a society
which barely earns £4 ss. per cent on its mean
funds for the year has no right to assume any future
4 per cent. ; the margin is distinctly too narrow. A re-
duction of assumed interest rate to 3$ would still leave a
huge surplus, but it would be about £2,500,000 less than
it now is ; and a reduction to the much safer 3 per cent,
would further reduce the surplus to, probably, about
£7,000,001). That ought to be enough for any office, but
apparently the Equitable wants more. No less than
£455,794 of the assets consist of that debateable item,
“ Deferred premiums on' policies When paid semi-annually
or quarterly,” an item which would be much better
struck out bodily. And £910,329 of the income for 1898 (also
£273,765 of the income for ’97 and various sums in the in-
come of some other years) is made ujfof “Gain in Valuation
of assets.” This item suggests that the Equitable, when
its assets go up in market value, is very prompt in taking
advantage of the improvement, and though the process
is quite legitimate (and also helps to make the surplus
look bigger) it necessarily diminishes the margin of safety
in case of a general fall in values later on. The moral of
all which is that the Equitable is very progressive and
very large indeed, but that it suffers from an incurable
desire to look even larger than it is.
* * * *
The Colonial Sugar Refining Co. buys cane now
in N.S.W. by the percentage of sugar it contains. Said
percentage is ascertained by its own staff by chemical
analysis, concerning which the co. gives the farmer no
information. Last year on the Clarence the results
were below growers’ anticipations, and the Farmers’
Union asked that a check on the analysis by a comparison
of the output of sugar should be permitted. The co. re-
turned an evasive reply, and didn’t budge. When pay-
ment by analysis was introduced in connection with
damaged cane two years ago, a mass meeting of farmers
asked the Premier to send a Govt, expert to check the
co’s analj’sis, but nothing eventuated then either. The
co’s method of purchasing cane by its own private valua-
tion in its own laboratories appears to call for legislative
interference.

* * * *
The report of the Engineer-in-Chief of N.S.W.
Works Department ought to be comforting reading for
Broken Hill Water Supply shareholders. The local
municipal council asked for a report on a possible rival
scheme, the Yanco Glen. Mr. Darley thinks very little of
it (it is true he is accused on the spot of making only a
superficial examination), but admits that the water-
charges of the existing co. are heavy. He remarks,
however (as The Bulletin has done several times), that a
co. which has to hand over its expensive works within
about 25 years of their completion without compensation
cannot afford to make ordinary profits. The suggestion
is, therefore, made that the co.’s rights should be
extended ; but a local suggestion, that the co. should be
bought out now and the sinking-fund spread over a
larger number of years, is likely to be more favored. The
co. isn’t at all likely to be given rating powers ; but if the
works were made public property those powers would be
the very first thing secured.

B.H. Water Supply is in trouble again. The
new filters, which have run into many thousands, have
completely failed to purify the water after the recent
rains, and, for a fortnight, Broken Hill has been drinking
beer-colored liquor. And to many in that droughty part
the color without the taste is maddening.

Cobar notes.— Cobar Gold Mines very leisurely
approaches the time for its big start. In a few weeks its
term of exemption from labor conditions will have ex-
pired, and then something really life-like must be done or
workmen set to kill time. The warden has declared that
he will grant no further exemption. The Chesney still
very quiet. Someone must be receiving a thumping
screw to mop up the £5OO a month which is said to be
spent—and, after all, what is £5OO a month in so big a
mine ? The Great Western has a faint hope of passing
into new hands. The mine is good, but has been sadly
butchered, and a new management would need a few
thousands to spare to overcome difficulties created by the
present board of directors.

• * * •
A miner writes to The Bulletin from Clermont
Queensland “ When I arrived here last year, and started
to work at timber-cutting in the bush, I was surprised
to find what I took for bike-tracks all over the place.
They turned out to be the tracks of the jigger-man’s
wheelbarrow. Nearly every old fossicker here owns a
dog, a wheelbarrow and a dry jigger. He starts out in the
morning, fixes his jigger, and puts through a load of dirt •
sometimes he does not obtain a color. Then he shiftsaway
and sets up again in a fresh place, very likely with the
same result. Often they trundle their barrows 10
to 12 miles per day, setting up in three or
four places during the day; they keep this up
for weeks without finding anything, when they may drop
upon a patch which will keep them going for another few
weeks. The majority of the fossickers here have been on
Clermont diggings for over a quarter of a century, and
have very old-fashioned notions re the distribution of the
gold. They imagine that there are no payable leads in
the cement, despite the fact that nearly all the recently-
discovered leads were found in it. All the old rich
leads that have been worked on this field were
found in soft alluvial formed by the disintegration of
the cement, and its concentration in recently-formed
gullies. The cement fills up old deep watercourses
which run in opposite directions to the present gullies
and sometimes through made hills, but there are no
surface indications to assist the prospector to sink, there-
fore there has been very little prospecting done in the
cement. The Clermont goldfield has, therefore, great
possibilities before it yet—there are four or five hundred
sq. miles of cement country not yet prospected—and it
Appears to me to be the source of all the alluvial gold that
has been won so far. The country rock is slate, permeated
with unmineralised white quartz leaders, containing no
gold. There are a few payable reefs at Copperfield and
McDonald Flat, but that is only a small portion of the
Clermont goldfield. The so-called Venus reef is simply a
dyke carrying gold in a big formation of lode stuff, without
sign of quartz. Writer never saw anything of the kind
before. It has never been properly worked, and is worth
a fair trial. An old man has been working it with a pestle
and mortar for .years and making a living out of it, but
his tailings are as coarse as pumpkin-seeds.

• * * •
Tin was first found in Queensland in 1872. In
’73 the metal was produced to the value of £606,184. In
’97, value of tin raised was only £37,509. Returns for ’9B
not yet accessible. As the price of tin recently rose to
£lO9 per ton, there should be better times ahead for the
industry. According to Geologist Skertchley, the future
of the Montalbion and Herberton districts must of neces-
sity depend on tin rather than copper. According to the
same authority, until the discovery of the Australian tin-
fields not a single tin-discovery of note had taken place
since the Christian era. Cornwall was worked in the time
of the Romans; the Straits Settlements’ mines can be
traced back to the ninth century, and were supposed to
be very old then.

Part of the S. A. Govt, policy is the erection of
smelters in the far north. But the Govt, regards as still
more urgent the provision of concentrating plants, which
will, accordingly, be undertaken in various places. It is
proposed to make them suitable for handling either cop-
per or silver. Two batteries and cyanide plants are
already running—one at Petersburgthe other in the
Mac Donnell Ranges, Central Australia.

The erection of the sulphide plant at the Aus-
tralia Mine, Kalgoorlie, is nearing completion. The
crushing is to be done by means of four sets of 36" x 16"
Roger Rolls, having a combined capacity of 500 tons per
day, and the whole of the product will subsequently be
fed into four Ropp roasting furnaces. The rolls are slow
speed, and the first of the size manufactured in Aus-
tralasia. The furnaces are well-known, being in use at
Broken Hill and Mount Morgan.
* » * *
Westralia hasn’t many kicks to spare for any-
thing just now, but one of those that it has left is being
given to some proffered mine-tribute conditions. Tribut-
ing is a pretty poor game at the best. Except here and
there it is sweating written another way; and when it
isn’t that, it is often landlordism in a bad form. Up on the
Murchison thingshave about reached their worst. The Cue
Consolidated (English, of course), which owns a big group
of mines and alleged ditto, offers any of them on conditions
that the trihutor puts down the main shaft 10ft. a month,
and does 10ft. of development work for every 100
tons of payable ore broken. To the man who
has to depend on wages this is dust and ashes.
Companies that have to sit back and get their
main workings made on conditions like these are ripe
for liquidation ; and the mining industry would be
the better for the change of ownership of leases which
that would involve. What it means is that the co. can’t
make a “do” of the mines itself; but it hangs on, and
draws rent from somebody who has either to take them
on the co.’s terms or go dry-blowing—and still stands in
to reap the richest harvest in the end.
Of course, the C.C. being Hinglish, the superior
local person is saying that if miners don’t accept offers
like this they deserve to go dry-blowing. “ The condi-
tions do not meet with the approval of those who think
that tributors should take up a mine, extract the ore
opened up by the co., and then leave.” What happens,
as a matter of fact, is that the co. takes out all the ore
that it can while paying day-wages, and, when the stuff
gets so poor that it can’t make any profit that way, it
turns-on the tributor. He doesn’t get wages ; he has the
privilege of taking out what it doesn’t pay the co. to
handle “ on its own,” and pays a percentage for the privi-
lege of doing so. Also the co. always stands to have
something better exposed in its mine than the unprofit-
able face upon which the tributor starts.

T h6 , Anstral Otis Engineering Co. Ltd., South
Melb., has completed and delivered a very fine set of
winding engines for the Oobar Gold Mines, N.S.W., capable
a load , of two tons from 1500 ft. at a speed of
1000 ft. per minute. The engines are a pair of 14" cylin-
ders, geared to 7ft. loose drum, and are fitted with'link
motion reversing gear, vertical indicators, and powerful
Drake gear. The pair of engines are set to one side of the
arums, and all the driving levers are brought behind the
drums. The co. has also just delivered a fine set of
P u m p!n F plant for the Paddy Lackey mine for taking the
shaft down to 1000 ft.

Investors in Tas. mines are just beginning to
r c?bse the value of ex-Tas. Govt.-Geologist Thureau’s
official reports. Nearly all his prognostications, con-
sidered far too sanguine in the eighties, have since been
fully realised. Despite these facts, the Tas. Govt, has
done nothing in the way of making' these valuable reports
available to present-day mining folks ; only one copy of
the most precious report is in Govt, possession, and en-
quirers are charged so much for a peep, or so much per
copied folio. It was formerly a shilling pamphlet. The
clever old geologist is now living in Melbourne.

Mildura, the Vic. irrigation settlement
which was established on no-drink principles, and
whose exports consist mainly of dried apricots,
deputations and empty beer-bottles, has had
another municipal vote on the great pub. question.
Nearly five years ago this question was answered
by 283 votes for the Gothenburg system against
77 for ordinary pub. licenses and 9 for total pro-
hibition ; but the settlers were then too busy
chasing the people who had undertaken to supply
them with water and weren’t doing it to trouble
much about other liquor. Now, however, the
channels are occasionally full, and the considera-
tion of bulk beer is again possible. The vote
which has resulted shows almost as strong a feel-
ing as before in favor of municipal pub.-keeping ;
and the Vie. Govt, is to be petitioned accordingly.
Renmark, Mildura’s twin irrigation settlement,
being within the jurisdiction of the more-experi-

she will to the jug."
mentally-inclined S. A. Govt., has had its munici-
pal pub. running for just two years. The profit
has reached £693 14s. ; and it is significant that,
though the pub. has, of course, a monopoly of the
trade, the bar business has not increased at all.
Last year the hotel committee (an elective body)
wiped off £2OB liabilities and increased the assets
by £l4O. Eventually, profits will go towards
brightening the local intellect via reading-room,
&c.

Gov. Brassey actually fell off at tennis in
Geelong (Vic.), the other day. He made a grace-
ful swoop at a ball and rebounded on his vice-regal
neck, and the newspapers reported the sad happen-
ing with bated breath. As an obstinate and per-
sistent faller-off, Brassey has no living equal. He
has been falling off ever since he came to Aus-
tralia, and this paper believes that if H.E. were
strapped down on the broad of his back in the
dead middle and centre of the Old Man Plain,
he would contrive to fall off within an hour. He
has deservedly earned the title of the Fallen Man.
a a a. *
When the jury found Kelly, arraigned for
the murder of Maria Flynn, guilty of man-
slaughter only, he devoutly made the sign of the
cross in the dock. Kelly was probably praying
for the soul of his saviour, Briggs, whose hanging
on the previous day for an offence pretty well on
all-fours with his own so impressed the jurors with
their personal responsibility that they shrank from
finding a logical verdict, i.e. , assuming that the
Briggs verdict was logical. Briggs clearly paid
for both. And who knows that Mr. Kelly was
not also saying a little prayer for John Norton,
whose fiery denunciations of murdered Maria
Flynn’s other friend, Mr. Pat Carey, raised among
a large section of the Sydney community a feeling
of sympathy for the deluded prisoner in the dock ?

Rev. “heretic” Ferguson is going to be
theologically snuffed out by Melb. Presbytery,
which is about the most desirable thing that could
happen to such a very half-and-half sort of
Thinker. The orthodox committee have reported
on Ferguson’s little book, and the report, ridicul-
ous as it is in its own conceits concerning Terewtb,
puts the wishy-washy rebel beyond the pale of
rational sympathy. For the preacher who doesn’t
go the whole dog-ma, or none, there is no particu-
lar place in the church (whichever church he may
belong to) or out of it.

“ I don’t like the looks of that gentleman,
yer honor,” said a prisoner recently tried before
Vic. Justice Hodges. “ That gentleman ” was a
bald-headed juror, who accordingly stood down
and sat out the trial, and, when his critic was
found guilty and sentenced, looked as pleased as
if he’d happened on a genuine hair-restorer. The
prisoner in this case was very deaf, and when the
Judge asked—“ Can you hear what the witness is
saying ?” replied, “No, your Honor, but I knoiv
he’s telling a pack of lies ! ”

The man Charlie, or Mahomet Ali, lying
under sentence of death in Wagga, is an example
of the maniacal effects of opium. This is by no
means common, the runners- 11 amok ” being gener-
ally mad from “bhang,” a decoction of hemp.
It is asserted by the advocates of opium-intoxica-
tion that it is a great preventive of crime, the
awful reaction and suffering, when stopped in
gaol, being so well known as to be a deterrent.
The writer knows one opium-eater whose coat-
edges and corners are padded with supplies of
opium-seconds, as a stand-by in case he should be
imprisoned. And after all they would take his
opium-lined suit from him and put him in prison-
livery.
Apropos, in N.S. Wales the interpreter’s trans-
lation of the Judge’s sentence, with its grave
peroration, is “Fhansi hoga ” — i.e., “ There will
be hanging.” Very explicit and curtailed of un-
necessary padding, but not & la Madden or Darley.

* * * *
Coolgardie’s exhibition was a pitiable frost
all through. The City of Tinned Dog was never
large enough for an exhibition, anyhow, and its
glory has faded of late, and the hotel-accommoda-
tion isn’t sufficient for a big exhibition crowd, and
it lacks water to wash a big crowd, and the dust
is gruesome and the scenery repellent. Westralia
has little of its own to exlii bit except models of
nuggets, timber, empty meat-tins, a fevv gaunt,
bony vegetables, sundry tough cows, the inevit-
able wool, some coal, the beer from the breweries
run by eastern capital, and photographs of “in-
dentured ” niggers working in chains. Nobody
from outside cared much about expensively hump-
ing goods to the W.A. interior; it is all very
good to push business when there is a chance of
getting paid for the goods, but W.A. has little to
give in return now except the green and ever-
flourishing bad-debt. Coolgardie municipal coun-
cil raised one arch by way of decoration when the
melancholy, half-empty show started, and the
Afghan camel-drivers shouted another. The rest
was mainly drink, and lonesomeness, and roulette
tables, and Aunt Sallies. The exhibition was
more like a moderate-sized general-store than any-
thing else, and even the Tinned-Dog City hardly
professed to be glad over it, though it rose man-
fully with sixpenn’orth of beer in its right hand
and made speeches wherein the ring of enthusi-
asm sounded like a big door-key falling into a
tomb.
Ooolgardie people now shiver when Exhi-
bition is mentioned. On the eve of the opening,
when Gov. Smith and Premier Forrest were pack-
ing their portmanteaux to give the thing a shove,
there was an unruly meeting to discuss whether
the Mayor of Coolgardie should be allowed £250
“ for the entertainment of distinguished visitors”
while the frost lasted. The proposal was carried,
of course, but only amid general remarks that
they didn’t know where “the stuff” was to come
from. And, anyhow, the spectacle of Coolgardie,
which lately flowed witli fizz, deciding to allow its
Mayor a whole £250 to “ do the honors ’’ is full of
sadness.
But Coolgardie Exhibition has one accessory
novelty, anyhow : a camel - firm (Afghan, of
course) is keeping a string of riding camels in
town ; and the incautious, curious visitor can have
his head jerked off for a shilling or so.

STARS
IN THE
SEA
FOR THE BULLETIN.
I took a boat on a starry 'night
And went for a row on the water,
And she danced like a child on her wake
of light,
And bowed where the ripples caught
her.
I vowed as I rowed on the velvet blue
Through the night and the starry
splendour,
To woo and sue a maiden I knew
Till she bent to my pleadings tender.
My painted boat she was light and glad,
And gladder my heart with wishing,
And I came in time to a little lad
Who stood on the rocks a-fishing.
I said “ Ahoy ! ” and he said “ Ahoy 1 ”
And I asked how the fish were biting—
“ And what are you trying to catch, my boy,
Bream, silver and red —or whiting ? ”
“Neither,” he answered; “ the seaweed mars
My line, and the sharp shells sunder —
I am trying my luck with those great, big stars
Down there in the round skies under.”
Good-bye from him, and good-bye from me.
And never a laugh came after ;
So many go fishing for stars in the sea
That it’s hardly a subject for laughter.
RODERIC QUINN.

The recent murder of a seven-year-old boy
by his mother at Fisherman’s Bend, Melb., recalls
the cold-blooded act for which Emma Williams
was hanged under Gov. Brassey’s first official
signature. The woman had a four-year-old son,
and, deciding that he was superfluous, took him
to Port Melb. lagoon, tied a big stone his
neck by a piece of braid, and tossed him in. That
bit of braid hanged Emma Williams.

Dear Bulle-

dosseries in which men-

know a well-to-do man who is

* * * *
A young man of about 29 told a strange
tale last week in Melb. County Court. He said
that he had been working for a blacksmith for
over 10 years, the agreement being for 15s. a week
with board, lodging, and clothing ; but his em-
ployer never paid him, and always spanked him
in a vigorous, fatherly manner when he asked
for the money. The blacksmith being dead
now, he was suing the executors for 10 years’
wages. About this stage the Judge showed signs
of falling into a state of insensibility, but he held
up with a great effort, and denounced the case as
the most incredible hen-and-cow narrative that
ever wandered into his court. The man who said
he was spanked off and on for ten years was then
shunted out with costs.

“ Dubberkelosity ” is manifestly going to
take root in the language. A St. Kilda (Melb.)
woman was fined £5 last week for placarding her
neighbor’s gate with a notice stigmatising her as a
Dubberkulous character. When two Iydies dis-
agree in Richmond or Oollingwood now the last
epithet hurled before the conversation ends and
the battery begins is “ Dubberke.” Then the next
remark consists of a handful of hair and a shriek
and a wrecked hat.

Sunday takings. The Wesleyan

“T.D.” : Went (for a
glass of ginger-beer) into a
Sydney hotel on Sunday and met
at the bar a plain-clothes officer who
was ministering to a cultured taste in tangle-
foot. Half-an-hour previously he told me that
he was “watching the pubs.”
On inquiry I found that his
mate (they run in couples) was
having a gratis whisky and a
simultaneous flirtation with the
cook in the kitchen. A week
before, those two policemen
had the gall to “fix” a neigh-
boring vendor of soulful beverages
for a fine of £5.
A Richmond (Melb.) boot-manufacturer
has got a dreadful showing-up at the local court.
It was his pleasant little custom to pay his em-
ployes the statutory wage, as enacted under the
Factories Act, at 10 a.m. on Saturdays. These
wages they duly signed for, and immediately
handed back to the manager. Then, at knocking-
off time, about noon, they were paid their
regular wages—considerably under the legal
scale. The employer's lawyer offered to plead
guilty, and wished to be heard in mitigation
of sentence without the evidence being taken.
The local J’sP., for a wonder, went into
the matter carefully, and then sat on this
interesting gentleman to the tune of =£s ss. fine,
with <£s ss. costs, in the two cases listed. It was
a good deal for a J.P. to do, but, as the boot-
manufacturer has probably defrauded his workmen
out of a good many times the amount of the paltry
fine, he is still better off through breaking the law
than he would be if he had kept it.

The selection of N.S.W. District Court
Judge Gibson for an Acting Supreme Court
Judgeship is adversely remarked upon by certain
legal critics, not on personal grounds, but because
such appointments tend to obliterate the line of
demarcation between the two benches. But the
trend of the last decade in N.S.W. is in the direc-
tion of doing this very obliteration, and, it is
held, the distinction between the benches never
should have been so wide in emoluments and
honor as it is. Several D.C. have been Acting
Supreme Court Judges. If fit to be Acting, why
not fit to be permanent ?
This is to give notice that Australian
Governors are not responsible for any psvate
debts contracted by their haydekongs.

“ M‘G.” : How is it that so few artists can

cent are not so careful

In a Northern town, recently, a wife-v.-
husband assault case resulted in sentence of hus-
band to find two sureties for good-behavior of
£25 each or do six months. Wife accordingly
straightens up her battered ear and departs, and
presently returns with one surety, she being the
other herself. Bond fixed up, and hubby goes
back to the bosom of his family. But, alas ! same
old row re-starts, and hubby is now doing his
“ stretch,” and Madame is mulct in <£so and has
to shift for herself.

An inquest was held in Melbourne the
other day on a baby that had been literally worried
to death by rats. The mother left the child on a
sofa in a house in Richmond, and, on entering the
room later, scared the vermin from their horrible
banquet, but not before the baby was so badly
bitten that death from loss of blood resulted. The
locality is described as a fearful one for rats. A
fine baby-farming district.

TO ONE SLAIN IN ABSENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And so we parted, love, oblivious
That we were parting ! With our laughter light
Flaunting the future—on the morrow bright
At our old tryst we would again discuss
The wonder of our love miraculous,
While with us Fate stood waiting, swift to smite!
And you have gone, large-eyed, across the Night;
And I wait, straining widowed arm 3 ! Yet thus
I want your memory—no tears, no pain,
No presage of chill death, nor any fears;
Your calm eyes bridging all eternity
With one firm hope ! Is’t not an augury
That somewhere in the tangle of the years
Your laughter and your lips I’ll find again ?
A. H. A.
A naval man behold in me—
The Sex’s dearest treasure—
Who merely lives to plough the sea
And give the ladies pleasure.
A cup of tea (I like it sweet)
From hands that one would gladly eat,
It makes my briny bosom beat
With rapture beyond measure.
My officers are flighty swains,
You’ll And them sadly fickle—
Whilst true to you this heart remains
Till Death brings round his sickle.
On angry seas I fight the storm,
On shore I squeeze each lovely form,
So, when your teas have made you warm,
O, please command-er-Ticlcell.
