A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, April 10, 1899.
Mr Dear Myek—
Govt. House ball came off last Thursday. Seven
hundred people can’t all develope in the narrow
V.R. ballrooms, and only a few danced, and many
were never seen at all. The girl who “comes
out ” at Govt. House doesn’t get a rosy impression
of the great, gay world. She generally stands up
half the evening, and sits down the other half.
The woman of 30, with her circular glance, is the
one who makes the ball her own. The young girl
in her new white satin, new white shoes, new
white gloves, new white bouquet-strings, and
fatally new unpractised smile, does what George
Moore says—evokes no ideal but that of home;
and, as hoiiie isn’t what dancing-men look for in
ball-rooms, she is left at her mother’s side for the
most part. By the way, Thursday’s prettiest
debutante was Miss Vera le Patourel, whose elder
sister is one of Sydney’s acknowledged beauties.
The frocking was characterised by the polonaise,
and the sequined net. Lady Darley was wearing
black satin embroidered in steel, and her daughters,
in blacks and' greys, were white and wan-looking
after their hard week’s work. “The Chief”
and Mr. B. R. Wise ran a calf-com petition in
which the latter was wofully beaten, but all the
other men in the room wore clothes to their boots.
Mrs. Percy Chirnside glittered in a diamond tiara
and silvered net over sky-blue. Mrs. Willie
Mitchell, whose frocks are always expected to
prophesy fashions, was not there. The Madden
bride, Mrs. Harry Osborne, added to her reputa-
tion of being the largest, serenest, happiest-looking
bride of the day, in white brocade with big white
buttons down the back of the skirt, and diamond
swallows in her hair.
' A travelling celebrity created a sensation
(and much envy) by asking a pretty Syd-
ney woman who can’t breathe in the inner
of inners, and isn’t reckoned to be in society,
to dance with him. He seemed to like his
partner, too. At last, another pretty woman
couldn’t stand it any longer. She moved forward,
and boldly bailed up the celebrity in the doorway.
“ Oh, how do you do ? ” said she, loudly. “ I was
so sorry our dance didn’t come off last night. Now,
which one are we going to have to-night ? ” He
blames his memory, but
The other big hop of the week, St. Vincent’s
ball, was somehow not the crowded dance it
usually is. At nine o’clock the hall looked quite
forlorn, but everybody comes late now, and about
450 folk were there eventually. I made a special
note of one part of the decorations, with a view to
warning anyone not to do it again. The" platform
was covered with drain-pipes, draped in white
stuff, and out of the white grew a gum-tree or a
palm. From the other end of the room it was like
a little cemetery taken out to have some fun—not
a cheerful bit of symbolism for a hospital ball.
There was the usual forinal set of Lancers, when
all the Everybodies, who must be deadly tired of
the other Everybodies, danced with Everybody.
Lady Darley, in black silk and jet and a diamond
star, looked regal beyond any genuine V.R. lady
that has ruled at Sydney, and, as isn’t always the
case, the male element further underlines the
aristocratic suggestions. Lord Hampden used to
go through these opening festivities with a weary
smile on one side of his mouth as though he knew
something about Sydney people that he would
never, Never tell ; but the fine, massive, Roman-
father-looking Acting-Governor Darley beams
graciously through the advance and retreat, and
sets to partners with a 1 vely combination of ducal
dignity and angelic smile.
As a dance, the ball was entirely successful.
The usual series of hungry-eyed women in pink
satin (there seems to be an idea that pink satin
will yet do something for the dear, good women
who are never asked to dance) looking wistfully
for a man with the magic query, “Are you en-
gaged for this ?” were not to be seen. Men rushed
about imploring dances, and this sort of thing lent
point and brilliancy to the smart frocks present.
Miss Slattery’s deep rose-pink silk was a gown
that could not be overlooked. It was made with
a polonaise that revealed pink-chiffon petticoats,
and a deeper-rose-pink velvet lifting up the cor-
sage. Fair-haired Mrs. Dicken showed that
blondes also can wear cerise. Her frock sounds
patchy on paper, but was striking in the real—a
pink glace polonaise edged with black chiffon-
ruches, over a striped black-and-pink petticoat,
and a white chiffon and black chenille bodice with
pink zouaves. A large lady in a black skirt and a
little white sleeveless coat wasn’t a happy
picture, neither was a pair of red silk gloves on
the arms of a girl in burning red with a coffin out-
lined in white lace on the skirt. These decora-
tions are a mockery when nobody restrains them.
The hon. secs., Mrs. Mullins and Mrs. Kenna,
worked hard in blue brocade and pink roses, and
in a white frilly frock with a touch of red velvet —
of all combinations the most becoming. Mrs.
Slattery, hon. treasurer, wore a grey poplin polo-
naise over pink underskirt. Mrs. Lovely sported
the only pair of red shoes present—under a plain
black net frock, which consisted chiefly of dia-
monds. Miss Ethel Inglis was in white silk with
a blue shoulder-bow.
The Broughs had another great house on Satur-
day, vice-regal command night. Potts Point and
country “Easter Eggs” alike finished up their
Easter revelries at “ Sans-Gene. ” The house was
even more brilliant than on opening night. In the
front row of the Governor’s box sat Sir Frederick
and Lady Darley, with a bright red figure—that
of Lady Ranfuriy in scarlet silk—between them.
Behind them were the Misses Darley, big, red-
faoed Harry Darley, a quiet-looking woman with
a charming smile, Captain Feil den, and a Ranfurly
aide with a blue collar. As for the circle, from
front row to back it was a continual succession of
“parties,” and it was easy to see that flowers have
come to stay in the hair.
Naturally, the piece went briskly and inspiredly,
with loud applause and many flowers. Mrs.
Brough was warmly worked-up, but had got over
the nervous exuberance of her opening night. How
charming she looks in that adorable brown “Em-
pire ” with bright green sleeves and bright green
zouave ! The worst of Mrs. Brough is that when
a house laughs and applauds, she does a little more.
And if they still laugh, she does a little more—
which is too much. For instance, in the tram
scene. That went “ big ” on Saturday, but even
a washerwoman would have to practise very hard
before she could tumble over her skirts so persist-
ently. Mrs. Brough’s other snappy frock is a
white satin that allows her to draw up her figure
to perfection. Long lines of gold embroideries
run all over the front of this creation,
which is cut a good way down to reveal a
shapely back. All through the play she
carries a little rolled-up ball of a handkerchief
which she waves by way of finis to a stormy scene,
dabs against her face when sentiments have been
flying round, and sticks to generally as if she
could not get on without it.
Mr. Lovell’s reputation as a handsome man
having arrived before him has probably injured
him seriously. He is tall, thin, dark, and a
striking replica of Cyril Keightly, the rather stiff
young Australian who played Mario to Potter’s
last performance of Tosca. Strangely enough,
Lovell has the same slightly-impeded speech, as
though some of his words were forced down his
throat instead of coming out through his lips. It
is only at intervals that you can hear without
straining, and this somewhat detracts from his
merits as an actor.
Pattie Browne’s benefit ran along on oiled
wheels last Thursday. An enormous house
gathered early—over £3OO worth of people—
and Pattie capped a brightly variegated pro-
gramme with a smarter “ few, words ” than the
usual ebullition of the overcome benefitted one.
Perhaps the speech was a trifle too comprehensive
for an impromptu effort, but it was delivered with
such happy archness that one forgave. In a high-
waisted empire frock, with a red wig and gold
sash ribbons, looking a little thinner after her ill-
ness, she gently buttered up the Firm, patted
“Goodie” metaphorically on the back, rubbed
some mild grease over Harry Rickards and his co.,
poured a whole bottle of oil on Charles Holloway
—a brother Australian —promised the house that
she would win more and more London laurels for
dear Australia., and finally piled words on words
in praise of the generosity of her country’s press.
Good Pattie ! There wasn’t an ounce of spleen in
the whole of the composition, and there might
have been reason for a little.
Said Pattie : “When Mr. Harry Rickards was
asked if he would assist at my benefit, he ex-
claimed, ‘ What, Pattie 1 Why, if it’s Pattie she
can have my theatre, my company, my home, my
wife, my children, myself /” Rumor has been
whispering for a long time of the ructions between
Harry Pattie all through the latter’s season,
and this little dished-up speech may stop gossip.
It was said that Pattie blamed Rickards’ manage-
ment for her bad season. But there can’t be much
ill-blood where a man offers to give himself in
with his family.
The programme rattled along without a hitch.
Charles Kenningham, looking like Launcelot of
1899 in round cheeks and eyes, and a long-tailed
coat, shed on a ballad the head notes that always
smite the house dumb with admiration. A clever
little prodigy, named Fanny Powers, relieved one’s
mind by singing a childish little song about dolls
instead of drunken men. Lottie Moore’s delight-
ful somersault showed a Frenchy black skirt with
big yellow flowers on it whirling away from legs
crossed with yellow ribbons. Pattie gave all too
little of herself in the brief, bright letter-scene
from the “ Country Girl. ” Walter Bentley brought
all his art and all his nature to bear on “Eugene
Aram, ” but the world is passing on, and leaving
Eugene behind it.
The general opinion concerning the Passion
Play exhibition of pictures at the Town Hall last
Friday was that there was tqo much Bentley and
not enough Passion Play about. True art in a
performance of the kind consists in leading the
audience to forget the personality of the lecturer.
The unstinted applause that greeted the actor’s
ranting delivery of some of the most sacred pas-
sages of Scripture was a trifle disconcerting, even
to many persons not over-reverent; and several
devout people arose and left the building, so com-
pletely shocked were they at such an exhibition of
bad taste. A rather weird effect was achieved by
the sipging, in almost complete darkness, of more
or less relevant songs.
Much sympathy is felt with Mr. Leonard Gib-
son, of this city, whose popular and much-admired
wife died so suddenly up North the other day.
Mrs. Gibson was a daughter of the late Mr. Far-
quhar, of Mort’s Dock, and used to be known as
the handsome Miss Tempe Farquhar. Strangely
enough, Mr. Farquhar died suddenly some years
ago from a chill contracted in a freezing-room,
while Mrs. Gibson died while bathing in a very
cold water-hole, supposedly from heart-failure.
She had gone North on a visit to her sister.
Captain Sloan-Stanley, late aide to the Hamp-
dens, lias gone from the battle with the Fat
Butchers’ Wives to active service in Africa. Lord
Beauchamp, who is bringing out three aides and
a secretary, may regret that Capt. Fielden is not
to stay on. This aide’s colonial experience and
nice, neat melancholy would be a ready-made
fence for keeping baok people whose friends and
relatives are not kind enough to restrain them. To
be able to gaze sadly at an infuriated hash-house
Eresidentess, asking “ ’Ow is it I’ve been left
out o’ the ball?” is really a great gift for a
diplomat.
The last, though not the youngest, of the late
Dr. Leibius’ daughters was married last week.
The bride was pretty, auburn-haired Miss Julie
Leibius, and the bridegroom Mr. Reginald Heber
Bode, second son of the late Rev. C. Bode. The
ceremony, which took place on March 29 at
Gladesville, was rather quietly performed.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The assistant staff at St. Vincent’s Hospital
fair is less motley than most gatherings of bazaar
banditti. Instead of fancy-dress hotch-potch, all
wear gay gowns with white caps and aprons.
Each group affects frocks of the same color and its
own special cap-shape, the latter in some cases an
idealised, picturesque version of one or another
conventional head-dress.
“T-he second Mrs. Wesley-Hall, at Toorak
stall, wore on Easter Monday night black brocade
skirt, boldly-blended black-and-white blouse,
cream ostrich boa, and black and grey metallic
millinery. Mesdames Edward Miller, Robert
Power, Nicholas Fitzgerald, and surgeon Fitz-
gerald’s daughter all turned an honest penny at
the same booth.
“As usual, their R.C. reverences have the
father of a good time at the fair. Colleens
literally mobbed them the first night or two,
especially crisp young curates, with a killing
glance and a soft answer to turn away the wrath
of every baffled bargainer in petticoats within
blarneying distance. One such broth of a bhoy
was tall, fair, and curly, with a divil of a dimple ;
another short and swarthy, with comic nose and
twinkling black eye.
“The male Paddy flirts from the cradle to the
frave. His womankind, on the contrary, are
rought up demurely, set a high value on them-
selves, and frequently get left. The old man
declares, in expansive moments, he’d rather see
‘me daughter in her grave, than running after
any starveling in breeches. ’
“Miss He'pner, youngest and comeliest of the
new faces in ‘Belle of New York,’appeals more
forcibly to the front rows of the stalls than to
friends at a distance. Hers is the cosy, comfort-
ing type of young womanhood. Take an inch or
two off her height and she would pass for one of
the bunch of Chief-Justice Madden’s daughters.
“ ‘Belle ’ Louise Hepner is an acquired taste,
evidently, and catches on more and more,
especially with the sex which relishes down-cast
eyes and up-raised ankles—mixed drinks, so to
speak. En passant , the charmer in question told
a local press-reporter that her boofy red-brown
plait is shop-hair, bought ‘ at a store on Broad-
way.’ Among the throng at a recent ‘ Belle ’
matinee was Mrs. Bland Holt (in smart tailor suit
and red hat) with Hubby and Miss Lucy Coppin.
“ But the chief beauties of ‘ The Belle
of New York ’ are in the choruses. But Natalie
Olcott and Lily Cooley, Yankee chorus girls,
are nice to look at through opera - glasses.
They are good-sized girls, turned in a good lathe,
and perfect models, whether from the painter’s or
the sculptor’s point of view.
“ As for ‘ Wine and Women, ’ the latter, with all
their faults, are dressy specimens. Mrs. Bland
Holt returns from her honeymoon with her Eton-
jacketed bridegroom in yellow muslin, hooped from
neck to hem with diagonal circles of black lace
quillings. She sees the Longchamps steeplechase
in white satin, sleeved and chemisetted with satin-
striped gauze, the apron and corsage outlined with
silver pendants. Another gown has emerald satin
bodice, jewelled-chiffon sleeves and gauze-veiled
skirt. Elizabeth Watson, as an extra-naughty
‘ (lay Parisienne,’ is smartest at the races in one of
the new black velvet ‘ swallow-tail ’ coats, with
scoop cuffs dipping to the knuckles of her white
gloves, plain, clinging, magpie-striped silk skirt,
silver beaded black stick, etc. Frances Ross, with
innocent curls a-hanging down her back, hat rib-
bons tied under chin, roses nestling in belt, etc.,
hugger-muggers an old, blind man till he can’t
call his soul his own, kissing his hand, stroking
his thin grey hair, and shooting him out of
draughts.
“ Had the Town Hall been half as large again,
it would probably still have been a tight fit for
the Amy Castles concert crush. As it was, many
reserved seats were jumped and held on to, and
many would-be hearers took their money back and
went disappointed away. Throughout the general
fizz and fuss the blithe young beneficiare kept a
stiff upper lip, looking very winsome as she came
forward for her first plunge, simply frocked in
white, her dark hair framing her flushed cheeks,
her eye steely-bright with suppressed excitement.
Of course ‘ she’s but a lassie ! ’ —no finished singer.
On Saturday night, the audience would have en-
joyed itself better and sampled the voice under
review just as well, had the heroine of the occasion
been heard in less ambitious music.
“ Nevertheless the gifted young thing received
a regular ovation, got piles of flowers, and alto-
gether made one of those slap-dash debuts com-
moner in books than in real life.
“At that concert I witnessed a Horrible
Calamity. A matron, whose chair had been
pushed forward into a lone, lorn, prominent posi-
tion, little knew, poor thing, that a white under-
garment, badly-torn too, worse luck ! had worked
up at the back, above her decollete corsage. Girls
sat behind her and giggled, and behind them
again, drinking in their jokes, writhed their butt’s
hubby, purple to his hair-roots, as visible on the
thinnish patches.
“Wallace Brownlow, who helped with the pro-
gramme, is still large and fair, though his English
roses, alas, have wilted by the way. Daughters
of Israel, in Saturday’s audience, reminisce regret-
fully of his beauty in ‘Ma Mie Rosette. ’ Miss
Leonora Bailey, in black ; white-gowned Meta
Blaubaum : and clever Zelinan, junior, with his
violin, were the other performers.
“Evidently His Ex. the Governor has quite
enough brains to keep him out of the wet.
When the officers’ mess tent at Langwarrin en-
campment sprang sundry small leaks m the roof,
Lord Brassey rose —or rather, he went down—to
the occasion by crawling under the table for
shelter. He didn’t stay there long enough to feel
lonely, the story says, and he came back into the
open looking all the better for his trip. For a
Governor who wanted to make sure of being dry
there could be no safer place than underneath the
table ; besides, it gave the distinguished guest a
chance to assure himself that none of the ladies
present had got their little feet damp.
“Melb. University has lost one of its oldest
identities. John Selby, who acted as assistant to
the anatomical lecturers and manager of the de-
funct department for the past 14 years, is returning
to his native London. Previous to his University
‘appointment,’ as the fruity-voiced old character
would call it, Selby put in eight years of attend-
ance upon surgeons at the operating-theatre of
Melb. Hospital, and he goes back ’Ome with a
decent bit o’ money in his pocket, feeling very
much like a fully-qualified scientist, or words to
that effect. Although he can remember the time
when there were not more than 20 medical students
at the University, and lady doctors, bless your
’art, wasn’t thought of, Selby is not an ardent
Tory. He reckons that the gals are ahead of the
young fellers—’cos why ? The young fellers is
mostly thinking about crick-et and /oot-ball,
where-as the gals goes to the University to work.
That’s about the size of it, sez John Selby, and
when his friends in London ask him what he thinks
of wimmin for doctors, he will intimate that the
wimmin are all right as far as they’ve gorne.
“ Marshall Hall was badly broken up about the
death of his friend, clever Ernest Moffitt. He
would not leave the cemetery after the funeral,
but asked to be left alone there, and stayed for an
hour or more pacing beside the grave. Hall is a
good man in a fight—a hard-hitting enemy—but
he is a firm friend. Such friendships as lie has
made among men of his set in Melbourne one
hears of in books, but very rarely encounters in
actual life.
“A sad circumstance in connection with that
Port Phillip yachting disaster was that two
daughters of the family, who were on a holiday at
Xmas, drove on Thursday morning to the railway
station to meet two of their brothers who were to
have come up—to receive instead a telegram
announcing the death of father and f.our
brothers.
“Melbourne’s dozen Chinese she-missionaries
are berthed in a>big terrace-house in Gore-
street, Fitzroy. On Sundays and sundry week-
evenings the twelve tall, pale, anaemic-looking
virgins file out of Gore-street and walk slowly and
smilelessly to Lilly Buk-street, clad in blue
dresses, with white collars and cuffs, and Dub-
berke hats with blue and white ribbons, and
turned out altogether in a manner which would be
fetching with any other sort of girls. They are
nearly all young, but at first glance look 40 at
least, and nearly all have figures strikingly mas-
culine.
“Be the mention of Prof. Marshall-Hall rubbing
shoulders with the black - shawled Sisters of
Charity, in the noble cause of charity-concerts,
many people will be staggered to hear that Holy
Church and the Chair of Music are on the best of
terms. The other Sunday night, at one of Dr.
Tom Bride’s literary and musical soirees at Kew,
the loose-lipped lyrist sat cheek by jowl to
Jesuit Father Ryan. The two poles represented
by the agnostic baton-beater and the shriver of
souls met in, no—on —harmony. They talked
music till the Prof, forgot to be sarcastic.
“ That handsome divorce has returned from the
torrid North to his old haunts at' Toorak. At
first the Naicest girls fought a little shy of their
former favorite, but they soon forgot their small
fictions about - ‘ feeling so awfully awkward, don’t-
cherknow. ’ The culprit comes of a good-looking
stock, and is closely related to one of the oldest
and staidest Tasmanian elans. A junior relative
of above grass-widower is also pleasing to look
upon, and confesses to- a chequered past. Upon
one occasion, at the height of his frisks, he was
converted by a visiting evangelist, and repented
ostentatiously for quite a long time.
Y ours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.

GOOD HEALTH WITHOUT DRUGS.
4.—Mothers and Children.
Mothers who would keep their children in good
health should give them, morning and evening,
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Ctiooa made with hot milk. Deli ■
cate men and women who have weak lungs, to be
hale, robust and healthy, should use Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Co.Goa morning and evening, and all men who
have to be exposed to the bleak uncertainty of our
trying climate should fortify themselves before
they face their daily toil with Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-
Cocoa, arid, then they can brave the fury of the
elements with equanimity. The writer speaks
from personal experience and from observation of
beneficial effects on others. Tea opens the pores
and temporarily excites, coffee stimulates the
action of the heart, whilst Dr. Tibbies’Vi-Cocoa
gives strength, stamina, and builds up arid
strengthens the lung tissues. It is indeed a
wonderful food beverage. Nothing has ever been
discovered that can approach it-in giving lightness
of heart, joy of life, fleetness of foot, And that
general feeling of comfort which only comes from
a full capacity to ‘enjoy every pleasure, moral,
intellectual and physical.

SOME SYDNEY AGRICULTURAL SHOW SKETCHES.
Nigger-flogger Brockman (struck off the
Westralian «T.P. list since his conviction) is a scion
of perhaps the oldest of the Six Families. The
founder of the Westralian branch, like the
ancestor of the Hammersley clan, was a cadet of
an Oxfordshire family. In the sister province’s
early days, Brockmans, Hammersleys, and Le-
froys were more or less intermarried.
Byrne, of Melb. Age, whose book on the
fossil “ Australian writers ’’ (he has not heard of
the new men at all) fell flat a couple of years ago,
is returning to London. He was for some years
in the Age s London office, and his soul pants for
more smoke and fog. Syme made him tempting
offers to stay in Melbourne, but, being a bachelor
with means and aspirations, Byrne made up his
mind to go.
Percy Spence seems to have as much work
in. London as he can find time to do. A very good
full-page drawing of his appears in a recent Black
and White. Harper’s Magazine has just re-
ceived delivery of a commission which it
recently entrusted to Spence, and has ordered
more. Besides this, Spence has executed a good
commission in illustrating W. E. Norris’ new
serial story for the Gentlewoman.

Victoria has just lost William Fisher, aged

Nearly 21 years ago Julius Yogel made an
abortive attempt to enter the House of Commons
whde still Agent-General for Maoriland. He
selected the seaport town of Falmouth as a likely
seat, and, to propitiate the electors, endeavored to
induce the M.L. Govt, to fix on that town as the
depot for departing immigrants, but without
success.
A Q. Ministerial candidate, with a reputa-
tion for wit, was told by a loud-mouthed Labor
barracker at the recent elections to “go to hell! ”
In response, the candidate remarked that he had
travelled in many parts of the world, and had
been courteously received everywhere, but this
was the first time he had ever been invited to the
headquarters of the Labor-party.
Of Premier Reid’s original batch of ten
nominees to N.S.W. Leg. Council Dan O’Connor
and S. E. Lees resigned to contest Assembly
electorates at the general election. Daniel dis-
appeared at Pyrmont, but Lees regained his seat
at Nepean. Of the remaining eight, John Hughes,
Dr. Cullen, Ed. Pulsford and Alex. Kethel
supported the Federal Enabling Bill; Jago Smith
and “Financier” Backhouse opposed it; and J.
Blanksby and S. H. Hyam were absent.
Rev. Dr. Bruce, travelling organiser of
N.S.W. Presbyterians, longheadedly instils as he
goes a new view on the ever-absorbing stipend
question. He urges house-fathers not to pay
church dues in their own names alone, but to
divide the sum into parts, and get each member
of the family to pay his or her portion. The
children, this philosophical stipend-raiser argues,
will thus acquire a habit of giving which will stick
to them when they earn money of their own.
“Jaeger”: I want to know this, if ever

S 3 the strings that make the Dibbsian figures

in the Australian XI. so as to keep his mouth
shut? To the Australian XI. Federation is always

“ C.M.G.” : David Syme is credited with
many fine qualities as a boss and as the head
engineer of a “ great daily,” but he certainly lacks
one happy knack—the ability to keep his good
men. He can find them, but he fails to hold
them. This reflection applies to the reporting-
staff. Of the staff David had seven years ago
only one is now left. The Argus succeeds better;
it makes things comfortable for providers of good
stuff, and has always one or two of the best avail-
able when D.S. is chasing round for a young man
who is superior to the bald work of telling about
the fire that broke out or the horse that bolted.
Just when the announcement was made
that Hallam Tennyson had been appointed S.A.
Governor because he happened to be his father’s
son, N.S.W. Govt. Gazette notified that a son of
the famous Charles Dickens had become lessee of
a far way-back Govt, tank, with surrounding
block. But in the latter ease it was, probably,
because Edward Bulwer Lytton Dickens offered
the highest price. Dickens, by the way, seems to
have given up all thought of re-entering politios
(it was the awful Sleath who ousted him from his
Wilcannia seat), and seems content to live out an
ancient jackeroo’s life among the far-western
mulga.
Mr. Forbes Johnston to The Bulletin
from Lower Hutt, Wellington, Maoriland
Two years ago I had a narrow escape from being killed
by a stag (red-deer) near Wairarapa Lake (M.L.), and an
account of the affair, having gone the rounds of the
papers, was ridiculed by Tub Bulletin as an exaggera-
tion, if,not an absolute concoction. When told of this,
I merely said that the brute would perhaps vindicate me
by killing somebody thereafter ; and, as the same stag
has now gored a Maori woman to death, perhaps you
will print this as collateral evidence of my truthfulness.
The red-deer stag, when solitary, is an extremely vicious
and dangerous animal, and lives have frequently been
lost in this way.

Wednesday. He was considerably over 100 years old and
bad been present at the engagements of Kaitake, Ota-
paoa, Te Ngntu-o-te-Manu, Pokaikai, Papatihakehake,

Q. Inclependent-Opposition leader Drake
displays, in his paper (Progress) a strenuousness
which he might advantageously emulate on the
platform. He thus answers some personal refer-
ences of the local Courier :
We have no intention, however, at present, by adopting
a policy of retaliation, to lower ourselves to the level of
the Courier. Our aim is rather to raise the tone of the
Courier to our own standard. Nor should we wish just
at this period to saddle the editor of the Courier with
the responsibility of the contents of that journal. We
have a considerable amount of respect for that gentle*
man, and' we prefer to think that most of the Courier’s
work during the last three weeks has been performed by
a hireling, engaged on the same terms as Shakespeare’s
“ First and Second Murderers i.e., to do some nasty,
disreputable work for cash payment on the understand-
ing that the agents will be promptly disowned as soon as
the job is done.
“ Saw Count Esterhazy, the triple traitor
of the Dreyfus drama, to-night,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, “and he looks
it. Might easily be labelled as a better class of
souteneur. Piercing black eyes in dissipated
leaden frames, shapely aquiline nose, heavy black
moustache spraying at the ends, a growing beard,
and a blank bald head in a horseshoe of sparse be-
spattered grey hair. Rounded back, covered by a
tightly-buttoned frock coat, uncertain spindly legs,
thin hips and no chest, are the bodily attributes of
this candidate for the Paris throne. And he says
he’ll get there if Dreyfus is again condem-ned. If
not, he will expose the generals. It is to be
hoped, however, he will on no account expose
himself—mentally’or morally.”

“Clod" : Apropos John Farrell re John
Mitchel. Many Bulletin readers may be inter-
ested to know that when the ship in which
Mitchel escaped from Tasmania put into Sydney
harbor it became known to a small number of
Mitchel’s sympathisers in the city that be was on
board, and they had him brought ashore and
entertained at a convenient place, amongst the
movers in the matter being D. H. Deniehy and
W. B. Dailey. Deniehy (present writer’s infor-
mant) said they spent a glorious night, and pru-
dently did not prolong the festivity too far into
next morning. On breaking up the whole party
escorted their guest to where, at Woolloomooloo,
a boat awaited him. The moment Mitchel was
aboard the ship weighed anchor and departed for
the land of the Stars and Stripes. I asked
Deniehy if he did not think it a rash thing to do,
and he assured me that several officials high in
authority were perfectly cognisant of what was
taking place.
Apropos recent cables announcing Signor
Marconi's wonderful success in wireless tele-
graphy. “ A.W.8.” writes to The Bulletin :
As there’s nothing new under the sun one need not be
surprised at the announcement in a Maoriiand paper that
a local savant had long ago succeeded in telegraphing
without wires. It seems probable, however, that if Vic-
toria had possessed a master mind some years back Signor
Marconi would have been forestalled, and the honor of mak-
ing such an important discovery been Australia’s. Nearly
ten years ago a boat, bearing a “ live ” torpedo, was pro-
ceeding down Port Phillip, and when a considerable dis-
tance off shore it most unaccountably exploded. Several
men were killed and injured, and at the subsequent in-
quest the officials could only explain the accident
by insisting that some one had touched one of the
electric connections or meddled with the battery on shore.
Abundant evidence was forthcoming almost proving the
impossibility of this finding, but the authorities could
come to no other conclusion. The late Cosmo Newbery
hazarded the conjecture that an electric current, carried
by an unknown agency, and transmitted through the air,
had passed from the shore to the torpedo ; but the
authorities, pointing out the distance of the boat, the
turbulent waters between, and other circumstances
“ fatal ”to the idea, laughed it out of court. Abetter
knowledge of the “ wave-length theory,” and the proper-
ties of the ether, a less busy Govt, official, or the presence
of a truly scientific mind, and wireless telegraphy would
have perhaps been a common thing enough to-day. Not
by any means an isolated case of a tremendous scientific
fact trembling in the balance of discovery.
A man writes from way-back concerning
N.S.W.’s biggest pastoralist, just appointed a
N.'S.W. Leg. Councillor
After all is said and done, Sam. M‘Caughey is probably
rieher than was Tyson. He was born at Ballymena, Ire-
land, about 60 years ago. He owns Toorale, Dunlop,
Goonong (his residence), Goolgumbla, Byalla, Singorimba,
and North Yanko, and is partner in Yarrabree with his
brother John. Has also a lot of money in Riverina pro-
perties nominally held by others. His Queensland runs
are Rookwood and Barenya in Hughenden district. His
first run was Singorimba, 50 miles from Jerilderie ; then,
about ’6l, he purchased Coonong. In ’BO he bought
Toorale and Dunlop from Salmon Wilson for £400,000,
and has made most of his money out of those two places.
Toorale has been famous for its horses—hundreds of
beauties have gone to India during the last 15 years.
Toorale shore 400,000 sheep in one year in the 80’s, and in
one of those years they sold 18,000 young ewes at
18s. per head, and 30,000 wethers at 14s. The
value of the Sam M'Caughey freeholds in New South
Wales is little short of £2,000,000, the areas amount-
ing to 545,000 acres. Mr. M’Caughey shore last year
1,300,000 sheep. He owns some 2000 horses and about
6000 head of cattle. Has also a big share in Coree, his
late brother David’s property. In ’B5 he went to America
and spent £40,000 in Vermont merinos, out of which he
has made another fortune. He leads a very active life.
Is his own general manager, and knows all that is going
on on all his properties far and near. Is always making
improvements. Pays his men well, and expects good
work in return. Is at breakfast by 6.30 every morning,
and midnight often finds him still toiling at his vaist cor-
respondence. Is a real good sort; popular alike with
men who meet him for the first time and those who have
known and dealt with him for years ; plays an excellent
game of billiards, likes a good dinner, and enjoys life in
a quiet way. Has a great penchant for machinery, and
has invented very useful contrivances for tank-sinking
and water-lifting. Made £3OOO in royalties out of one of
his earth-scoops.

The M‘Gooey breaks out in a fresh place :
Grabben Gullen is dose to Burrowa, which explains all
about it in a single sentence. Its population consists of
Ryan and Dwyer, with an odd Rooney and Mulcahy
thrown in. Thirty years ago, when the Grabben Gullen
man —the original and genuine artiole —wasn’t bashing
his “ old woman ” with a wooden shovel, or running about
with nothing on but his boots and trousers yelling out
for someone to come and “ pick him up ” while he fought
his nearest neighbor, he was training a billy-goat to fight.
The Grabben Gullen man who in those days didn’t own
at least one billy-goat, was regarded with suspicion. And
the bigger his goat, the more respect the Grabben Gullen-
ite received. The mere swapping of goats established a
species of clanship which it took lots of backsliding,
thieving, and general misconduct to dissever. Every
Sunday a battle royal took place between every man’s
goat and his neighbor’s, the stakes being a bucketful of
potatoes. Sometimes a quart of whisky (home-made),
and even a sucking-pig, changed hands on the event.
Sometimes, also, the men themselves started fighting,
and a Grabben Gullen fight was a sight for the gods and a
job for the Goulburn doctors. The G.G. man always
fought best and most effectively while up in the air (on
the wing, so to speak). He was eminently dangerous in the
rear, for when he bounded 6ft. into the air, cracking his
ironshod heels 120 times to the minute, he pummelled
the man in fr'ont with fists and head, and, kicking out
backwards with both feet at once, contrived to cave-in
the faces of those behind him. . . . The following Grab-
bengullenalities are immortal, viz. : A Grabben Gullen
“bullock” is a native bear, a G.G. “cheque” is a pig.
When the stranger had finished reaping he got his choice
either to carry away his “ cheque” by the tail, and get it
cashed at the nearest pub., or go away empty-handed. . ..
A G. Gullen “pie” is a pumpkin stuffed with potatoes.
The man who ate meat on Fridays was known as a “ yaller-
belly.” A traveller who once ate meat, on Good Friday
was followed 16 miles by 27 Dwyers, 18 Ryans, 4 Rooneys,
and 2 Gallaghers, under the belief that he would “ doy
(die) at shundown ” —but he didn’t. When a G. Gullen
man means “ yes” he says “ yaf,” the old women “ yef,”
and the young women “ yif ” ; the young men say “ yaaf.”
They rang no bells at. G. Gullen at dinner-time, but a
young woman wearing- a native-bearskin skirt, trimmed
with dogchains, and her father’s old black-cloth waistcoat,
came within three chains of the reapers or potato-rooters
and snorted out, “ Kum along; they’re boilt,” meaning
the spuds.

“ s.” : No worse insult can possibly
be offered to a way-back aboriginal than to
say that he has a weak or thin skull. To the
white, “ thickheaded ” is an insult; to the black-
man it is a great compliment. To say “you got
em head no thicker than an egg-shell,” will bring
about a fight any day of the week—a prompt dar-
ing of the scandaliser to make good his words.

“6x8”: All. our aborigines aren’t lazy.
Watched a blackfellow, recently, dig out a ground
gohanna with hands, tomahawk and yam-stick.
Had to make four or five tunnels ere he found the
right hole. Worked hard and sweated for nearly
two hours. Thinking he was hungry I gave him
some meat. He took it, and thanked me, but
went on digging. “My word,” he said, “me
likem that feller guanna bedder ’u meat.”

The M‘Cooey : The Bulletin is correct
(21/l/’99) about lizards running on hind-legs,
Many Australian lizards run, when frightened,
entirely on the hind-legs, with tail quite off the
ground, and body inclined forward at angle of
about 55. “ Leseur’s water lizard,” common on
the Wollondilly and Tweed Rivers, has this habit,
and to see it “ scooting ” for the river, across the
GOING TO THE SHEARING.—A FAR WESTERN IDYLL.
sand, is about as ludicrous a sight as one could
imagine. A small Varanus, found always in
waterless, plain country, runs also most comically
upon its hind-legs, as do several other species.
Owing to the angle that the feet of large Austra-
lian lizards have with the axis of the body their
run upon all-fours is extremely clumsy.

“Tin-Lip” on a toxicological subject :
Dear Bulletin. — Re oil cure of strychnine-poisoned
dog (8., 18/2/99) it was this way. Sheep-do”-hud swal-
lowed bait, and had one convulsion. I ran to station,
got first oil procurable (cod-liver) and jammed a tumbler-
ful down dog’s throat. Result, dog alive to-day. Have
heard of same cure repeatedly from old-time shepherds.
Another remedy which I have seen used effectively is to
powder some charcoal and likewise jam it down dog’s
throat as best you can, using water if necessary. Another :
Moment you see dog eating bait catclrhim by hind legs
and swing him round and round—result, bait vomited
up. As The Bulletin wished to break George Pile’s dog
up recently, give it strychnine. I’ll supply the oil and a
£5-note if it fails to cure him. Any oil, I believe, will do.
I plank down 2s. 6d. to purchase oil meanwhile, and if
you or George Pile’s dog aren’t taking any, contribute
said amount to appeal made in Bulletin on behalf of the
Afghan camel-drivers against the Bourke Carrying Co.
Will bet any Bulletin reader £5 re the oil-cure, and let
you, if you will, hold the stakes. — Tin-Lip.
If “ Tin-Lip’s ” theory is sound, what is its
rationale ?

‘ ‘ Old-Time ” on certain native foods :
The N. Australian native tribes in and around Port
Essington use very largely for food the root of a species
of bull-rush, not unlike the combunga, so common on
the Murray flats around Swan Hill, where also it was
largely eaten. The Port Essington natives, grow fat
during the time this root is in season. They call it
marro-ait. It is eaten as a paste, after being bruised
between two stones, and then browned over red-hot
coals. I found it sweet and pleasant to eat. It favors
the small, shallow lagoons and big plains with shallow
water-courses thereon. The roots of the water-lily (Nym-
phia) are also much used, and are excellent eating.
The aborigines eat largely also of various kinds of yams.
There grows in rich profusion on the northern peninsula,
a really excellent vegetable, the “cabbage palm,” the
edible part growing near the top, sometimes 60ft. from
the ground, and forming the heart of the plume-like
bunch of leaves on the crest of the tall trunk. The
cabbage is bulky and palatable. It is obtained by tearing
away the surrounding leaves until the heart is reached.
A want of knowledge of these edible plants has cost
many valuable lives and much hardship. No better
instance need be given than that mentioned by Capt.
Simpson as having occurred when he was looking
for the papers of the unfortunate explorer Kennedy
and the missing members of his expedition. When
the Captain reached Shelburne Bay (at which
place eight men had been left behind, and where six of
them had died from sheer starvation) he was, he says,
“surprised to find some cabbage-palms growing in the
vicinity of the camp, the tops of which are very nutri-
tious and would be most desirable to starving men had
they been aware of it.” The pith of this palm, as well as
the fibre of the bottle-tree, is now well-known to be rich
in farina. There is also a very agreeable native goose-
berry, almost identical with the Cape variety, it is
sometimes found only as a bush, but in certain localities
it reaches to the dimensions of a tree. In either case the
fruit is equally good, and quite worthy of cultivation as
a domesticated fruit.
* * * •
“Long-’Un ” : In the 90-Mile Desert is a
little oasis—Hawks’ Nest—-through which the
gold-escort passed in the old Bendigo days.
Track since abandoned, but this solitary gum-and-
sheoak-covered basin was once the abode of man,
evidenced by a fallen-in well and the remains of
an old letter-box (half a kerosene-tin fastened to
a tree with fencing-wire). Camped alongside, and
in the morning, rolling up bluey, killed a couple
of centipedes under pillow. Biil kicked his feet
out of his rugs, and out wriggled a beauty. The
other Bill got up and started fossicking. One
under his ear on pillow ; shook another out of his
swag-bag. After breakfast, went to tucker-box
for lunches. The cook had sent out a large tart,
and, stuck in the centre of the jammy part—more
centipede! Went to same box in evening for
a candle, and found yet another coiled around the
inside of the packet. Following days, scores more
killed. Only place for miles untouched by bush-
fires ; hence the centipedes.

“YangAnyut” : Old inhabitant of Rose
Bay vouches for following. Had large and
ancient rooster, which I one day saw fall down
dead. Thereupon dissected him, and found cause
of death in 18in. brown snake, which popped out
alive from abdominal cavity. Couldn’t find any
trace of place of entrance.

Applied for a remove next day, on account of

infant of the widow’s happened todie, whereupon
she ordered a gorgeous coffin, with a big brass
plate thereon, setting forth that the occupant was
“ the dearly beloved son of Silas S. Smith, manager
of the Unbusted Bank,” and at the funeral was
produced an immense wreath, purposing to come
from “a sorrowing father.” Smith fled the town
next morning without waiting for his remove.
* * * *
“ Trotter B.” : I have never seen a Maori
woman suckling a pig, but I have seen one
suckling a puppy. When I was staying at the
Maori settlement of Hikutaia, Upper Thames,
the chief (a man about 50) had a wife of about 18.
She had no children, but she used to have a puppy
constantly pulling away, first at one breast and
then at the other. What amount of sustenance
the puppy derived from the process I am unable
to say. The chief had also a sister of about 40
living with him. I could not then speak Maori
and I quite misunderstood the situation, taking
the chief’s sister for his wife and making love to
his wife under the impression that she was his
daughter. The old fellow made numerous
attempts to set me right, but failed utterly. One
day he and his sister went pigeon-shooting, and as
they thought it was not good enough to leave me
behind they took me with them, but as soon as
we got in the bush, I gave them the slip and set
off hot foot for the settlement. But fast as I was
the chief was there before me. Three times more
I played him the same trick, with the same
result, so I gave it best. Not long after a
white man came from Shortland with a load
of provisions, and lectured me on the enor-
mity of my offence in making love to the wife
of a chief. I said, “I thought all the time she
was his daughter.” “ No,” said the pakeha, “ she
is his wife.” “But who is the other one?” I
queried. “That is my wife,” replied the pakeha.
I thought after that it was time to leave, so
bundled up my traps and cleared for Ohinemuri.
OTHELLO and DESDEMONA, in George-
street, Sydney.
“ Long’un ” : Camped on a bare limestone
rise subject to the full force of southern ocean
gales. Returned to camp one evening, found one
side of tent-fly ripped half across ; all the ropes off
pegs, and tied in one large knot from constant
whirling ; other side had the old cook hanging on
to a corner, ropes round his legs and arms ; a deep
furrow ploughed in the ground for yards around
by his swinging feet. “ Been hangin’ on for four
hours,” he gasped, “ prayin’ ter God ye’d come
and deloosin’ me !’’ An old resident cocky came
along shortly afterwards. “ Windy, d’yer call
it ? Why, I’ve knowed it blow that ’ard that the
blessed crows used ter have ter walk home to
roost.”
DROUGHTY SCRUB.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
M'Grogan ran a business in the town of Droughty Scrub,
Where he made a decent living from the profits of a pub.;
Though the products of the district were primitive and
few,
And the people lived by catching wild dog and kangaroo ;
But the price of skins fell heavily, and things were rather
bad,
And even leading citizens were feeling somewhat sad.
Then it happened that a barber man, who cut the local
hair,
And cut it very badly—when he wasn’t on the tear,
Just mentioned to the grocer, when he made a passing
call,
How the townsmen ought to agitate to get the Capital;
And the grocer thought the barber quite a genius in his
way,
And consulted on the matter with the butcher ’cross the
way.
The butcher thought it splendid, as the climate there
was right—
Always very clear in daylight, and fairly dark at night;
And the editor—a writer by some considered tame —
Brought out a powerful article to bear upon the same,
Showing how the country round them, if only rung and
scrubbed,
And the mountains slightly levelled, and the rocks all
burnt and grubbed,
Would be suitable for settlement; no country far or near
Was nearly equal to it, said the Dingo Pioneer.
So the matter was decided by the people one and all
That Droughty Scrub was suitable to be the capital.
M’Grogan knew the distance from the city very well—
He had tramped it in the ’Bo’s, when he kept the Gum
Hotel,
And the member for the district has sent them word to
say
He is almost sure to get it, if he can but have his way.
So they held a monster meeting, and the Mayor spoke
loud in praise
Of the good times in the future, of the coming better
days,
When no longer they will live upon the ’possum, bear,
and grub,
And a gorgeous marble capital will rise at Droughty
Scrub.
THOMAS SMITH.

OLD AGE PENSIONS—FROM A TORY
STANDPOINT.
“ WITH THE FLIES.”
A bushman thinks the meanest man— on earth, at all
events—
Is he who has a liquor “ with the flies ; ”
They’ll forgive a man who robs them, but not a man
consents
To look at him who boozes “ with the flies.”
He may not know a single soul of those who fill the bar ;
He may be but a stranger who has travelled from afar ;
But let him liquor solus, and the lads say, “ There you
are 1
He’s a crook 'un, for he’s drinkin’ with the flies.”
Stan. Warrington.
* * * *
“ Alleera ” : He iguana swallowing shin-
bone of calf. Was once working in the bush,
and, at mid-day, threw away a ham-bone picked
clean. Shortly afterwards heard tapping, and
found that an iguana was trying to swallow
the bone. One end projected inches from his
mouth, but, as the other was apparently too large
for his throat, he was assisting its progress
mechanically by backing a few inches from a log
and then rushing up and striking the end of the
bone against it. Kept on till the outer end of the
bone was flush with his mouth.
“ Yes,” said Old Barney,
“ bullick s was bullicks them times.
I remember one wet day goin’ up
the Big Range looken fur a tree
fur shingles. I was wallten’ along the
top of the Range when I turns round
and sees a big red bullick stalken’
me. I makes down the mountain-
side, and he after me as hard as he
could lick. I sees a big holler log,
and runs for it, and just as I crawls
inter it down he comes ‘ Whooh ! ’
and takes the sole of me boot with
his near horn. I crawls up the log
fur about fifteen foot, and I hears
him walken’ round and sniffen. I
gets me eyes to a crack and looks out,
and sees him looken’ in at me
through the same crack, that close
that if I could ’a got me knife I could
’a jabbed his eye out; but the log
was too tight. I was wonderin’ what
he meant to do next, when I hears
him hooken’ his horn under the log,
and away me and it goes rollen’ down
the side of the mountain and the
bullick after us. It was steep, and
we (me and the log) must ’a rolled
three miles in a little more ’n three
minutes—the bullick racin’ close be-
hind—when bump we goes ag’in a
big stringy-bark ; the log breaks in
two the middle, and one half
goes down one spur, and the other
half, with me in it, down another;
and the last I sees of that bullick
he was chasin’ the wrong half.
Presently my half stops at the foot
of the range, and I crawls out.
Didn’t look any more for good-
splitten’ timber that day.”

“ Roley P.” : “Recently saw
porcupine in orchard lie down under
apple tree, erect his spines, and roll
over the apples which had fallen
from the tree. When he arose he
had a fair cargo, and made for a
hollow log, into which he backed
himself, the apples dropping off as he squeezed
in. He then ate them at his leisure.” Looks
like a tozer.

At Clermont (N.Q.) the town, band, which
is only in its infancy, for three days previous to
the decease of a popular publican, whose dying
wish it was that the Dead March in “ Saul ” should
be played at his funeral, industriously practised
and labored heavily under the strains of the Dead
March aforesaid. How consoling for that publican
in his last hours !

“H.S.”: I once dug up an aboriginal
grave on the shores of Lake Menindie, N.S.W.,
to obtain the skeleton. I found the remains en-
cased in gum-leaves, which, though the burial
had taken place over 20 years before, still retained
a shade of green. The body was about Gft. below
the surface, rolled in soft and Pj iant .) >ar ttn(
bound with thongs of same. Ihe tribes there-
abouts on burying their dead go down in pairs
into the grave, and wound each other severely on
the head with nullas and waddies until the blood
flows freely on to the corpse. This at tunes is
kept up by relays of warriors anxious to take part
in the proceedings. Green boughs are then
usually placed over the body, which mostly lies in
a bed or nest of gum-leaves. Ihe females near
relatives of deceased, used to cut off their hair,
place a net on the head, then plaster their heads
all over with gypsum, often to 2in. in thickness.
One of these “widow’s caps” in my possession
weighs nearly 101 b. They were worn continuously
from a week to a fortnight, then deposited on the
grave along with some peculiar-shaped balls, from
size and form of an emu’s egg downwards, made
of gypsum, sand and pipeclay. Occasional speci-
mens are still to be found on parts of the Darling.
Related of an English swell that he was
travelling alone by rail with a lovely female
stranger, when, after scant preliminaries, she tore
her toque from her head, dishevelled her hair,
and, as the train pulled up, shrieked for help,
declaring that she had been brutally assaulted.
But her fellow-passenger stirred not—he lay
quietly back, smoking his cigar. “What,” ex-
claimed the station-master, “ have you to say to
this charge?” “Only this!” was the answer,
and the cool hand pointed to his cigar, which
showed a beautiful grey ash over an inch long.
The good old railway-boom days in Vic-
toria are over with a vengeance. The long-talked-
of Mildura railway is at last being surveyed.
Proposed terms of construction: Line to be un-
fenced ; laid with second-hand rails; any private
land required for the line to be handed over free
of charge ; the Mildura residents to send all their
produce bound for Melbourne by rail instead of
water carriage for three years ; a yearly rate of Is.
in the £ on the rental value of Mildura lands to
be levied if necessary to make the line pay 3| per
cent, on cost; proceeds of Govt, land sales for 15
miles on each side of the line to go towards paying
off cost of construction; also, for 20 years, all State
land rentals in same area, over and above rentals
now received, to be similarly applied. And only
10 years ago anybody who wanted a railway just
asked Dunky Gillies for it, and Dunky simply
rang the bell and told his menial to bring up two
or three railways at once.

Australian who is struggling for a title. The man
who wants to make a big sensation and get a title
of unusual dimensions must strike something new.
The church and university donation and all the

who presented the country with a much-needed

The Vienna Fbjemdenblatt has the oldest
of living journalists actively engaged upon its
staff. Herr Leopold von Blumencron, the curiosity
in question, celebrated his 95th birthday lay walk-
ing down to the office as usual and reeling off a
little “copy” for the evening and next morning
editions of the paper. Younger members of the
staff took the opportunity to decorate his desk
with flowers, which was, perhaps, not a very
cheering compliment under the circs. For a
Blumencron, born on 21st Feb., 1804, floral
tributes must surely come as a reminder that all
flesh is grass.

IMPRESSIONS OF BENDIGO’S EASTER FAIR.


THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

Concerning some of the new Q. Labor-
members : —
Among the new batch of Q. Labor-men Worker Higgs
stands easily first in general interest. A Sydney native,
and about 6ft. 3in. in his socks, he has some of the limp-
ness usually associated either with the Cornstalk or the
long-drawn-out man. He has ferocious energy, masked
by a deceitful deliberativeness in speaking, both in public
and private, which is decidedly effective. He is a fanati-
cal “straight-goer,” but is a man of affairs withal, and
knows his book. His besetting weakness is amiability—
he lacks gall to make opposition bitter. “ Joe ” Lesina is
another ex-Sydneyice. Used to be a flash-light at public
meetings in the old Single-tax days. Was, and still is,
probably the most fluent and rapid speaker in the
Southern Hemisphere—could even out-point, in these re-
spects, Holman, of N.S.W. Labor-party. Went North,
developed Socialistic and unionistic views, became editor
of Charters Towers Eagle, and settled down into resneo-
tability, matrimony, and M.L.A.-ship. Enjoys talking
for its own sake, and will be a thorn in the side of Q.
Government Hansard. Givens ran for Cairns last time.
Was then a Freetrade crank. A hard grafter, personally
irreproachable, and, barring hisfiscalomania, level-headed
enough. Fisher, of Gympie, is a stolid and immovable
Scotsman, stirred to a slow fire of consuming indignation
by the financial iniquities of the Q.N. Bank. Formerly
member for Gympie ; defeated last time, he owes his re-
habilitation mainly to the steady work of that smart
paper Gympie Truth, of which he is part-owner. He
knows the art of saying nothing for good long spells, and
often uses his knowledge.
THE WHISKY OE THE WISE
is the title bestowed on D.C.L. Scotch (black
bottle) by many hotel-keepers. They remark that
the more brainy customers, veterans who think
twice before ordering, say—“Mine is “D.C.L.”*
Significant that Barr-Smith, S.A. depart-
ing millionaire, has had his beautiful residence and
grounds, three miles from Adelaide, in the market
for ten years ; price, .£45,000 ; no buyers. In the
silver-boom days W. R. Wilson offered £40,000,
but was refused, and Barr-Smith has cursed his
want of judgment ever since.

THE

WORKER,
HAS AGAIN RECEIVED GRATEFUL OUTPOURINGS FROM
GRATEFUL HEARTS.
REAL TRUE HONEST WORK
NO INFLATED STATEMENTS, but HIGH MORAL PRINCIPLE.
Grace-street, Yarraville,
23rd March, 1899.
Dear Sir, —I have much pleasure in adding my
testimony to the value of “ Mr. WEBBER’S ”
well-known “HERBAL REMEDY, VITA-
DATIO.”
It has cured me of an obstinate ease of
“GOUT-ECZEMA” in the upper lip, which,
in spite of the best medical advice, afflicted me
for over two years.
My health was also bad, owing to SUP-
PRESSED GOUT, and all the treatment which
I had hitherto been under only aggravated the
disorder.
At last I was induced to give your remedy a
trial, which I did, with the happy result of a
permanent cure, and, at the same time, greatly
improving my health.
I hope this testimony will induce anyone suf-
fering as I did to give the VITADATIO a
FAIR TRIAL, and the result will be, as in my
case, a permanent cure.
You are at liberty to refer anyone to me for
further information in reference to my case.—l
am, dear Sir, yours very sincerely,
J. JOSEPH.
ANOTHER SPONTANEOUS TRIBUTE.
MOST ENCOURAGING NEWS.
READ THEM FOR YOURSELVES.
SUFFERERS WILL BE INTERESTED.
Glengower, near Clunes, Victoria,
22nd March, 1899.
Mr. S. A. Palmer, General Agent for
Vitadatio.
Dear Sir, —I must write to tell you of my re-
covery from serious bladder - trouble. About
seven years ago I was seized with it, passing
blood and gravelly matter, and in intense
pain ; it was excruciating and prolonged, and
ultimately incapacitated me entirely, so that
I had to take to my bed and give up work.
The doctors told me I could not be cured, ex-
cept by a painful and dangerous operation,
and I was advised by them to go to Melbourne
and have it done. Bad as the pain was, I pre-
ferred to die from the disease rather than
from the operation, and I got very much
worse. All the medical men could do was to
inject morphia to ease the great pain. Every-
body knows in this district of my condition,
and I was expected to die soon, but Vitadatio
came to my rescue. I was a mere skeleton. A
friend called my attention to a paragraph in the
Southern Cross newspaper about Vitadatio,
and he pressed me so to try it that at last I did.
I took about seven bottles; the first two made
me feel very queer, but I persevered, and got so
well as to get about, ride on horseback, and re-
sume my work. I have never looked back since,
and it is the grandest medicine in the world for
those suffering as I did. I have taken several
bottles since to get thoroughly strong, and I
have no hesitation in giving you permission to
use this letter in the press, and in any other way
you like, so long as others get to know of this
wonderful, life-giving, operation-saving Vita-
datio.
I am now strong and hearty, with a ruddy
complexion, and always actively at hard work,
after many years of suffering, and I have no-
body to thank but God Himself and the friend
who told me of it, and those who make it and
sell it.—Yours gratefully and truly,
JOHN WEATHERSON,
Farmer, Glengower.
Witness—John Young.
A VOICE OF CHEER FROM THE PAR-
SONAGE.
A VERY GRATIFYING TESTIMONY.
WILL GREATLY COMFORT OTHERS.
DISCIPLES OF THE “GOLDEN RULE.”
The Parsonage,
Clunes, 23rd March, 1899.
To Mr. S. A. Palmer,
45-47 Bourke-street,
Agent-General for Vitadatio.
Dear Sir, —It gives me pleasure to state to
you, for the benefit of others who may be suf-
fering and who may read this letter, that I
have been completely cured of what the doc-
tors called “ Chronic Lumbago,” by the use of
Vitadatio, after nine years of great suffering.
Four months ago Mr. Weatherson, a well-to-do
farmer, of Glengower, near Clunes, induced me
to try it. He has been, in my opinion, almost
miraculously cured of a long-standing and
painful disease, through Vitadatio alone, when
all other remedies had failed. He is an attend-
ant at my church, and I know him well.
During my illness I was for several weeks at a
time unable to rest through terrible pains in my
back. Several doctors (whose names I can give
if necessary) attended me, but could give me no
relief. Being a minister of religion, my duties
took me long journeys into the adjacent coun-
try, and you can understand how the dreadful
pain often seriously interfered with my minis-
try. On my journey I have frequently had to
alight from my buggy (quite unable to sit up-
right), and endeavor to get ease by holding on
to the buggy behind. In my extremity, the
doctors advised that the only likely remedy left
to me was an expensive, nay, ruinous, journey
to New Zealand to undergo the sulphur-springs
treatment, and, no matter what the cost, I re-
solved to go. Providence interposed, however,
for friend Weatherson’s case came prominently
before me when I removed from my ministry at
Camperdown to Clunes, and I can truthfully
say that I was saved from the terrible expense of
such a journey, and my health restored, after
many years’ suffering, by Vitadatio.
It is all the more remarkable because I took
about four bottles only. I cannot speak too
highly of the medicine. It is curing several
friends of mine to whom I have recommended it.
I will willingly answer any inquiries that may
be addressed to me about my case, and others
that are within my knowledge, and you may
make whatever use (in the newspapers or other-
wise) you may choose of this letter, for the sake
of those who need freedom from pain. It has
made life a pleasure to me once more, and is
enabling me to carry on God’t work with all the
vigorous health of mind and body He originally
endowed me with.—Yours faithfully,
J. CHENALL,
Bible Christian Minister, Clunes.

THEN IT WENT ON ALL RIGHT.
The writer of the letter which I am going to
copy for you in a moment has a complaint to
make. Rather, perhaps, a complaint to place on
record, as the reason for it is passed away for the
present, and she hopes—and we hope with her —
that it may not return. The complaint does not
refer to any relative, friend, or foe, but to her own
heart. It did not work well. It was weak, and
for a long time she was unable to find means to
make it do better. Which was a serious matter,
inasmuch as the vigor of the circulation of the
blood always depends upon the force wherewith
the heart drives it.
Still, it seems to me we ought to be a bit indul-
gent towards the heart in view of the labor it has
to p‘rform. Remember that it never takes a full
minute’s rest at one time, night or day, from the
instant it begins at your birth until, like a muffled
drum, it stops for good* and all —life’s funeral
march to the grave being over. During all this
while, ten years or a hundred, the heart has got to
keep on pumping blood through your body at the
rate of from 130 strokes a minute in childhood to
50 or GO in old age. If you happen to have a
mechanical turn of mind, it may interest you to
figure out how much this stands for in units of
horse-power for a given case and time. If .not,
you can take my word for it that, merely as a
machine, the heart deserves your respect. So long
as it goes ahead steadily, up hill and down dale,
haminering away softly but strongly, you haven’t
a word to say for or against it; but when it begins
to get weak, maybe skipping a stitch now and
then, you call in the doctor, who puts the tip of
his finger just below the base of your left thumb,
looks wise and solemn (as befits the occasion), and
says, “ Ah, yes, yes ; I see, I see.” But what does
he see ? He doesn’t tell you that; he leaves medi-
cine, and mentions when he will look in again.
But as to the letter I spoke of. “ For many
years,” the lady says, “ I suffered from indigestion
and weak heart. Very little exertion made me
feel weary and tired. Cold, clammy sweats broke
over me. I had a poor appetite, and after meals
an aching pain at the chest and a miserable sink-
ing feeling at the stomach. I had also much pain
at the left side, and my heart would flutter so as
to frighten me. At length I became so weak I
was barely able to get about, being no longer able
to do my housework.
“ Owing to the trouble at my heart I obtained
no proper rest at night, and often walked about
my bedroom at night. Many times these attacks
were so bad I thought I was dying. During the
day a sense of suffocation sometimes came upon
me, and I was obliged to go to the door for fresh
air.
“ Year after year I suffered like this ; now a
little better, now as bad as I could be. In No-
vember, 1887, while on a visit to Croydon, my son-
in-law persuaded me to try Mother Seigel’s Syrup.
He got me a bottle, and after taking it I experi-
enced great relief. The pain at my heart was
easier, and I felt better as a whole. I could eat
well, and the food agreed with me.
“ I now felt encouraged to continue using this
remedy. Soon I was in better health than for
years, the heart trouble having disappeared alto-
gether. Since that time when I feel anything
ailing me a few doses of Mother Seigel’s Syrup
never fail to give the desired relief. I have told
many persons of the benefit I have derived from
it, and hereby consent to your publishing this
statement should you wish to do so.”—(Signed)
(Mrs.) William Harrington, near Wickford Hill,
Clare, Suffolk, November 12, 1897.
Now what ailed Mrs. Harrington’s heart ? Why,
precisely the same things that ailed her lungs, her
nerves and her muscles— weakness. Therein she
is right. It was a weak heart, but not a diseased
heart. The heart is a muscle, and (seeing the pro-
digious lot of work it has to do) necessarily a
strong, active muscle. But it will not work with-
out pay any more than you or I will. With all the
rest of the body, it has got to be sustained and
strengthened by food. Here we have the point,
then. The lady was afflicted with chronic indiges-
tion. For this reason her whole body grew weak
—the heart, of course, with other parts of the
engine. Hence all the symptoms she names. Her
immense all-round weakness and puller-down is
that same old dyspepsia. When Mother Seigel’s.
Syrup made the digestion of plenty of food pos-
sible, the heart went on all right, like a newly-
wound clock.’'

They all take to it, and the more they drink
the better results, as witness our visiting sports-
horse-owners, and bowlers ; the latter simply be-
came enthusiastic in their praise of Zetz Spa Water,
and maintain had they but another week on “Zetz”
the scores would have been different at the finish of
the game. However, this they are determined on,
wherever they go, the cry is “Give me ‘Zetz.’”
Why? Because “It’s orlright.” Let ’em all come,

quality undeniable. “Zetz,” the federal governor.*
A bad young man named Hancock, at
Beaufort (Vic.), lately incurred the wrath of a
policeman because he “maintained a sullen
silence ” when reproved by that officer for “ riot-
ous conduct.” Riotous conduct consisted of
smoking in the face and presence of authority as
represented by senior-constable Somebody, and—
with others—passing “ right in front of me, in
defiance of my order to go home ! ” Beaufort
beaks thought this such shameful conduct that
they fined the sullen Hancock 5s. and 25s. costs.
The sullen one didn’t see it. Appealed, and the
beaks were at once humiliated. Decision quashed,
with £15 15s. costs. And now there is one senior-
constable and four Beaufort J’s.P. who have to
take their gruel in “sullen silence.”

Tact.
Tact is born with some men and women, and
those who have it use their gift instinctively. It
is not measured alike to those who have it. Men
possess it in different degrees ; while others, again,
are wanting in it altogether. It is the outcome of
intellectual and temperamental qualifications, and
implies possession of clear perceptions, quick
imagination, and delicate sensibilities. It is
these that give a tactful person his subtle intui-
tion of another’s mental processes and modes of
feeling, and it was tact and sound reasoning that
actuated Mr. J, Stewart, 407 Collins-street, Mel-
bourne, Manager of the Campbell’s Creek Dredg-
ing Co., Castlemaine, in laying aside prejudice
and adopting the use of Warner’s Safe Cure when
suffering from liver complaint. He had the tact
to give credence to hi's friends’ experience. Now
he writes : “I have used Warner’s Safe Cure for
some time for my liver, and find it superior to
anything that could be obtained. lam fully con-
vinced it is a splendid medicine for the above
complaint, and I feel justified in recommending it
to all liver subjects.”*

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Dibbs push was well-advised in calling
its ragged regiment the Colonists’ Anti-Convention
Bill League of N.S.W. The Australians are on
the other side.
The recent accident at Auburn (N.S.W.)
meat-works was the third fatal blow-up in N.S.W.
this year, also it resulted in the third verdict of
accidental death. And still that Land Boilers In-
spection Act shows no sign of coming !
Premier Forrest steps into ex-Premier
Nelson’s shoes in the matter of Federation. Nel-
son favored Federation, of course—so long as it
consisted of mere talk, but show it in concrete
form, and—“ What’s the hurry?” Lip-worship
only.
The politician who resigned his seat to let
Barton into N.S.W. Assembly (Francis Clarke) is
made M.L.C., and the politician who assured the
world he scared Barton from Bourke (E. D.
Millen) is also made M.L.C. Yes —No. Bane
and Antidote.
Yic. appointed Agent - General Andrew
Clarke to his present billet for three years, with a
written proviso that if Federation be accomplished
earlier he will resign without allowance in lieu of
salary. That salutary provision should be at-
tached to all appointments likely to be affected by
Federation by every province which really treats
the Commonwealth as an approaching reality.
Premier Reid's an-
nouncement that he is
in favor of a State
Land Bank and has
been in favor of it
for a long time,
but has never
started one,
and isn’t go-
ing to start
one—he is
only going
to fix up a
little tem-
porary in-
stalment of
one to ease
the tempo-
rary dis-
tress in the
West —isall
so like Reid.
It is almost
impossible to
count the number of progres-
sive ideas that G. H. Reid
has been in favor of ever since
he assumed office State
Bank, State Land Bank, State
note issue, resumption of large
estates for closer settlement,
a good Navigation Act, re-
form of the Leg. Council, the
diminution of borrowing, a
good Local Govt. Act —and
Heaven knows-what besides.
And at time of writing every
one of them remains just
where it was four years ago.
M.L. Premier Seddon
upsets the theory that it is
necessary for a successful poli-
tician to have strong news-
paper backing. The Govt.
“ organ ” in Wellington has
never possessed half the in-
fluence of the rival Post,
and in Dunedin the Liberals
oertainly used to have a
journal of a kind, but it
shut down suddenly over
five years ago, and left its
two Tory contemporaries to
fume verbosely and point the daily finger of scorn
at Digger Dick. Neither of those hostile journals
has fever been known to allow that Richard was in
the right for. once, but their misguided readers
remain the most persistent of all that very bad
man’s supporters.
Govt, papers in Queensland are rejoicing
over the fact that all the Ministers were safely
returned at recent elections. Nothing unusual
about that. In 11 years only one Minister of
the Crown has b«en rejected by a Bananaland
constituency.
Premier Reid’s decision to appoint Labor
members (jo the Upper House seems to have con-
siderably damped S.M. Herald’s Federal ardor.
It would be the last straw in Topsyturvydom to
find Grandma approving of such a recognition of
the hornyhanded.
; After last N.S.W. election Sydney D.T.
constantly reminded its readers that “ at last we
have a Federal Assembly.” That paper how
praises heaven that “we ” have an anti-Federal
Council. If the D.T. would only reprint and re-
circulate its Federal issues of Aug., Sept., Oct.,
and Nov. last !
A .member proposes to prod N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly with a Bill declaring that any
Minister yvho leaves the province and remains a
whole month beyond its borders shall forfeit his
portfolio. With a provision attached allowing a
Minister to be absent by leave of the House, such
a Bill should have the good effect of killing such
abuses as the Want farce.
Dear Bulletin. —Can you inform a con-
stant reader if the financial expert who discourses
so fluently on the Braddon Biotin Sydney Sunday
Times is the same gentleman who advised us to
invest in Shellback scrip right up to the smash ?
Yours, Milparinka.
There are six candidates for the two-man
seat of Wallaroo (S.A.), now held by Hooper and
Grainger, and three of them, including present
member Hooper, are local preachers. Also, four—
present member Hooper again included—are tee-
totallers. Cheerful prospect for Wallaroo.
M’Millan, Wise and Bruce Smith per-
petuate the Parkes hatred of trimmer Reid and
scorn for his methods. Other politicians, have
been placated or converted, but the three named
breathe to-day the same aversion and contempt
which filled the Parkes parlor in the last year of
the old man’s life.
By no stretch of the imagination can one
of the small minority who voted against the
Enabling Bill in N.S.W. Assembly be termed
“influential.” Let G. Republican Dibbs publish
the name of the weighty man who dare not declare
himself on a national issue unless he had the pro-
tection of the ballot-box.
Jack Want’s opinion of the Amended
Federal Bill may or may not differ from his
opinion of the old Bill, but it is altogether un-
likely he would back up N.S.W. Leg. Council
in holding back the measure from the referendum.
Jack is too experienced a lawyer to openly
express his fear of the Jury’s verdict.
There is every sign of a huge rush of
candidates at the coming S.A. election. There
usually is in the saintly province. At the ’96
election not one candidate got in unopposed. The
mildest scrimmages were at Light, Mt. Barker,
Northern Territory and Stanley, in each of which
places there were three candidates for two seats ;
but at Yorke’s Peninsula the two seats were
scrambled for by nine applicants. Electoral
arrangements are rather curious in S.A., where
Encounter Bay in ’96 had two members for 2178
electors and E. Torrens two for 11,355.
“Capital is valueless without
labor,” said Sydney Telegraph the
other day in the course of a
fatherly admonition to the
*‘ working classes. ” Why,
certainly!! When Adam
came into the Book of
Genesis the first thing
he did was to go
to the bank and
get an overdraft.
OVERHEARD OUTSIDE THE PUB.
TATTERED PERSON : “ Wot’s the row, Jerry?”
JERRY : “ On’y th’ ole man. I bin barneyin' with
'im for about four hours. Mum idle me not
to leave 'im till I got some stuff or we'd get no
dinner to-morrer. So jest now 'e gives me
six bob out o' thirty-five."
T.P.: “ Wot did yer say?”
JERRY : “O, I tole 'im to go to 'ell, an' biffed 'im
on the bugle. 'E's tellin' 'is pals over there
'e'll fight me in the mornin ’ for anything from
a pound to a 'unded. A pint o' Tooth’s all
'e’ll fight, I'm full of'im. Come on."
Otherwise the human race would never have got
really started.
A Sydney branch postmaster, appointed
before last election as Deputy Registrar, piled the
work on to his assistant, who, though he had no
legal power to do so, issued rights wholesale. As
soon as the P.M. discovered his error he rushed
down and had the assistant appointed Assistant
Registrar. But the rights were abroad—issued
and signed by a totally unauthorised person.
Now, what about the validity of the holders’
votes ?
When news got abroad that Barcoo elec-
torate, from the roll of which 112 names were
illegally expunged by creatures of Q. Govern-
ment, had nevertheless been successful in again
returning Democrat Kerr, there was joy in
the ranks of Q. radicalism. The Govt, candidate
had the audacity to assert that this disfranchise-
ment of voters cost him the seat. Perhaps, how-
ever, he was right. It may be that the disgrace-
ful outrago’on justice turned many a dubious vote
in favor of the Labor candidate.
The Cabbage-tree sneer about “Premier
Reid having the support of the Melbourne papers
including those printed in this colony (N.S.W.)
as well as on the banks of the Yarra,” is typical of
that class of “ Federalist.” The anti-federal Sydney
l ij S / rutl i a . n ex 'Victorjan staff, and was

subscribed in Melbourne and by ex-Melbourneites.
Ihere is certainly nothing of the abused Mel-

while what is more tho-
roughly “Pure Syd-
ney ’’-than the E.
