DREYFUS IN SYDNEY
DOMAIN.
FIRST DEADBDEAT : “ Who’s this yer
secret dosser wot’s kickin’ up all the row in France?”
SECOND DITTO: “ Dunno. S’pose he’s some bloke wot’s struck a pitch the cops can’t find.”
Macfarlane, M.P. (N.S.W.), declares that
the sugar districts are likely to go Cabbage-tree
on next referendum. This is the Macfarlane who
was decoyed by the same old George (with the
other sugar-members, including Meagher) into
voting against his party to keep Reid in power on
the plea that the preservation of the sugar-duties
depended upon the retention in power of the
gentleman who had solemnly promised to take
them off.
The mantle of No-Yes has fallen on Lyne,
whose speech to N.S.W. Chamber of Manufac-
tures (enthusiastic Federalists for the most part)
was worthy of a rail-sitter of the deepest dye.
By the time this sees print the erstwhile Oppo-
sition leader may have declared himself before the
Assembly, and the chances seem to favor his
going right against the Reid-Barton party. A
proceeding which will surely need much explain-
ing to the electors of the Hume.
Sydney Telegraph last Friday talked
scornfully about Premier Reid “cocking up with
the title of ‘hon.’ two or three coal-miners;”
also “ the brilliant idea of going down into coal-
mines with a batch of Legislative Council nomi-
nations ” and “ packing the Council with mine-
employees.” Considering that the Labor-party
in N.S.W. Parliament has been rather an anti-
Billious combination than otherwise, these angry,
jibes suggest that the D.T. has gone off its head
or taken to secret drinking.
Christchurch (M.L.) Weekly Press : “If
Mr. Seddon is not going to borrow, then the
railway which is necessary to join Auckland and
Wellington, and which will cost upwards of
<£1,000,000, will never be built.” Rats ! The
total wealth of Maoriland is estimated at nearly
<£200,000,000, and if .£1,000,000 can’t be found
out of that pile, without borrowing, to build a
valuable public work which will almost certainly
return per cent., then M.L. had better take in
its shingle and retire from business.
N.S.W. Col. -Sec. Brunker’s dolorous moo
about the scandals attaching to the N.S.W. Govt,
practice of appointing honorary magistrates comes
self-aecusingly from the man who has so long
played chief part in the nauseous game. In
the Parlces Govt, he was similarly industrious.
Why does he not, if sincere, table a bill to take
such patronage out of the clutches of Ministers ?
In a community which has spotted the balefulness
of the current system there is no salvation for the
man who merely bellows over its evils.
The late Queensland elections are not cal-
culated to make a Democrat go off his head with
joy. Yet there were certain cheerful phases of
the contest. One, the expulsion of certain notori-
ous “ rats ” from the Labor-party. A second still
more satisfactory feature was the cold shoulder
shown to aspiring members of the legal profession
who rushed into the political arena. Out of eight
fresh lawyer candidates only one, Rutledge, was
successful, and his success was only due to a
foolish splitting of votes on the other side.
Premier Turner is drafting an Old-Age
Pensions Bill for submission to Vic. Parliament
next session. Hardly anyone calls Turner a
democrat, because he doesn’t hang a big label on
himself to that effect. And yet, when one con-
siders his minimum-wage law (which he has been
game to enforce), his State land-bank, his law
for the resumption of large estates, his attempts
(which failed only because the property-elected
Council is wholly beyond his control) to enact
one-man-one-vote and to exclude cheap alien
labor, and his present old-age-pension scheme,
there is no prominent politician in Australia
whose record can compare with that of the present
Vic. Premier.
M.L. A. Spruson’s reference to the strangers’-
galleryites, and Premier Reid’s retort, recall a
former reference by R. C.. Luscombe (Parkes*
“ withered tarantula”) to the galleryites, and the
consequences thereof. Luscombe appealed from
an unsympathetic Assembly to “ its masters, the
people,” and indicated with a gesture the gal-
lery. “ Giraffe ” Taylor immediately pointed out
to the Speaker that Mr. Luscombe had drawn
attention to the presence of strangers, and under
the then existing rules of
the House, the galleries
- were at once cleared.
Perth Morning Herald warns the Sand-
groping people, like a naked Solomon Eagle crying
in a doomed city, that Federation will put an end
to border customs —which it certainly will—and
therefore land and income taxes will have to be
levied to make up the deficiency. The working-
class of the West should gum that warning in the
limited amount of hat the depression has left to
it. On the authority of the anti-Federal paper,
Federation is the one blessed institution which
will make the rich ’groper pay a fair share of the
public burdens.
The ’99 Queensland elections again empha-
sise the oft-repeated assertion that the backblocks
and the mining towns (one might add now—the
sugar towns) are the strongholds of Queensland
Liberalism. The towns dependent on Govern-
ment sops and patronage, such as Maryborough,
Ipswich and Townsville, are rotten to the core.
Also, the unspeakable cockatoo constituencies are
nearly all ardent admirers of Oily Jimmy’s policy,
while the Jap and Chow vote of Cooktown and
Thursday Island always goes straight for the
“plopputty Guv’ment.”
South Australia will have a great treble
event on 29th April—general election, referendum
on the Commonwealth Bill, and referendum on
the question of household suffrage for the Upper
House. The latter is to be taken on the Assem-
bly’s authority only; it is that body’s private
appeal to its own constituents for an expression of
opinion. This is the “outrage on the Consti-
toosh’n ” about which the Council stopped supplies
late in December ; but, as the Council got down
off its dignity and resumed supplies again early in
January, no serious breakage was done.
Nothing is so small that the anti-Federal
gang of N.S.W. can’t make it a grievance against
the Commonwealth Bill. Lyne’s latest curse, at a
recent gathering of N.S.W’. Chamber of Manu-
facturers, was that the late Premiers’ Conference
met in a “ hole-and-corner place ” ( i.e ., it met at
Melbourne, the largest city in Australasia; and
the largest, save Rio Janeiro and Buenos Ayres,
south'of the line), and that, though the terms of
the agreement arrived at have been published,
there is no verbatim report of all the conversation
and refreshments and cigars that happened while
that agreement was being talked over.
The enemy put a tremendous -weapon in
Premier Seddon’s hands over the Old Age Pension
system, and by the time Maoriland election comes
along, next December, it will probably begin to
realise the fact. Largely through the opposition
of the Tory party, the Old Age Pension law was
passed for three years only ; if it isn’t renewed in
1902 the whole thing will stop automatically.
And all the aged multitude, and all the multitude
which expects to be aged later on, remembers that
if it puts Russell in office the anti-Pensi'on crowd
will almost certainly be oil top when 1902 comes
round. The biggest cash inducement to vote for
Seddon that the country could have was supplied
by his opponents.

The jibe goes that you can always tell
when Premier George Reid sees danger ahead ;
for then he sits down at Parliament House and
affably plays dominoes with James M'Gowan,
head (or, rather, leader) of the Labor-party.
More or less actual conversation over the domino-
table between the pair (as reported by the malig-
nant Sydney D.T.): —
“ You must stick to me, Jim, now."
“ I will, George.”
“We’re getting into a tight place, Jim.”
“We are, George.”
“ But we have always been good friends, Jim ? ”
“ We have, George.”
“ You will stick to me, Jim ?”
“ I will, George."
The game goes on, then the silence is broken.
“ Things are beginning to look black, Jim ? ”
“They are, George.”
“ But you’ll stick to me, Jim? ”
“ I will, George.”
There is a shuffle. The game is slow. One of the players
is beginning to nod.
“ Are you down, George ?”
“Down ! (Wakes up in a fright). Don’t startle me
like that, Jim. Say, ‘ Can’t you go ! ’ It’s ominous, that
• down.’ But you’ll stick to me, Jim ? ”
“ Trust in me, George.”
“ I will, Jim.” (Sleeps).

“Junior Counsel ” on “ desertion ” and Bul-
letin’s note on Belton (Vic.) divorce-case :
The almost universal definition of “ desertion ” embraces
as one of its necessary elements “ the putting an end to
an existing state of cohabitation.” Hood, J., showed his
courage in 1895 (though then only a light-weight, in a
technical sense, of course—not weighed down by long ex-
perience on the Bench) by administering a heavy cross-
counter to this definition (as a hard-and-fast rule). In
Johnston v. Johnston (21 V.L.R.) he held that “deser-
tion ” was proved in case of man who seduced young girl,
married and left her, without living as man and wife.
The English courts have at last reached the same point
in the latest case (“latest” is a relative local term—we
are generally about three months behind actual English
decisions). Monsieur de Laubenque was a fascinating
Frenchman (who wasn’t a Frenchman, but a British
subject, from islands remote) with an (imaginary);
wealthy father in Paris and influential uncle to set him
up (a medical student) in large practice. The young
lady of 17, a Roman Catholic, failing to be sufficiently
overcome by these inducements to elope with him, he
married her at Registrar’s Office, drove with her to R.C.
Cathedral, married her there regularly, walked to church
door and left shortly after “ to legalise the marriage in
France.” Never cohabited with his “ wife.” After long
negotiations the Pope annulled the marriage —ratum sed
non consiimmatum— and a suit for divorce was brought.
Sir F. H. Jeunk, President of Probate and Divorce, again
got home on good, old, above-mentioned definition
(which is getting a bit “ out of form ”) by holding “ deser-
tion proved and granting a divorce.” In both these cases
the young “brides” had to prove “ willingness ” (not
“wishfulness ”) during desertion to enter on a state of
cohabitation. In neither case was “an end put to an
existing state of cohabitation.”
“ Melbourne ” on a deceased notable :
If our refulgencies go out as they have been doing this
week, we will have only farthing dips directly. Almost
in a day came news of the deaths of Hosie of the pies,
sec. Vasey, of the Gas Co., and Pastor Robinson (Presby-
terian), of St. Kilda. Hosie was a man who seemed to
lounge through life, like Book-Arcadian Cole, but these
are the sort that plan and carry out big things. Hosie’s
start was a mere transient Melb. appetite, a furore, for
Scotch pies, of which hardly any are eaten now ; but
Hosie knew when he had gorged the city stomach, and
was off on other specs, like all men of business genius,
who never look for birds in last year’s nests. So he was
gone upon Turkish baths, and all kinds of timely and
ephemeral fads in the hotel line. Nothing Big Clarke
ever did in charity is comparable to Hosie’s dab-down of
£9OOO years ago for the Homoeopathic Hospital, and the
public’ knew, or suspected, nothing of it till after his
death.
Angus and Robertson have prepared an
entirely new edition of Dyson’s “Rhymes from
the Mines,” printed from new type on fine paper,
and in the best style of the firm’s previous publi-
cations. The destruction of the stereos by fire
rendered necessary the re-setting of the volume.
“ Sherlock Holmes ” outrivailed. Any
reader wanting news of a missing relative, or a
copy of a will, certificate of birth, etc., or needing
a delicate inquiry made, had better write to J. W.
Holdswokth, the leading Melb. inquiry agent.*
“ Snakejuice ” writes :
What’s Reid’s little game now? People were under the
impression that, to pass the present Bill, Reid would
appoint to N.S.W. Upper House men who were in favor
of it. If that is so, how comes it that A. J. Gould is one
of the elected? The opposition of Mr. Gould to the last
Bill was one of the main factors in his defeat at the
general election. He opposed the Bill publicly and
privately. If the present Bill is much the same as the
last, how in the name of all that wriggles can Reid expect
support from Gould !
The Bulletin simply assumes that Gould has
slewed round like Brunker—and Reid. In any
case it is practically certain that Gould promised
to vote for the Bill before he was made an M.L.C.
and got his free railway pass returned to him.
This paper doesn’t mind much where votes come
from, and it will take even Gould’s. If that
icy person likes to get on the stool of repentance
when Brunker has finished weeping on it, why
shouldn’t he ?

“ Biter Bit ” : Re Sydney Safe Deposit, a friend
of mine, going: on holidays, got even with an enterprising
burglar recently. He took his plate, etc., to the Deposit,
and posted this notice inside his safe:—“My friend, I
regret you should have all this trouble for nothing. I
anticipated your call, and have left my valuables at the
Sydney Safe Deposit, in Ash-street, back of Paling’s. Try
there. I have also arranged so that when you open the
safe-door it warns the police. Au revoir.” The latter
was bluff, but I guess the e.b. scooted some.*
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Randvvick steeplechases are generally rather
sorry shows, and last Saturday’s race was no exception.
Fivrf started and dashed off at ’bus pace ; two promptly
fell*. The other three blundered along with big intervals
between, and Mangare eventually won by the length of
the straight. Parnell ran off at the last fence, hut poor
okhCardigan was so far behind that the jock, on Parnell
hail time to bring him back, jump the fence, and then
beat him home by about a furlong. Mangare is a Maori-
lander, who falls if he is taken at a fence at any bat, but
a nag doesn’t want pace to win a Sydney Steeple.
* * * *
Merloolas very easily beat Lee-Metford and
Clarion intheAJ.C. Plate at Randwick on Saturday,
running the 3 miles in the very fair time of 5.31)4mm.
The pace set by Lee-Metford just suited the big, game
Queenslander, as it kept him from fighting for his head,
and at the finish he had it all his own way. Merloolas is
one of the hardest horses to ride in a race, as he won’t stay
on the rails, and fights all the way to get out wide. In
his work he has to be galloped right out on the outside
rails. ,
• • * *
Fairy Prince’s performance (2.35) in the Place
Handicap at Randwick, on Saturday, equals Survivor's
record for the mile-and-a-half. Up to a very short time
before, the raee started it was uncertain whether F.P.
wo'uld compete, as no “punter” was available and the
amount necessary to qualify had to be arranged for.
Consequently, the trainer had little or nothing on the
animal of which he is lessee, and, according to agreement,
must now part up half the prize-money to the owner.
Fairy Prince could have been bought a while hack for £2O.
Fairy Prince is by Stockdale out of a Stafford
mare, and hails originally from Camden Park, Macarthur-
Onslow’s homestead on the southern line. None of the
breed were ever raced before owing to the Macarthurs’
rooted objection to the sport.
The industry that flourishes most on Randwick
flat just now is “ticket-snatching.” The “snatcher”
simply stands beside a “ book,” and when a lucky inves-
tor comes along to collect his winnings, said snatcher
barefacedly grabs his ticket and sprints off, and, if bettor
chases him, he gets tripped up by the push and badly
trodden on. Apparently the snatchers have got the
officials and police quite beaten, for although writer was
on the flat each day of the recent meeting, and saw
several tickets grabbed, only one of these highwaymen (a
darkey) was removed from the course—and he was back
again within half-an-hour.
• * • •
An old-time Bulletin prediction that a pony
would yet win one of the big Australian handicaps, has
been fulfilled by the victory of Diffidence in Sydney Cup.
She is the smallest animal that has ever won a two-mile
raee in Australia in first-class company. Her dam is the
Maribyrnong Handicap winner, Etraweenie, and her
grandam the grand old mare Nellie, who gave Trenton to
the turf.
• * • *
The true inwardness of the purely-malevolent
and venal portion of an attack lately made upon “Tatter-
sall ” Adams down South will shortly become very
apparent to the public. Negotiations are proceeding
between a certain crowd and the Queensland Govt, for
the re-establishment of the sweep business at Brisbane.
Should permission be gained, certain enterprising people
with money behind them mean to have a big cut-in at
the business.
* * * «
Truth Norton’s allegation that duplicate
marbles have been drawn from “Tattersall’s ” barrel was
possibly an off-shoot from an incident in the St. Alban’s
stud lottery. When the numbers of the 130 prizes were
published Mr. James Gregg, Melb. manager for James
M’Ewan and Co., found himself the winner of a brood
mare. His ticket corresponded with-the number of the
animal on the advertised prize-list, but he got no notifi-
cation of his luck from "Tatt.” On putting his prize up
for sale Mr. Gregg found that somebody in Adelaide had
received “ Tatt.’s” telegram informing him that he had
drawn the same animal. Gregg sought a clear explana-
tion of the matter from head-quarters, but George Adams
merely gave him authority to sell his prize, so he sold it,
and to this day Mr. G. remains in the dark as to what was
done with the other man. He supposes that the other
man ranked also as owner of the horse, and made his own
terms with “ Tatt.,” who, at that rate, had to pay the price
of the twice-won prize out of his own pocket. Mr. G.
doesn’t know whether his ticket won the horse, and the
wrong man got the telegram, or whether the right man
gob the telegram, and the wrong number got into print.
He believes there was the mistake of a “0” in one case
or other : and, anyway, he swears by the integrity of
“ Tattersall.”

The good luck of Bobadil in having had
“ nothing to beat undeniable. When his wins this
season were not in w.f.a. events he rarely had more than

And who were the runners-up? George Frederick twice ;

booming of Bobby is absurd. Here are Cocos's per-
formances from the time when he met W. R. Wilson’*
vaunted “ crack ” in Caulfield Guineas, giving him 41bs.
and finishing second. He won the Eclipse Stakes (1 mile)
at Caulfield, beating Wait-a-Bit and The Chief, who
carried lots of money, and dead-heated for second place.
Cocos had 71bs. the worst of it with the tw r o older horses.
Then he finished well up in Caulfield Cup race, after being
knocked out of all chance of winning by a catastrophe.
His next performance w 7 as the Derby win. Then he ran
third—only a length behind—to Grafter and Wait-a-Bit
for Melbourne Cup. At same meeting he beat Bobadil
and finished a head behind Lee-Metford (in receipt of
lOlbs.) for the valuable Spring Stakes, Lee-Metford being
disqualified for boring. And then Cocos broke down. .
Bobadil as a “ great” horse reads like a joke in
the light of Carbine’s record. Carbine met everything at
w 7 .f.a. or at a big disadvantage—and some of the horses
that finished second or third to him w 7 hen he won the
stake were ten times, Aberoorn four times, Dread-
nought five times, Lochiel twice. Highborn twice, Car-
iyon and The Admiral once each. Melos, it must be re-
membered, was good enough to win the A.J.C. Derby
and Leger, and carry 9st. 21b. into second place in the
Australian Cup which Dreadnought won. Highhorn won
Svdney Cup, carrying 9st., in the same season that
Carbine gave him 531 b. and a beating for the Melb. Cup.

“Scotty ” : The mare Thelma, which performed
so well at the late big N.S.W. show, is atout 11 years old.
When exhibited b.v her former owner she was not even
looked at, although a year younger, and in such con-
dition and coat that sports wrote to enquire on what she
was fed. But there’s a difference between a country
Lutcher and a “ toff,” and between Thelma, a fancy name,
and Myrtle, her late rather common one; and perhaps
there’s a difference in the judges, the judging, and ce
tainly in the judgment.

igain.

A large field of smart hacks went out for a
Dash Handicap at a minor M.L. meeting, March 16 (writes
a Bulletin correspondent), a mare with the Maori appel-
lation of Mongonui bolting away and winning by 15
lengths. The crowd looked on open-mouthed. It then
transpired that Mongonui was by Carbine—Moonga, and
full sister to the one-time much-fancied Melb. colt Bun-
dook, raced by the Millers.

Little Charles Dawson, the billiardist who
travelled to Australia last year on the strength of an
allegation that Roberts was afraid to “take him on” at
the “legitimate” game, has been duly taken on and
defeated by 1814 in a game of 18,000 up. The big,
grey - haired champion seemingly held his oppo-
nent safe throughout the race, but the cable re-
port doesn’t make it certain that the victory
would have been much more decisive but for gate-
money considerations. Probably it would. The match
was purely a gate-money business as far as Roberts was
concerned, whereas Dawson had every inducement to do
his darndest. A defeat of the remarkable old champion,
on level terms of one kind or another, is only a matter of
time, of course, but the time is not just yet. The history
of English billiards championship has been Roberts
to’ujo wrs, up to now. First there came the spot-stroke
discovery by the original Roberts, who had no rivals
among the men of his age. About 29 years ago, when
Roberts pere was only a little on the wane, the billiard
world heard to its astonishment that young William Cook
was a better spot stroker than the old professor, and that
John Roberts, junior, would be prepared to play Cook as
soon as the championship had gone out of the family.
Cook beat the original Roberts. Then young Roberts
came along and showed himself to be quite as much of a
champion as Cook. Their proficiency at the spot-stroke
grew wearisome, so a new championship table was devised
—a table that made long sequences of spot-strokes im-
possible. Roberts proved himself best man on this abor-
tion of a table, which he subsequently repudiated as a
failure. The public didn't care for the game under its
new-fangled championship conditions. Roberts then
became a law unto himself. He dropped the spot-stroke,
as being too dull and mechanical a means of exhibiting
his supremacy, and devoted himself to the all-round
game.
After taking them all down at the plain all-round
game, Roberts laid himself out for the cultivation of the
“push” stroke (which had always been sanctioned) in
“ nursery-cannons.” His supremacy as a nursery-cannon
pusher enabled him to give away 40 or 50 per cent, of the
points to anybody at the spot-barred game on an ordinary
English billiard table. He bossed the show for years, and
then young Dawson and others somewhat meanly con-
spired to reduce the gap between themselves and the old
man by declaring the “ push” to be a foul stroke, though
there had never been any doubt that—to quote an axiom
coined bv Tiie Bulletin—" he plaveth best who pusheth
best.” Dawson challenged Roberts to play him with the
push barred, which meant that the champion of 28 years’
standing had to re-practise parts of the ali-round game at
which he had grown rusty whilst he was sticking so
clos-ly to the push cannons. They took away his trump-
card, but he beat them all the same.
* m m *
A humorous row started in Perth W.C.T.U.
over the decision of the executive of that body, in start-
ing a “ Leisure Hour Club, ’ to include a billiard-room
amongst the club diversions. Idea was to snatch male
brands from the burning—or, rather, from the drowning
—at the pub., by inducing them to play billiards under
the eye of the W.C.T.U. ladies, and in a place where
nothing stronger than ginger-ale was sold. The billiard-
table was started, hut it burst up Perth branch of the
W.C.T.U., which disbanded as a protest, when the mem-
bers, according to published account, “ in sorrow and
regret removed their white ribbon-bows.” The “Y”
Branch, otherwise the young ’uns, however, supported
the billiard-table and let the old ’uns slide ; and, despite
the latter’s disbandment, the W.C.T.U., led by an aggres-
sive propagandist named Mrs. Clarke, still flourishes in
Perth.
The billiard-table question soon found its way
into the Perth papers, and became the chief silly-season
topic, which “Pro Bono Publico,” “Constant Reader,”
and other well - known writers, emerged from their
shells to discuss. The correspondence, according to
latest files, was still being carried on with vigor. Con-
tributors were divided into two sharply-defined sections,
one of which pictured the billiard-table as a sanctified
implement of pastime, while the other regarded it as an
engine of the devil.
* * * *
Wood-chopping contest at Agricultural Society’s
Gronn Is (Sydney), last week, drew a big crowd (8000) and
created great interest on a wet afternoon; Twenty-three
brawny, tawny-pelted bushmen, with axes keen as
razors, competed. Among them were four brothers—
Ernest, Caspar (last year’s winner and runner-up respec-
tively) William arid J. J. Heckenberg (Liverpool, N.S.W.)
all fine, sturdj, upstanding sons of the soil, who were
confidently expected by their supporters to cut up the
£5O prize-money. But M’Kindlty,' erst of Victoria and
Tasmania, and now of Clarence River, split the quarter
and won in 2min. 12|sec., with William, Casper and
Ernest Heckenberg second, third and fourth, as named—
J.J. having gone down in the fourth heat before the ulti-
mate winner. Every man among the four placed was
over 6ft. high, and the way each handled his implement
was a revelation. Logs were spotted gum 16in. through
and about 4ft. round. MTvinolty’s style was the easiest.
While others knocked chips off he hewed V-shaped
chunks. Writer heard, on good authority, that M’Kinolty
has a record of lmin. 32sec. for a similar contract.
’Tis said M‘Kinolty originally came from the
Land of the Wooden Ham, where he earned fame with
his axe, and settled in Huon district (Tas.). Has since
won championship of Victoria (under-hand cutting), 1888
and (two 6ft.-girth-logs) 1896; big handicap and cham-
pionship Tas. (1896), and N.S.W. championship at Maclean
(C.R.) May 24 last. M’Kinolty works left-handed and
takes to under-cuts best.
* * * *
Sturdy Jack Conlon, aggressive forward of the
Pirates F.C., and Ned Burden, virtual lightweight cham-
pion pug. of N.S.W., met at Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney),
Saturday night last, for a £4O-purse and wager £5O
a-side. Upwards of 1000 people squeezed into the hall,
and sweltered there over two hours without an inch of
elbow-room: you couldn’t have cut the “air” with an
axe. Hundreds, unable to gain admission, stood outside,
eagerly gulping down whatever information the door-
keeper passed out concerning the progress of the fight.
Fight! It Was anything but that. Conlon stood inches
over Burden, had a stone or more the better of the
weights, and was in superb condition ; but after nearly
outing his man in the third round, and having him
tottering in the sixth, he refused to mix it except on rare
occasions, being content to keep as far away from the
enemy as the hempen bound iry of the 24ft. square would
permit. The crowd |ollied Conlon unmercifully. “Give
Burden a bike and then he might catch the big fellow,”
they screeched, and every time Conlon advanced away
from the centre he was hoo-hooed, and advised to look
for an engagement at the Tivoli. In the 16th round
Conlon badly damaged his opponent’s left eye with two
heavy left-hand jabs. At the termination of the 20th
three minutes the referee declared the match a draw, and
thus ended an exceedingly tame contest, rendered more
so by the fact that it had been the talk o’ town for weeks.
* * * *
The much-talked-of Conlon-Burden fight, Satur-
day night, last, demonstrated the extreme poorness of
N.S.W. boxing form. “Gentleman” George Dawson,
“ Shadow ” Maher, or Griffo, as they were in early part of
, present decade, could go through the lot and think no
rtiore of the effort than -they would of an ordinary spar.
* * * *
Novice boxing-tournaments have been the vogue
in Sydney for some time, and writer has experienced
much amusement in watching said novices weighed at a
certain Sydney club. Before first round begins, spring-
balance scales are brought in with much flourish and
suspended from a beam across the roof. Referee gravely
points to a piece of paper pasted on face of scales, and
informs the spectators that any man who pulls the indi-
cator lower than the position marked on the paper exceeds
the limit and will have to stand down ; but nobody ever
does stand down, though all get perilously near the brink.
Wonder if it’s possible for the indicator to pass that scrap
of paper? Writer has seen apparently-llst. men taking
part, in fist, tournaments, and heard their fellow-com-
petitors complain about it. Novices are scarce, but
their rough-and-tumble style of fighting draw's, and up to
date promoters do not split straws over letting ’em in at
a lb. or two over the limit.
* • * *
Veteran bruiser Abe Hicken is now in Sydney.
Writer remembers Abe’s great battle with L. Foley at
Echuea, on the Murray, 20 years ago. No Australian
fistic duel, before or since, caused such a sensation.
Police were badly bafHed. Thousands of pounds were
lost and won, and the winner of the match, Foley, got a
royal reception at every station when returning to Melb.
by train. Later—upon arriving in Sydney—he was pre-
sented with 1000 sovs. at the old Vic. Theatre, amid a
scene of great enthusiasm. 31 years since Hicken fought
and heat Maguire at New York (U.S.A.) for the light-
weight championship of Yankeedom, breaking the
American’s jaw. Abe is 54, while Foley is six years
younger.
• • • •
Looks as if Griffo, the incomparable—perhaps
the greatest boxing freak the world has yet produced—
will end his days in a lunatic asylum. No sooner is he
out of gaol than in he goes again. They released him at
Chicago (U.S.A.), Jan. 4, after a long stretch, and he
straightway went on a wild jag, and was arrested again.
Half-a-dozen officers had a bad time before securing their
man.
* * * *
Delegates of N.S.W. Amateur Swimming Asso-
ciation have discussed “Mud” Bishop’s lmin. 1 3-ssec.
performance for 100yds. at Fitzroy Dock (Sydney), last
week, and, though some stoutly advocated allowing it to
go as a world’s-record for a straightway course, others
just as strenuously objected on account of the strong
wind that blew down the water at the time it was put up.
Ultimately, it was decided to refer the matter to the
’Ome swimming authorities, and ask how they would act
under the circumstances.
* * * *
Australasian champion-swimmer Freddy Lane
caught the England-bound Orient liner Omrah at Melb.,
but prior to going aboard had a 100yds. flutter over a
straightway course in Hegarty’s baths, St. Kilda, with the
Vic. champion sprinter, Pearson, and beat him by 7 or
Byds. in lmin. 4 3-ssec. Smart going, considering the
speedy little Sydneyite had been travelling all the night
before without sleep.
• » « *
The drowning of Melb. yachtsmen by the foun-
dering of the Queenie on Easter Sunday again emphasises
the riskiness of yachting in Port Philip, which is prac-
tically an inland sea. There are ten times the number of
capsizes in Sydney Harbor, yet the drowning of an ex-
perienced yachtsman is there very rare. The southei’n
fatalities are due partly to the immense sailing area and
partly to the comparative incompetence of southern
yachtsmen. Half those who have strutted in brass
buttons since the arrival of Gov. Brassey don’t know a jib-
sheet from peak-halliards, Not so, however, with the
drowned Clarks—all yachtsmen from infancy.
* * * *
Young Johnson, one of the victims of the re-
cent yacht disaster in Hobson’s Bay, had evidently
reached the pier at Werribee, because his hands clutched
a quantity of mussels, which must have been torn from
the pier piles when he sought to support himself after
his long swim. It was then probably that he was attacked
by sharks. Sad as the affair was all round, Johnson’s
case seems hardest, by reason of his almost escape.
» * « *
Mark Foy has given Joe Donnelly an order
for a boat for England to meet the Maid of Kent. She is
to be of the Maid’s class a very different sort to the old-
time Irex. The difficulty will be, however, in getting a
crew to sail her.
« * • * *
Bike-raping bookmakers in Victoria are now
appearing with “Licensed by the L.V.W.” on their
leather bags. The League’s recent meeting at Melb.
Exhibition track revealed some of these half-fledged Ikeys
as bawlers who had paid a five “ bob ” registration fee for
the privilege of laying the odds in “ a place within the
meaning of the Act.” So much for the League’s alleged
determination to check roguery on wheels. The unlaw-
ful parasite, having paid his footing, is practically licensed
to rob the public. Anyway, he doesn’t meet his liabilities
in sundry cases, and the League which pockets his dollar
leaves the young blackguard to slink away rejoicing. It
ought to be about time that lawyer Robert Best cut the
connection between the Vic. Ministry and bicycle sport,
for if he remains a member of the League’s new licensing
board, the nickname of “ Five-Bob ” Best will stick to him
for ever.
Young Hack, who was chosen to play against
the Australian team in Adelaide, stands 6ft. 2in., and is
prophesied to be a future international player. He has
the distinction of havirig twice been dropped from Ade-
laide clubs, but now heads the S.A. averages in club
matches.
* * • *
Six and one-day bicycle races are the rage in
’Frisco. In a recent 24 hours’ go two men covered 459
miles, and the next two 455. The miserable wretches ride
the whole 24 hours w thout dismounting.
The Fremantle Strike.
The lumpers’ strike at Fremantle (W.A.) has
come to an end after the. Forrest Government
had made of itself nearly every kind of ass
that was possible in the circumstances. The
trouble began over a proposed reduction of
wages. It wasn’t a very large reduction, and,
in the reduced circumstances of Westralia, the
employes might possibly not have objected very
much, though even a small -reduction was a
serious matter to them ; the drop from Is. 3d.
to Is. per hour, and from 2s. to Is. Bd.
per hour for overtime, with the 2s. 6d. per
hour for Sunday work reduced to 2s. 3d.,
doesn’t look much, but when there is also a
grievous falling off in the number of hours’
work available each week it becomes serious.
Bub when the Lumpers’ Union asked the union
of steamship owners to meet and talk the
matter over, the latter haughtily refused to
recognise any union of employes, or to deal
with any such union, or to admit of its
existence, and then the row commenced in
earnest. It wasn’t so much a dispute over wages
as over the*right of the employes to combine
as well as the employers. Had the conference
been held the men might have given way over
the wages, but on the question of their right to
have a union and to have its existence recog-
nised there was no surrender. And the Govern-
ment, in its indecent haste to help the steam-
ship owners to cub down wages and destroy
unionism, made an unspeakable fool of itself.
It proceeded to reduce the wages of its truckers
on the same scale, or one closely resembling it,
and thereby identified itself with the owners.
In the midst of the shindy the German liner,
Essen, arrived at the port. The Government
would only allow cheap blackleg labor to put
cargo into its trucks, and there wasn’t enough
of it available. The agents of the Essen offered
to engage union labor, and. to load the trucks
at their own (not the railway department’s)
expense, and the offer was refused. Then the
German steamer departed with strange curses
and took most of its Fremantle cargo away with
it. Considering that W. A.’s cheap-labor Govern-
nienfc has been struggling for many months
to get Fremantle made a port of call for
the German mail-boats this cheerless reception
was woefully bad policy. Another weird
feature of the case is that while the lumpers
are W.A. citizens and taxpayers, and spend
their money in the provinces, the Steamship
Owners Association is almost purely an outside
institution. All the shipping which W.A.
owns itself is barely one-third that of Tas-
mania and about one-twentieth that of N.S.W.
So the money saved by the owners off the
wages of the local men would be taken out of
the country, and the gross income of W.A.
proportionately reduced; and the Forrest
Government was indirectly helping to diminish
the resources of its own country for the benefit
of outsiders. In the end the owners so far
climbed down as to meet the employes’ union
and arbitrate, and the greater part of the pro-
posed reduction was accepted. So the Govern-
ment, which stumbled clumsily into the busi-
ness without knowing exactly how it was doing
it, now stumbles out again, and the axe is care-
fully interred all round.
Last week three sentences of death were
passed at N.S.W. assizes, and in none of them, it
is safe to say, will the sentence be carried out.
Which is indeed farcical.
Sydney D.T. observes that the number of
applicants for the post of Prof, of Physics at
Sydney University is very small. But, on the
other hand, it seems rather startling that 20
scientific men in England, of whom 19 are bound
to be disappointed, should be prepared to come
such a distance for so moderate a salary and into
a narrow sphere where there is always the danger
of stagnation—an ever-present fear to a Univer-
sity lecturer in a sense that does not apply to an
astronomer or a botanist. To the latter a new
and wide field like Australia is practically invalu-
able. Out of these 20 names, three have been
selected and sent to Sydney University Senate
for final choice.
Groom at Toowoomba :
He had seen the place grow from a village of 150 people
to a town of 13 or 14 thousand people, As he looked back
on the long vista of years gone by he remembered that
there were now lying in God’s Acre many 7 of their rude
forefathers who had risked ... to support him at his
first election.”
Every few years Toowoomba comes to the con-
clusion that sinuous old W. H. G. is an oleaginous
humbug. As soon, however, as William comes
to the “God’s Acre” and “rude forefather”
business the lachrymose town rises up suddenly,
embraces him with unspeakable emotion, and
votes him into the House again.

Legal Notice.
OTICE OF TESTAMENTARY
BEQUESTS.
The Late JAMES WEIR
of Saint Clouds Blackrockin the County of Dublin
Esquire by his Will dated 12th of October 1893
bequeathed the following legacies amongst others
namely :—One thousand pounds to Mrs. Rachel
Sandiland; One thousand pounds to the children
of the late Andrew Brown ; One thousand pounds
to each of the seven children of Testator’s wife’s
sister. Isabella Fallow, namely James Fallow,
William Fallow, John Fallow, Gavin Fallow,
Janet Fallow, Maty Fallow, and Agnes Fallow.
One thousand pounds to Mrs. Grace Barr,
formerly Steele. One thousand pounds in equal
shares amongst the children of John Steel son of
-Mary Steel; One thousand pounds to the issue
living at the death of the Testator of James Weir
who died in his lifetime ; One thousand pounds to
each of the six children of Testator’s brother
Thomas Weir deceased, namely James Weir,
Francis Weir, Andrew Weir, Agnes Weir, Eliza-
beth Weir, and Margaret Weir.
The Testator recited that sonie of the persons
who might be entitled to legacies under his Will
were, the Testator believed, resident in America,
Australia and New Zealand, and it contained the
following clause: “I hereby declare that every
person entitled to a legacy Under this my Will
shall within a period of three years from the day
of my death send to my Trustee or Trustees a
claim (in writing) for such legacy and any person
who shall not send in such claims as aforesaid
shall absolutely forfeit all benefit which he or she
might have otherwise claimed under this my Will
and the Legacy hereinbefore bequeathed Jb him or
her shall be dealt with as a lapsed legacy.” And!
the Testator directed his Trustees as soon as con-
veniently might be after his death to cause
advertisements to be inserted in newspapers
requiring all persons entitled to legacies to send in
their claims. The Testator died 30th October,
1898, and said Will and one Codicil were on the
18t.h November, 1898, duly proved by Sir William
Findlater, Robert Bell, James Anderson Merry
the Executors and Trustees thereof.
Now all such of above-named Legatees as have
not already done so and all other (if any) persons
claiming to be Legatees or to be beneficially entitled
under the said Will and Codicil are hereby re-
quired to send in the said Executors and Trustees
at the office of the undersigned solicitors claims
(in writing) and the necessary proofs of identifica-
tion, and the Trustees will at the expiration of a
period of three years after the death finally wind
up the Estate and distribute the then remaining
assets of the Testator without reference to any
claims except those which shall have been sent
in within said period. The Executors have col-
lected and realised a very large proportion of the
Testator’s assets and they have paid the Testator’s
debts and thus will shortly be in a position to pay
the above mentioned legacies and to distribute
their Testator’s Estate (except of course such part
of the residuary property as it may be necessary
to retain three years).
The Executors believe that they already possess
the addresses of the abovenanled legatees but
for extra precaution and in pursuance of their
Testator’s directions publish this advertisement
and particularly request each named or indicated
legatee to send in a formal written claim without
further delay.
Dated at Sydney this 10th day of April, 1899.
W. W. BILLYARD ANDREWS and MOSE-
LEY, Solicitors, 12 Castlereagh-street, Sydney,
Agents for WILLIAN FINDLATER and Com-
pany, Solicitors for the Executors* 53 Dame*
street, Dublin.
At Poverty Point.
Congratulations to Mr. George Coppin, who celebrated
bis 80th birthday on April Bth.
The author of “ The Belle of New York ” has a curious
way of disarming criticism. Upon the programme is
printed: “In extenuation —the author begs to say that
whatever the play may be, it is all that is claimed for it.’
The engagement of Henrietta Watson for Miladi, in
“The Three Musketeers,” is an inspiration. Mrs. Brown-
Potter looks the part, but Miss Watson has the very
style for it. One fancies Cecil Ward as Aramis—compare
Maurice Leloir’s splendid illustrations to Dumas’ master-
piece and you have Cecil Ward looking at you from the
page. But what of Athos, Porthos, and D’Artagnan ?
Thi- e is no ideal D’Artagnan on Australian soil just now,
and without a first-rate D’Artagnan what would the
“ T n ee Musketeers” be ? Besides, imagination demands
a d ishing fellow to play up to such a Miladi as Miss
Watson must surely be.

ample and recoup his losses touring.”

During the last night of the “ White Heather” at Melb.
T.lt., as soon as Lady Godiva began to unrobe, there
arose from the front row of stalls an elderly and obvious
back-blocker, who advanced to the end of the orchestra
and, resting his chin on his arm, gloated for a full five
minutes, drawing meanwhile long and very audible
breaths of unspeakableness.
The hilarious chorus of “Ta-ra-ra-boom-de-ay,” which
was in everybody’s mouth a few years ago, is
almost extinct now. Still it isn’t quite forgotten. The
other day a sivagman, perishing of thirst 70 miles north
of Oodnadatta, on the great lone north road leading to
the centre of Australia and Port Darwin, scratched the
ri liculous old shout on his billy—and then died. Why
any man should want to “ Ta-ra-ra-boom ” in such hard
circumstances as these no one is ever likely to know.
Dear Bulletin. —A paragraph appeared in a recent
issue attributing to me the designs for certain furniture
used in “ Under the Red Robe.” Let me disclaim the
authorship. The designs were taken from “Pugin’s
Examples,’’and were admirably executed by Mr. Hassan.
—H. Desbrowe-Annear.

John Plummer to The Bulletin “ As the individual
responsible for the introduction of Clement Scott to
London Figaro, of which I was working editor, let me
say that whatever fault may be found with his later
dramatic criticism, in those days they were the most
honest and outspoken in London, and did much. to get
rid of the wishy-washy style formerly in vogue. Both on
London Figaro and Weekly Dispatch he had a perfectly
free hand, and only once within my experi*-nce had I
occasion to expunge a sentence of his. It led to a row
between us, but saved the paper from a lawsuit. As
regards giving lectures in Australia, I have already
advised him to the contrary, and reminded him of the
failure of Arthur Sketchley, our mutual friend, who was
disgusted to find Australians unable to appreciate his
creation of ‘ Mrs. Brown, and her Adventures in the New
Cut and other London localities.’ ”

Will Whitburn begun as a little boy negroio performer.
Then he became groom to Dr. Gunst, the eminent
homoeopath. The favor bestowed on him at the Temper-
ance Hall led to his adopting the regular stage. Pony-
racing is his hobby, wherefore he never appears at
matinees. He has held every Melb. engagement as long
as he liked, but Sydney did not care ‘for him.
J. C. Williamson and Brough have to pay income-tax
to the Vic. and N.S.W. Govts, on the earnings of Gilbert
and Sullivan, Pinero, and other dramatic authors whbse
works they produce here. The English author insists on
the fees, and as the income-tax has to be paid by some-
body it has to come out of the managers’pockets. But
it is rough that these should have to pay tax on Income
they certainly don’t enjoy. They are really taxed on
their payments, not on their earnings.
Melb. newspapers, reflecting severely on Robert
Brough’s ill-success in the Bijou and Princess’s, gave
themselves away, since throughout the season they
cheerfully reported “large and enthusiastic” audiences.
Local theatre-goers now stand charged with the offence
of neglecting dramatic talent and clever plays, and dis-
couraging a manager of discrimination and ability by
persistently crowding bis theatres to the doors, and
showering rapturous applause upon , his people and his
plays.
Dampier was playing Hamlet at Adelaide Royal t’other
night, and, when he came to Yorick’s skull, in the grave-
yard sc-ne, a dog jumped upon the stage, gazed in-
quiringly at the property-skull, and went off disgusted.
The quadruped had mistaken it for a real bone.
“C.C.” : A Sydney daily expresses surprise that Rig-
nold does not make Othello begin his wild career of
jealousy somewhat earlier in the play. But, histrionically
correct or not, Othello’s unsuspiciousness isn’t half so
startling as Desdemona’s later on. Judging by her shrieks
during the operation, Desdemona didn’t at ail want to be
smothered. And considering that the custom of the time
was to knife or smother a faithless wife without troub-
ling a divorce-court, the wonder is that even the
absurdly-innocent young thing could have gone to bed as
she was bidden, without first locking the door and push-
ing the chest of drawers against it to keep her lord and
master out. Thoughts of this sort obtruded "themselves
during the play, and sounded even louder than the gilt
buttons which showered off Cassio in the last act when he
was dragged off the stage trying hard not to smile.
“ The Divorce Court has just robbed,” writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent, “ Florence Ethel Sproule,
actress, of Sydney, of her ‘ Buckley’s chance ’ for the role
of Countess of Durham. In ’BB Florrie married Hon.
D’Arcy Lambtori, eighth son of the late Earl and oneof 13
‘ progenies ’ that blossomed in the ancestral hall within
16 years. St. Bartholomew’s, Pyrmont, cast its blessing
upon the twain and straightway Florrie became Flossie
and giddyish. There were several of them, but only three
figured in the invoice list —the last, one- Berridge, being
regarded by petitioner as an asset, and so was cast for
costs. The frail Antipod, in a long and doleful screed,
acknowledged all, and more : 1 they were strangers,’ etc.,
was the text of it, strongly embrocated with alcoholic
absorption and economical veracity. The present Lord
Durham is, if possible, more unlucky than the possible
future one,, his wife being a hopeless lunatic in perfeot
bodily health and an asylum."
That storm in Sydney Philharmonio tea-oup over the
wider privileges given to orchestral members was amus-
ing while it lasted. Full half the orchestral players are
accomplished prof, musicians, and the society stood the
risk of losing its most effective section for the sake of a
number of throaty, but very perky, tenors, muffled
bassos, sopranos whose efforts after A-flat are painfully
ineffectual, and would-be contraltos who imagine that a
low, rumbling noise somewhere near the diaphragm is
the ideal of the singer. Time that many wheezy warriors
dropped out of the Philharmonic.
Mme. Frances Saville has been complaining to N.Y.
Herald that her operatic-studies are disturbed by the
swarm of barrel-organists who infest the district where
she has her “flat.” At about the time when Frances
Saville uttered her bitter cry, brother Jules Simonsen was
getting arrested for burglary. Destitution drove him to
it, he said. Yet, if poor Jules had only thought to borrow
an organ, and grind it all day long outside his sister's
house, she would doubtless have bribed him handsomely
to go away. Lots of musicians earn a good living by
being paid to move on.

country with the original Dion Boucicault, and remained
awhile with the Firm.
The Voice of the M’Million.
Ex-Treasurer M'Millan on N.S.W. finance
under Premier Reid :
The finances of this country had never yet been placed
upon a sound foundation. During: the last three or, four
years .there never had been a solid, legitimate surplus;
and he would go further, and say that outside the ques-
tion of policy, whether of Freetrade or Protection, or a
particular kind of taxation, it ought to be the duty of
the press and of both sides of the House, and especially
should it be the duty of the great press of this country
which employed experts high in economical and financial
fame to weigh the public statement of their accounts,
and to see that everything was put fairly and clearly
before the public, and that they were not in any way
misled as to the real state of the finances. Now,
they might have a cash system, and still have a
very rotten system of finance. The key of finance was in
the loan estimates. If they put into the loan estimates
those things which ought to come out of legitimate
revenue, they were committing a fraud upon the finances
of the country. (Cheers). He said without any hesita-
tion that there never was a period in the history of this
country when the loan account was so used for purposes
that ought to be defrayed out of general revenue.
(Cheers). Then there had been a system introduced in
place of the one where they used to spend out of ordinary
revenue about £200,000 a year upon small matters; such
as post-offices, gaols, and so on. But a very ingenious
system has been invented to get that out of the revenue
by means of a thirty years’ sinking fund. Well,
that was a very nice thing for the man who came
in first, because everything was put over for 30 years—
(laughter)—and during the ordinary tenure of any Pre-
mier’s life, even if he remained 10 years Premier, he might
only have to pay a very small proportion of these loans.
He would only have to pay from year to year the interest
on each fresh loan of the previous year, and one-thirtieth
of the whole, and only a small aggregation arising out of
the others. Now, what was the use of this system ? In
a certain number of years the interest on these loans,
when the present Treasurer would be out of office, would
be equal to the total expenditure—(laughter)—and was it
not far better and far more straightforward to pay this
straight away from the revenue of each year, instead of
imposing upon the country a sort of bogus loan system
which was only meant to help the present, and to be
liabilities put forward on to posterity ? (Cheers).
The. Bulletin regards this as very kind and
complimentary of ex - Treasurer M'Millan.
For The Bulletin is the only paper in N.S.W.
which has constantly pointed out for four years
past that N.S.W. charges more of its ordinary
expenditure to loans than any other province ;
that this swindle has been growing worse and
more glaring every year since Reid took office ;
that Reid’s surpluses were purely bogus ; that
he has faked the public accounts by steadily
cutting down the Works expenditure out of
revenue till it now stands at only £597,000,
against £1,487,000 in ’9l, and by using loan
.money to make up the difference ; and that his
finance has been a sham and a lie all along.
And if ex-Treasurer M‘Millan had talked a
good deal more in this strain while he was in
politics it might have been a good deal better
for politics.
The Sabbath Paper’s Arithmetic.
Sydney Sunday Times’s latest anti-Federal in-
vention :
HOW THE TARIFF WILL WORK OUT.
One of the largest firms in Sydney has, in response to a
request, supplied the Sunday Times with the following
table, showing the difference in prices now charged the
public for the undermentioned classes of goods and the
prices that will be charged should the present Federal
Bill pass:—
Present Prices for Calicoes, Flannels, Flannelettes,
Blankets, Piece Dress Goods, Clothing, Shirts, Hosiery,
Mercery, Bootware, &c.
London Invoice .. .. .. ... £lOO 0 0
Importing Charges ... . .. .. .. 10 0 0
Cost in Warehouse .. .. .. .. 11000
Warehouseman’s Profit, say, 25 per cent. 27 10 0
Charged to Retailer .. .. .. .. 137 10 0
Retailer’s Profit, say, 331-3 per cent. .. 45 16 8
Charged to the Public £lB3 6 8
Equal to 83 1-3 per cent, above London invoice cost.
That is, in respect of all the articles enumerated above,
the public at present pay 83 1-3 per cent, above London
invoice cost. Now see below :
11.
Prices for above goods that will be charged under a
Federal tariff, when probably a 30 per cent, ad valorem
duty will be imposed—
London Invoice £lOO 0 0
Importing Charges 10 0 0
30 per cent, duty, equal to 33 per cent, on
Invoice Cost 33 0 0
Cost in Warehouse .. . . .. .. 143 0 0
Warehouseman's Profit, say, 25 per cent. 35 15 0
Charged to retailer 178 15 0
Retailer’s Profit, say, 331-3 per cent. .. 59 11 8
Charged to Public £238 6 8
Equal to 138 1-3 per cent, above London invoice cost, and
55 per cent, above the present prices charged to the
public.
In otherwords, for every pound’s worth purchased now
31s. will have to be paid under the Federal tariff.
Present Price—2os. Price after Federal Bill passed—3ls.
The Sunday Shellback makes even a worse
failure here than usual. For £238 6s. Bd. isn’t 55
per cent, above £lB3 6s. Bd. by a long way ; it is
£55 above it right enough, but that is only 30 per
cent. And the difference between the present
price and the one which the Sunday Shellback
assumes will be charged after Federation is not
the difference between 20s. and 31s. but between
20s. and 265. The Shellback paper simply adds
on ss. to make its case look better. Of course the
30 per cent, federal tariff on which the whole
thing starts is purely the Sabbath tipster’s
assumption, but that is a mere'side' issue ; the
wild joy of the thing lies in the assertion that
“ one of the largest firms in the city ” made these
awful arithmetical blunders. A paper which
doesn’t know 30 per cent, from 55 is. indeed a
beautiful guide on any financial problem.

Eloquent scrap of storm-reporting from
S.M. Herald :
Yesterday the sun rose thickly veiled by inky cloud-
banks, which at intervals broke, letting down a torrent of
rain for a few seconds now, and then minutes,' when,
driven by the tearing force of the storm, the dark masses
would fly across the sky, leaving the veil brighter and the
rain heavier than a good Scotch mist. . . . The
streets were then running “bankers high,” and the
driving rain to pedestrians defied their escape from its
moistening influences unless safe within the shelter of
closed doors.
The Herald would seem to have good reason for
its practice of discountenancing “descriptive”
reporting, if all its young men are liable to go
upon such wild bursts of incoherent English.
An anti-Federal meeting was held the
other day at Marrickville (Sydney), to roar prin-
cipally about the clause providing that the capital
should not be within 100 miles of Sydney, “ under
which Sydney was declared a politically-infected
spot.” When the provincial push first demanded
that the capital should be in N.S.W., and, there-
fore, not within 160 miles of Adelaide or Mel-
bourne, 400 of Hobart, or 1500 of Perth, it set the
germ rolling by declaring all these cities and all
the provinces attached politically-infected spots.
And after declaring 2,650,000 square miles of the
other people’s territory infected, how these fair-
minded and gentlemanly persons do curse ab
having about 10,000 sq. miles of their own terri-
tory similarly quarantined !
The late French President, Faure, it is no
secret, was seized with his sudden fatal-illness in
(if he did not actually die in) a maison-de~joie.
This circumstance, trumpeted forth by some of
the less “respectable” Parisian papers, is thus
discreetly touched on by the S.M. Herald’s Paris
correspondent: —
His appearance of late showed signs of failing health.
I may add that he sought relaxation in doubtful quarters.
The true version of his dying moments can scarcely be
given, but it is known to his intimate friends. He was
quite sensible for at least two hours, and asked M. Le
Gall, his private secretary, to send for a priest. To-mor-
row's religious obsequies are, therefore, quite logical, and
they will be very imposing. I have just returned from
Notre Dame, where the preparations are nearly com-
pleted. The vast Cathedral is draped in black and silver
from the door to the sanctuary. Two thrones are erected
on each side of the altar at which Napoleon I. was crowned
by Pius VII., and where so many official ceremonies have
taken place. The Archbishop of Paris will face the Papal
Nuncio, and a batch of French bishops in dazzling white
mitres will be in the stalls. The Ambassadors and repre-
sentatives of the Courts of Europe will occupy the first
row of seats. The Czar’s brilliant mission will surround
the new President of the Republic, and Her Majesty has
sent the Earl of Pembroke and Montgomery, Lord Steward
of the Household, with a special autograph letter to Mine.
Felix Faure, and a gorgeous wreath from the villa where
she will stay next month. The funeral will cost 150,000 fr.
The lying-in-state at the Elys6e is drawing tens of thou-
sands of not too respectable citizens.
Cardinal Moran, when he authorised that grand
function at St. Mary’s, Sydney, probably “ didn’t
know it was loaded. ”

My landed estates (on which I am entirely dependent

under 200 acres each ; and 262 oottasres, all with gardens,
of which most are let at Is. a week and under, some going
up to Is. Bd. a week ; 1400 allotments near YVycomhe, and
1000 village allotments in Bucks and in Lincolnshire, let
at from Bs. to 30s. an acre, the landlord paying all rates,
taxes, and outgoings.

“Hermes”: I was in the Maoriland rail-
way service once, and the Rakaia smash wakes up
recollections of the old happy-go-lucky days when
the M.L. railways were run oh principles that
were mostly incompetence, chaos and beer. OnCe
an engine left Burnside with 19 trucks and a
guard’s van for Caversham, and just as the engine
reached the top of the hill between the two stations
the couplings between the engine and the hist
truck broke, and the 19 trucks and guard s van
dashed back down hill towards Burnside, ihe
guard rushed to put on the brake, but his assistant
promptly knocked him down and sat. on him till
the train reached the bottom of the hill. Had he
put the brake on, in all probability the van would
have left the rails and killed them both. Ihe
guard, firmly believing that his mate’s brain had
turned, lay quiet in the hope that the show might
stop before the lunatic started to “ play up.
Writer saw guard two days after, and
his hair was still standing and plentifully
sprinkled with gray. Solemn fact! . . .
Another thrilling episode struck me at Milton.
The 10.45 a.m. was just about due, and I called
up Millburn, the next station, and asked him if
she had left. A torrent of blasphemy came
through the telephone, and when my staggered
senses had come back to me I was told that the
blanky driver had, in a moment of abstraction,
started off for Milton, after shunting a truck into
the siding, and left his train standing at the plat-
form. Went outside just as the engine sailed into
the station, stepped on to the footplate, and asked
the driver where his train was. The man looked
round, and when he saw the naked rails behind
him gaped in blank astonishment, and then asked
the fireman where the train was. And the flab-
bergasted fireman gaped back at him and gasped
out that he was somethinged if he knew. This
wasn’t exactly quite a common occurrence, but still
it wasn’t so astonishing, according to the old M.L.
methods, as one might think. In the old days, before
the progressive management of Maxwell had in-
troduced steam brakes, it was common for trains
to bolt down the steep inclines that are so frequent
in Maoriland. One greasy day, a train bolted
down Bushy Grade, just outside Palmerston
South, and the guard, turning to his terrified
brakeman, remarked, “ I hae me doots if she’ll
get tae tha bootom aall richt.” Then, raising his
eyes to the van-ceiling, “ Forgie us if what we dae
be nae pleasin’ in Thy sight.” And then he
calmly unhooked the side-chains, knocked out the
draw-bolt, thereby severing the van from the
train, and, by means of his brakes, soon brought
the van to a standstill. The train rushed on and
got through safely, and, when the guard was asked
to explain, he swore that the bolt broke, and that
he had neglected to fasten his side-chains before
leaving the terminal station. He was fined 10s.
for not having his side-chains fastened, and the
thing was forgotten till the brakeman told his best
girl the facts of the case. His best girl threw him
over by-and-bye, as best girls will do, and married
the fireman of the same train, to whom she told
the truth of the matter. The brakeman owned
up, and shortly afterwards there was a vacancy.

Tom Parker, the Zeehan publican with the
Peacock laugh, is standing down to allow miner
Matthews a fair run for Lyell (Tas.) Assembly
seat in the Labor interest. Matthews is a
vigorous A.M.A. official, and a willing barracker
for West Coast importance. But his two oppo-
nents, wobbly mediocrities with a “ property ”
following 1 , have the best of the contest, inasmuch
as of 2000 miners on the field only about 400 per-
sons are entitled to vote ! The ’97 rolls are to be
used ; of 800 persons whose names are on that
out-of-date affair, nearly 400 have since left the
district. So unless the 2000 miners chain the fat-
men to a stump on polling-day, the Labor man has
Buckley’s chance.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Mat,yon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir, —For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salis,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.

“ Hamilton is a man, and vve won’t vote against him,
but damn his politics,’’ was frequently heard at the poll-
ing-booths last Saturday.
Which about sums-up the case of genial Jack, the
white man who fights with Boodlewraith on be-
half of the black man.
The offer for military service in Samoa of
M.L. volunteers recalls the gimcrack jingoism
which Julius Yogel sought to introduce into
Maoriland politics as a means of rehabilitating
himself in the good graces of the Maorilanders,
15 years ago. Vogel preached the doctrine that
his province should be the suzerain state of all
the Pacific islands, and would have dearly liked
to get his fat forefinger into the Samoan pie. The
establishment of the protectorate over Raratonga
under F. J. Moss was the sole fruit of the
“Pacific policy,” over which the masterful trea-
surer had so dramatically spread-eagled himself
On one occasion, an Auckland Star reporter
interviewed Vogel at Waiwera —the hot springs
near Kawau —when Vogel unrolled quite a
Rhodesian scheme of annexation. The reporter,
satisfied that he had “ a sensation ” in his notes,
proceeded to transcribe in a quiet room in the
hotel. In about ten minutes Vogel’s orderly
sought out thp pressman, and said : “ Sir Julius s
compliments, and he will feel obliged if you will
refrain from referrin’g to the New Hebrides.” The
reporter sulkily promised to excise the reference —
one of the plums of the interview. A feyv hours
later Vogel sent requesting another excision, but
by this time the pressman was hastening with his
“ copy ” to the city, anxious to get his prize into
cold type. When he reached the office he found
that Vogel had wired to the editor, cautioning
him not to use a single line of the interview until
he had seen it. “ Wire it to me —collect 1” said
the telegram. And so the 5000-word interview
was telegraphed back to Vogel at Govt, expense,
to be bov/dlerised before publication.

The cable lately mentioned that our old
friend Talmage had retired from his ’Murkan
tabernacle in a hurry. Every true Christian must
sincerely hope that there wasn’t a lady in the
case.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a

Racked with Fearful Pains
TOO GIDDY TO STAND,
CLEMENTS TONIC
AND A PERMANENT RECOVERY.
The Case of
: l'.
V.
z
Mr. A. ST. CLAIR.
(specially reported.) " r ‘
Some interesting details concerning the
case of A. St. Clair, Esq., having been
furnished to us, we despatched our reporter
to Byron-street, Guildford, near Sydney,
where Mr. St. Clair resides and carries on
business as a photographer. Our reporter
explained his mission to Mr. St. Clair, and
that gentleman consented to afford the
desired interview.
“Then will you kindly furnish me with
particulars as to the initiation of your
malady ?”
“lam convinced,” replied Mr. St. Clair,
“ that it originated in there.” Our reporter
followed with his eyes the finger of the
photographer, which was directed to the
half-open door of a dark room. “Yes,”
continued Mr. St. Clair, “I worked a great
deal in there, and I believe my system was
first
Thrown Out of Gear
through my getting poisoned by some of the
chemicals which we in the trade are obliged
to make use of. I began to feel sick and
faint, and my head was
Racked with Fearful Pains.
I couldn’t sit down to my food, and all I
was able to eat was a mouthful or so between
meals, which did me no good, for I started
retching and vomiting immediately after-
wards. Pains seized me all over, principally
in the back, and as a blue line distinctly
appeared around my gums, the doctor whom
I consulted said this was a positive
Sign of Lead Poisoning.”
** Did you take nothing to allay the
violence of these symptoms?”
“Yes, many medicines ; but got not the
least benefit, and the more physic I took the
worse I got. I was now attacked with that
most terrible complaint—insomnia. Sleep-
less I passed night after night wearily toss-
ing to and fro, and I seemed as though I
should never have natural sleep again. I
was completely worn out, and my nerves
were shattered. I was not yet confined to
my bed, but I daren’t go out.
I was Frightened.
I can’t tell you of what. A vague terror of
something dreadful about to happen hung
over me. Strange feelings, as though I was
going to fall forward on my face, came over
me. When I woke in the morning I felt
choked and suffocated, and I should lump
out of bed only to find myself
Too Giddy to Stand.
Every doctor I went to said that my nervous
system was completely broken up. But all
the same their physic did me no good. I
was miserable, unhappy, melancholy, and
so nervous that I couldn’t stand the least
•annoyance. I heard that I could get some
Special Treatment in
Melbourne,
which would effect a cure, so I went there
with renewed hope. ”
“ Were your expectations realised ?”
“ Not much ; for I derived no benefit from
the treatment I obtained, nor were the
doctors
At the Hospital
there able to afford me any relief, and I was
forced to take the long painful journey
home again, and arrived just as ill as when
I started.
“ Did you try any special remedies?”
“Yes, I used many, but they were one
and all alike—they had absolutely no effect
upon me, and I got into a complete state of
nervous collapse. I had
Fallen Away Terribly
in weight, and was of a ghastly colour. I
was laid up in bed unable to move, and had
to be taken to the doctor’s in a cart. I
thought I was going to die. In fact, I made
sure I was gone. Then a friend mentioned
the virtues of Clements Tonic to me and
told me it was a
Perfect Nerve Remedy.
I answered that I didn’t think there existed
a medicine efficacious enough to drag me
out of the terrible state of nervous prostra-
tion and general debility I had reached. My
friend, however, persisted, and I at last
gave Clements Tonic a trial, and I shall be
thankful as long as I live, for that remedy
saved my life. Without Clements Tonic r
should now be
A Dead Man.
It commenced to act beneficially from the
moment that I started to take it. I felt
more restful, and didn’t tremble so much,
and the insomnia began to give way. The
gloom was removed from my spirits and at
intervals I now slept deeply. As I con-
tinued with Clement* Tonic, so did my
condition improve. Once more I could eat
without retching and vomiting up my food.
I obtained more and more sleep, and a better
appetite every day. My sudden improve-
ment was a
Revelation to My Friends.
When I was able to get about again I found
all that nervous, tremorous feeling had dis-
appeared, and I soon put on weight, and it
was not long before I was as well as ever.
Of course, I took Clements Tonic regularly
until I was assured of
A Permanent Recovery.’*
“ And you are entirely cured now, Mr-
St. Clair ?”
“ Yes ; I am as sound as a bell and ready
to travel anywhere that is necessary in
pursuit of business.”
“ And your recovery and present healthy
state you ascribe to Clements Tonic solely ?”
“I do most unreservedly. As I said
before, it saved me, pulled me back, as it
were, from the
Very Verge of the Grave
after all the other medicines, doctors, and
hospital had signally failed. I shall be for
ever grateful.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Albert Thomas St. Clair, of Byron-street, Guild-
ford, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting of eight folios
and consecutively numbered from one to eight, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish the same in
any way ; and I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of an Act made and passed m
the ninth year of the reign of her present Majesty,
intituled “An Act for the more effectual abolition of
Oaths and Affirmations taken and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New South Wales,
and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof, and for
the suppression of voluntary and extra-judicial Oaths
and Affidavits.”

Declared at Fairfield this 30th day of June, 1898,
before me. DAVID DALE, J.P.
