Blackberries.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was not iny fault that I did not marry Desir&e.
It was Jack’s fault, for he had married her be ore
I met her. So that even the most censorious cou c
not hold me to blame. Having married her, >e
took, her away to his run on the sea-coast a
lonely place, 50 miles from everywhere. There
were herds of cattle and flocks of sheep, but no
human beings ; so after two years of that awful
solitude she needed a change and came to town.
I called her Desiree because she was essentially
the Desirable One. She was perfection, from the
slender foot that felt to your clasp through its
silken slipper like some flexible and delicate gloved
hand, to the topmost curl that escaped from the
wreathed masses of her dark-brown hair. In those
long, empty afternoons of her married life she had
gazed out so many hours over the barren sea that
her blue eyes had caught the tone of its unfathom-
able depths, veiled now and then by shadows of
mysterious passing clouds. I was always fond of
sea pictures, so I pursued my studies of marine
art in Desiree’s eyes.
I, too, had come down for a change. I had
worked hard and felt the need of rest, yet here
was a new task for me to take up. Jack’s wife
had to be taken care of, and as he was not there
to do it, the duty evidently belonged to some
devoted person. It was true that I did not know
Jack—had never even seen him, but what of that?
Shall we never act except at the selfish biddings
of mere personal friendship ? I hope that we have
better moral guides than that, and I, who have
ever been a slave to conscience, here saw my path
of duty plain before me.
Desiree and I soon made friends and became
Absorbed (it is the only word) in the work of
studying and understanding each other. Know-
ing, however, that a heartless world, bound in the
trammels of a cold artificiality, looks with dis-
favor upon Uncertificated Absorption, I proposed
to Desir&e that we should escape for a little time
from the observant eyes of Society and fly to
Nature. “Nature,” as I pointed out to her,
“ keeps ever a fine soul of freshness and inno-
cence. Let us take our own fresh and innocent
souls to her, and feel the sympathy of the Great
Mother applauding and blessing us.” Desiree
approved, so we went together for a day in the
country.
Nature aid all for us that I had felt sure she
would ; in such a case one can make sure of
Nature. I cannot reveal the full account of that
day of days, for it is hazy with happiness, but it
is marked by two main reminiscences, of which one
is general and one particular. The general is that
it was a “Symphony in Azure,” for it was per-
vaded by the blue of the heavens above and the
blue of Desiree’s eyes beneath. The particular is
that I learned the way to eat blackberries.
Perhaps you, dear reader, think that you know
how to eat blackberries. I thought so too, and,
indeed, early in that day ate some myself—gross
berries, gathered with common hands, and put
into an earthly mouth. Listen, and I will tell you
how perfectly to eat them —even as I was taught.
Go to the edge of the birch forest where it looks
down upon the pleasant fields, when outside the
mid-day summer sun is warm, and there is no
sound but the drowsy hum of bees. There throw
yourself down on a cushion of the dry, < u «t‘ L ..
fallen leaves. Then let the Delightful One
wander away for a moment until she has
gathered a handful of the ripe berries and seated
herself near you. At your length reclining, yon
lay your cheek in the soft lap, warm from the
outer sunshine and from her own dear self. Then
will she approach the cup of her fair hand to your
lips, straightening the finger-tips that the berries
may lie flat along them. With a finger of the
other hand will she move each berry on, and, as it
comes to your desiring lips, you leave a kiss in
thanks, and await the next. When the last is
gone you reverently kiss once more the sweet-
stained fingers that have fed you with angels
food, and—the Divine Repast is over.
Folly? No, indeed. What better shall we ever
have offered us in a world of fools? Go to, good
Signor Greybeard, I am not taking any of your
kind of wisdom just now.
But Jack ! Jack !—what did I not endure that
day for duty’s sake and yours !
Maoriland.
EDWARD TREGEAR.
Sir Henry Campbell-Bannerman (he’s
Scoatch, ye ken), who has just succeeded to the
leadership of the “ Great Liberal ” party in the
Commons, is in his 63rd year. His father, oir
James Campbell, was a big draper in Glasgow,
and before he entered politics Henry worked in
the —er —emporium in Glasgow. He got the
hyphen and the Bannerman together with a fat
fortune from his maternal uncle, who was a
draper in Kent.
The other day, at Campbell Creek
Gold Dredging Co.’s works, near Castlemaine,
the man at the hydrant turned the jet on a boulder
which behaved as no other rock in his experience
had done—it changed into billows of lather. And
when the Old Inhabitant came he remembered
that a flood—“the fatal flood,” he called it—of
New Year’s Day, 1889, had swept through the
Winter’s Flat soap-works arid washed down a ton
or two of soap. This block, weighing nearly
scwt., had become embedded in debris, and so had
been preserved for a decade.
That celebrated litigant, W. R. .Merry,
lately became bankrupt at Melbourne. Liabilities
sworn at £2207 95., assets at ,£428,090 4s. 9d. But
the latter only exist on paper—like Treas. Reid’s
surpluses. Among other items occur these:
“ Balance on contract for construction of Geelong
and Ballarat railway, £264,590 4s. 9d.,” and
“due under decree of Privy Council in action,
Merry v. Queen, £140,000 also half of £25,000
advanced by Evans and Merry to a Melb. firm for
purposes of litigation against Queen and Board of
Land and Works.” Insolvent sets down his house-
hold expenses for the last two years at 10s. per
week, and his house-rent at ss. per month, winch,
for a man whose assets total nearly half-a-million,
is distinctly moderate. Poor Merry’s troubles
seem to have been mostly caused by going to law
early and often, and keeping at it steadily ; and,
as his first action in the Hereafter will probably
be to issue a caveat claiming a prior right and
title in that lot or messuage known as Seat 1, Row
A, it is to be hoped legal expenses “ up above
are on a lower scale than hereabouts, or this m-
solvent will be in danger of losing by foreclosure
his wings, harp, and halo.
Land of the Crocodile.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Probably no Australian Governor was ever
placed in a more embarrassed position than Lord
Lamington on a recent tour on the Burnett. At
one spot he was presented with an address by the
chairman of a Divisional Board. This magnate
was accompanied by a band of wild bushmen,
their trousers full of spear-grass and dead gum-
leaves in their beards. The address was read in
the open air. The chairman took off his hat,
placed it reverently on the ground behind him,
hauled his address from an inside coat-pocket,
and proceeded to read it in a voice that left the
listener in doubt if he was chiefly influenced by
reverence or fright. The Governor’s only com-
panion was a small, favorite dog known as “ Jor-
rocks.” This intelligent animal, being highly-
connected and accustomed to all the usages and
privileges of polite society, regarded the chair-
man’s act of placing the hat on the ground as an
act of courtesy to himself, and a pleasing proof of
the perfect sanitary arrangements of the local
Divisional Board. So Jorrocks bowed to the
chairman, and strolled over to the hat, where he
executed a graceful flank movement common in
dog society, and did it with a precision and com-
pleteness rarely attained by common dogs. Every
bushman present owned a dog of some species,
“ mongrel, puppy, whelp and hound, or curs of low
degree,” and this motley canine crew proceeded to
follow the example of Jorrocks, and started for
the chairman’s hat. The Governor was a witness
of this thrilling spectacular drama, and when the
27th dog had completed the hat act, His Ex. was
nearly too weak to stand erect without holding on
to something for support. When at last the
chairman took up his hat and placed it on his
head ! the Governor prayed fervently to be in a
lodge in some vast wilderness, some boundless
contiguity of shade, where his too oppressed soul
could be relieved by one demoniac yell.
* * * *
The death of the late Dr. Hancock recalls
a Queensland Club incident of the night Groom
was elected Speaker. After the election, old
Billy Miles walked over to the club and found the
doctor, with his left hand beneath his coat-tail
and his right pawing the air, fiercely disclaiming
against the Govt, for “ this scandalous business,
sir!” “Do you mean to make any reflection on
me or the Ministry ? ” said old Miles, with a war-
like, Caledonian glance in his eye. “Ido ! ” said
the doctor with great emphasis. “ Then come
outside,” said old Billy as he proceeded to remove
his coat. The attitude of both was entirely new
to scientific boxers. The doctor ducked his head
and shot out his right as if he were poking bread
into a hot oven, and Miles brought his left round
with a swipe as if he were brushing cockroaches
off a shelf. But this swipe caught the doctor on
the ear and laid him out. As he rose he glared at
his opponent and hissed melodramatically—“ By
heaven, sir, I have killed men for far less than
that! ” To which the ancient squatter sarcastically
observed, “I’ll be d—- if ever you’ll kill me,
for I’m not fool enough to take any of your
physio ! ” After which there was a fine icebergian
coolness between these warriors for many a day.
Peace to both the kindly-hearted old pioneers !

When “ Majah ” Lyons was recently in
the old country as immigration-lecturer,, he had
among his lantern-slides a reproduction of that
Bulletin picture of “people who goon the land,”
where the “ farmer ” and his wife think of starting
to feed a clutch of chickens for fear the hen has
not enough milk for them. He tells me that this
picture always sent an Irish audience into boister-
ous laughter. He tried it once on a Scotch crowd,
who gazed at it as seriously as if it had been
a picture of the Crucifixion. Next day a solemn
Caledonian advised him not to use that slide again,
as it looked like “ freevolous tretment o’ a serrious
subjec’ ” !
* * * *
An English firm has written to me asking
at what price large quantities of the seed-
cones of the bunya-trees could be shipped from
Brisbane. This beautiful tree (Araucaria
Bidivilli) is indigenous to the Bunya Ranges,
in S. Queensland. The tree attains a diameter of
seven or eight feet with a bark up to twelve
inches in thickness. The cones weigh up to 8
and 10 lbs., and one of them falling from a tall
tree would kill a bullock. The nuts in the cone
are highly nutritious, either raw or roasted. The
first cones exported were sent to London in 1849
and brought 10 guineas each in Covent Garden
market. The tree bears annually, but the full crop
is only triennial. In the old days, blacks came
from distances up to 300 miles for the “ Feast of
Bunyas,” and were the guests of the tribes owning
the range. Leichhardt, in 1845, mentions seeing
the bunya on the Isaacs River, but that was a
mistake. After the bunya season, the blacks
sometimes kept the cones for months by placing
them in water. The smell of these steeped cones
would have knocked out Limburger cheese in one
round.

Ex-Governor Bowen’s death recalls his
trip in the Pioneer to Cape York and Somerset in
1862 ; also his flowery description of the scenery.
“ The general aspect of the coast along which we
sailed for nearly 2000 miles resembled that of
southern Italy and Greece. The mountain ranges
of N.Q. have much of the picturesque outline and
rich coloring of the Apennines in Calabria, and of
the hills of Euboea and the Peloponnesus, while
the island groups recalled the isles of the JE gean
and lonian seas.” I have a copy of Bowen’s
“ Ithaca in 1850,” a small book of 70 pp., dedi-
cated to Gladstone, and containing a brief but
eloquent description of the noble defence of Mis-
solonghi in 1822 by a handful of Greeks under
Mavrocordatus, who defied a whole Turkish army
under Reshid Mehemet Pasha. There is nothing
more heroic in the annals of warfare.
* • * *
If Dr. William Macgregor has accepted the
Governorship of Lagos, we may be certain it was
because he was offered nothing better, or possibly
nothing else at all. And if this man, with his 23
years of Australasian service, and 11 years’ hard
work (with his life in his hands) among fever and
savages in Papua, exiled from wife and children,
is sent to a West African Gehenna like Lagos,
with still worse exile from his family, then the
fact displays on the part of the Imperial Govern-
ment a black ingratitude that must be an eternal
disgrace to all concerned in the responsibility.
MAROOGLINS.


Editor Sanders, of Australian Star, in
reply to Wise, Q.C., in the Hutchinson libel-case,
stated, believing he was compelled to do so, that
the Star circulation was 36,000 daily. But there
seems to be no compulsion on a newspaper to
state its circulation when defending a libel-action.
Parnell, in his action against London Times, ob-
tained a Judge’s order to force the Times’ hand in
this respect, but the order was quashed on appeal,
the decision of the court being that the ends of
justice would be met by the defending newspaper
admitting that its circulation was a large one.
This the Times did.

Excruciating
Neuralgia Cured.
Capt. Agnes H. Amos is the chief
of the Salvation Army in South-
port, a township 50 miles from Bris-
bane. Her good works are many,
and there is little doubt her over-
exertions in the cause she has at
heart induced general ill-health and
anaemia, which resulted in acute
neuralgia. She tried many reme-
dies without avail, including hot-
water cloths wrapped round her
face. Once—for eight long weeks—
-56 days—she was rendered almost
entirely sleepless by the acuteness
of the shooting, burning pains in her
face. A galvanic battery did not
cure her, but Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills did. Two boxes quite relieved
her—the relief commencing before
she had finished the first box. Ulti-
mately the pains entirely ceased,
she could sleep well, exhausted
nature was recuperated, her form
became plumper, her eyes brighter,
her cheeks rosy, and her whole
nature energetic and vivacious fust
as it had been before her illness.
No matter how acute, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills conquer nearly all cases
■of neuralgia, even when everything
■else has failed.
His Lights Are Out.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Have I hung my cap on the main truck, lass, or
stood on the royal yard,
That you clutch my arm at a deck-wide cliff with
a motherly damned regard ? . . .
It’s little they reck of the way it hurts when they
think of being kind,
But there isn’t a rock as sharp down there as the
knife that sent me blind.
Aye, a sotted fool and his dirty lance, and a twice
his thousand pound
Won’t pay for the ship that was half my soul— her
keel on the outward bound.
Blind ! Blind, oh, my God, as a crawling mole !
and never again to see, —
A star in the sky of the meanest thief, but a
starless Night for me. . . •
They’re breaking a bit on the reef to-day. It blew
half a gale last night,
And I laid and listened and fell asleep,—to dream
again of the light,
Till I heard the wind in her cordage chime. Oh,
she was my Queen of May,
And I dressed her up in her Sunday clothes, and
laughed when she waltzed away.
How I paced the poop like a desert chief and felt
the splash of the foam,
As she took the road like an Arab mare when I
turned her head for home !
I woke in the dark. And my face was wet —it s
hardly a sick man’s fault,
When he tastes the cup of his bitterness, if he
finds that tears are salt.
Great Lord of Hosts, how they ca’l down there !
Just hark how the young ones sing,
And tumble and roll away to the beach to play at
kiss-in-the-ring !
Kind Lord of Hosts ! how yon sun must shine, and
glitter the heaven through,
And dance and blaze in a shower of gold out there
on the white and blue.
They are cracking it on up-coast, I’ll bet. ’Tis a
rattling tops’! breeze ;
lean see the gloss of their wetted sides, a’wash
with the clinging seas ;
I can catch the gleam of the Baltic pine with the
caulking lines between ;
And I know the rig of the yards of Tyne from the
build of Aberdeen.
How the smell of it sets my heart to beat! There’s
never a garden rose
With a scent like that from the salt sou’-east,
when a ten-knot monsoon blows ;
Oh, it brings things back in the Dark to me—the
dreams of the crippled dog—
Till I hear the grunt of the rudder chains, the
running out of the log ;
Till I hear the churn of the bow-cut waves; the
kiss of her cuddling dip ;
’Till I’m live and whole and a king again on the
deck of my bonny ship.
Yes, I’m whole and live and I’m free to strive to
pleasure me as I can—•
So haul away on vour halliards there ! and follow
your chantey man!
I have laid her out for the brave west wind to
battle the Horn around ;
I have laid her up on the Channel course, and I’ll
home her safe and sound.
For its north of Scilly the soundings shoal—a
bottom of ooze and mud —
And the water’s deep on the coast of France, with
a sand as red as blood.
Aye, I know the lay of the Seven Stones; I’ll
steer by the lights in line;
Where the devil has set his traps for ships—the
devil must wait for mine.
We have lost the fog at the Farallones, and carried
her by the chart;
We have left the fog at the Farallones, but we’ll
find it at the Start.
’Tis book and beacon, and beacon and book, and
never forget the lead !
And beware Scar Rock with anor’-east wind when
the Whitby light turns red !
So a double reef in your topsails, lads ! There’s
hardly a ’prentice fool
But knows o’ the flood in Hasborough Gat, and
the lights at Hartlepool.
And I’ll steer my ship by the laws of God, as His
wind or tide allows ;
By the rules of the Merchant Shipping Act, and
the rules of Trinity House.
She will take her berth in the London Docks, and
add her load to the loads,
Though I stood at the wheel with Beelzebub,
from the Downs to Margate Roads.
If I moon out here on the cliffs and think, my
face to the open sea,
’Tis only because of the things I love that throng
in the Dark to me.
’Tis only because of a world I knew ; a world that
was wholly mine ;
It had three parts water and one part land, and
plenty of teak and pine.
It had three parts water and one part land, and a
Sun to light them o’er,
And the Sun and the Land and the Brave Deep
Seas —I shall see them nevermore ;
It had three parts water and one part land, and
plenty of hemp and oak ;
With Four Main Roads for the feet of Trade, and
a crossing at Cape St. Roque.
The water's blue in the Indian Seas and salt in
the Great Gulf Stream,
And the little trade clouds they are fair to see,
with the steady Trades abeam ;
And the moonheads shine in the tropic nights
when the sea-fire’s at the bow ;
And the Cross hangs high in the Southern arch —
but it doesn’t matter now.
I will never rest in a vault ashore, I couldn’t sleep
in a hole;
They must take me out in a clipper ship and leave
me under the roll;
They must lay me down at the Crossing-place, in
the mid Atlantic sea,
And I’ll say “Good-night” to the Voices then,
and the Night will sleep with me.
E. J. BEADY.
Melb. Leader cribs from Lippincott and
prints in.innocent faith a story entitled “ A Fight
with Tasmanian Devils,” which deserves the hot-
cross bun for frantic and fearsome perjury. The
devil, we are told, “is not larger than a fairly-
large wild pig.”
Drought.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dawn, and even now, in its birth, is the daylight
hard and sere as though already old. No lapping
water to usher in the early morn as it should be
ushered, no song of birds, not even the chirp of a
careless cricket. All is still, deadly still, without
sound or stir; only the light creeps slowly over the
dusty sky, hardening as it grows, till the soft,
timid stars fade away as though ashamed of their
delicate beauty.
As the light spreads and expands we see far and
near the skeleton frames of starving sheep moving
slowly through the dim shadows and desolation,
like weary ghosts of darkness marching before the
dawn, longing for the shelter of the night to hide
their misery. From far-off, somewhere in the
dark and gloomy scrub, comes the hopeless low of
a bullock. Men say that only man speaks ;
parsons assure us he is the only animal that prays.
Could they but hear the dull, hopeless low of the
cattle, with their heads uplifted to the brazen sky
as the dawn breaks over the dreariness of the
drought-stricken West, they would, perchance,
hear a greater and more touching appeal for
mercy than ever arose from church or cathedral.
And still no answer !
But the dawn is brief. Before we have time
to appreciate the full emotion of its misery, day is
upon us. The sun is up, burning with fierce eye
between the bare, straggling trees, and glaring
down hot and resistless over the scanty bushes
that seem to be suffering a perpetual struggle for
existence.
Slowly the sheep gather round a tank. At one
time they raced in, bucking and bleating. That
was when the drought was- young ; now they are
too weak and wasted to do more than is absolutely
necessary. Crawling down to the edge of the
water, they pass in around the carcases of their
dead comrades, and, with lowered heads and
bloodshot eyes, drink desperately and without
intermissiou till they can swallow no more. Turn-
ing wearily, they face the long expanse of dust
again, and wander away
in search of a stray
leaf here and there
fallen from the trees,
but some are too weak
even for this. Gorged
with water, they have
stuck in the edge of
the fast - drying tank.
They make one or two
weak efforts to free
themselves, then, if
Providence is not kind
enough to let them
smother themselves,
wait wearily for their
long torture and linger-
ing death.
Three or four kan-
garoos hop slowly out
of the scrub and ap-
proach the tank.
Avoiding the bogged
sheep, alive and dead,
they drop on to their
“hands” and crawl
down to the water. A
sheep plunges and
struggles to get out.
But the kangaroos are
no longer the quick,
springy athletes of bet-
ter times. With scarce
a look they begin to
drink. Suddenly comes
a sound of breaking
scrub. Jerking them-
selves upright, the kan-
garoos listen. Only
the cattle coming for
their morning drink.
The kangaroos finish
their drinking, and hop
away quietly into the
scrub at the back of
the tank. The cattle
drink, walk round slow-
ly and aimlessly, drink
again, and seem to be
wondering how they
will get through the
long, hot, dreary day
that is before them.
Another mob of sheep
comes in through a
cloud of dust. They
gather round and imi-
tate the performance of
the cattle. The sun
gets hotter. Gradually
the sheep and cattle
draw off to the shade
of a few miserable
trees and stand still,
wondering what to do
with themselves. A
few emus saunter lei-
surely across the plain
up to the tank, have
their drink, and join
the sheep and cattle.
They, too, seem to be
considering. Soon they
begin to amuse them-
selves by chasing the
sheep out of the shade.
Having cleared space
enough for their liking,
they settle down to
make themselves com-
fortable. Away in the
distance, two swagmen
are following the track
across the plain.
The crows begin to
gather. After a few
preliminary skirmishes,
one alights on the back
of a bogged sheep, and
makes quick, savage
pecks for its eyes, first
on one side, then on
the other. The sheep
throws its head about
wildly. At length it
begins to tire. The crow watches his opportu-
nity, and with a quick dab has one eye out. With
the slow, approving manner of a gourmet he swal-
lows it. Then he manoeuvres for the other eye.
A couple more join him. Between them they
soon get the remaining eye. Then they coolly set
to work to pick their way into the meat on the
back and ribs. The sheep struggles hopelessly,
throwing its eyeless head from side to side in vain
attempts to see its torturers. The crows keep
quietly on, pausing only now and then to settle a
dispute amongst themselves. The ants scent the
blood and arrive in swarms. Two long red and
black lines lead up and away from the suffering
sheep. Hungry they come, satiate they go.
Gradually the holes the crows are picking widen
and deepen till the vitals are reached. The
swarm of ants increases. The rest is indescribable.
Before midday a boundary-rider appears. The
emus sight him in the distance, get up, and with
many stoppages for inspection, stalk sedately
into the scrub. As the boundary-rider approaches
at the eternal jog the crows fly up into the
neighboring trees. Dropping the reins of his
horse, he walks down to the water to pull out the
bogged sheep. The crows croak harshly above
him. Cursing them, he goes on with his work. His
horse creeps down to the water’s edge, the bridle
reins dragging on the ground. The man is too busy
to notice him. After a few vain attempts to put a
sulky sheep on its legs he leaves it and goes on
with the rest. Here and there one struggles on to
its feet and staggers away ; now and then he cuts
another’s throat with his pocket-knife to put it
out of misery. When he is finished, his horse is
standing on the top of the tank. Without
noticing he goes up and takes hold of the reins.
An angry exclamation, and he drives his boot into
the horse’s ribs. The poor old horse hardly
moves. With a couple more kicks the boundary-
rider remounts, and at the everlasting jog dis-
appears in the heat and haze shimmering over the
plain.
The sun crosses the meridian. Nothing stirs.
Looking out across the plain, the whole earth
appears to be smoking and trembling with heat.
False lakes, mirroring imaginary trees and bushes,
appear and disappear. The scrub hangs dark,
dusty and motionless.
Gradually the wind arises from the west, swel-
tering along slowly and lazily, scorching the face,
and especially the eyelids, till they ache with the
heat. Little by little it grows stronger. The
dust begins to rise.
The sun droops towards the horizon, but as yet
there is no perceptible difference in the tempera-
ture. The stock begin to come in to water again.
Down in the west a storm is gathering. The wind
increases, the dust rises thicker and more blind-
ing. At the tank the scenes of the morning are
repeated, only now the iguanas, most cruel of all,
have come to help the crows and ants.
Slowly the sky becomes overcast, and the sun
seems to sink behind a bank of cloud. A distant
peal of thunder is heard. The new-chum, who is
out on the run, heads for home; the bushman
rides on and takes no notice. The storm comes
closer. With startling suddenness a couple of
claps of thunder rattle amongst the clouds
overhead. The new-chum rides faster; the
bushman’s lip curls itself with the natural
bush bitterness as he turns his back to the wind.
In half-a-minute the storm is upon him, and it is
—dust! . .
The sun sinks wan and weary-looking behind
the clouds of dust, as though he, too, were weak
and wasted with the long, weary struggle against
the drought. Slowly he sinks till, with a sudden
fall, he appears to lose his hold and drop right out
of the sky.
In the east the moon rises slowly, red and
hard and grim. The ’possums begin to ap-
pear on the hollow trees, the mopokes break out
into a mournful hooting along the dry river-bed,
the trees grow dark and ghostly, casting strange,
uncertain shadows, and another day is gone. To-
morrow, we know, will be just such another.
N.S.W.
WILL. M. GLEMING.
